The Great Difference between Proving God by Signs and by Syllogistic Proof
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes proving God through signs from syllogistic proof, emphasizing direct entailment and the mental nature of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers sign-arguments more fundamental than syllogistic proofs for establishing God's existence, and why absolute universals cannot serve as premises for certain knowledge of God.

Core Idea
Every contingent thing directly entails God; universals are only mental.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya contends that the Islamic method of proving God through signs (ayat) from creation is more immediate and certain than the syllogistic proofs championed by the logicians. He argues that each particular contingent being entails the existence of its Creator necessarily, without needing universal premises. Furthermore, he emphasizes that absolute universals, such as 'existence' or 'agent', exist only in the mind and cannot be the foundation for proving the Creator, who is uniquely real in the external world. This critique strikes at the heart of the Aristotelian theory of demonstration and reaffirms the Qur'anic approach of reflecting on signs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Ibn Taymiyya frames the contrast between 'proving by signs' (إثبات بالآيات) and 'proving by syllogistic proof' (إثبات بالقياس البرهاني). Note how he positions signs as direct and immediate, while syllogistic proof relies on universal premises.
Signs include both external objects and mental conceptions.
2
Understand the entailment (istilzam)
He asserts that every contingent thing (كل ما سوى الله من الممكنات) necessarily entails the essence of God (مستلزم لذات الرب). This means the existence of anything other than God is impossible without God's existence—a direct connection, not mediated by a syllogism.
He uses the term 'mustalzim' to indicate a necessary connection.
3
The mental status of absolute universals
Ibn Taymiyya argues that absolute universals (e.g., absolute existence) have no reality in external objects (لا تحقق له في الأعيان). They exist only in the mind. Therefore, any proof built on universal concepts is not grounded in external reality, undermining the logicians' claim that demonstration (burhan) rests on universal premises.
This is a nominalist position, contra the realist logicians.
4
Qur'anic confirmation
He cites Surah al-Alaq to show that God is both Creator of external existents and Teacher of sciences. This demonstrates that both the physical world and our mental concepts are signs (ayat) that point to God and are taught by Him. Thus, the sign-argument is the original and comprehensive method.
He contrasts this with the logicians' reliance on man-made syllogistic forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عِظَم الفرق بين إثبات الرب بالآيات وبين إثباته بالقياس البرهاني

ومعلوم أن كل ما سوى الله من الممكنات فإنه مستلزم لذات الرب تعالى - يمتنع وجوده بدون وجود ذات الرب تعالى وتقدَّس. وإن كان مستلزماً أيضاً لأمور كلية مشتركة بينه وبين غيره فلأنه يلزم من وجوده يلزم من وجود المعين وجود لوازمه، وتلك الكليات المشتركة من لوازم المعين - أعنى يلزمه ما يخصه الوجود المطلق من ذلك الكلي العام والكلي المشترك - يلزمه بشرط وجوده، ووجود العالم الذي يتصور القدر المشترك. وهو سبحانه يعلم الأمور على ما هي عليه، فيعلم نفسه المقدسة بما يخصها، ويعلم الكليات أنها كليات فيلزم من وجود الخاص وجود العام المطلق، أي حصة المعين من ذلك العام كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان وجود الإنسان، ومن وجود هذا الإنسان وجود الإنسانية والحيوانية القائمة به.

فكل ما سوى الرب مستلزم لنفسه المقدسة بعينها، يمتنع وجود شيء سواه بدون وجود نفسه المقدسة. فإن الوجود المطلق الكلي لا تحقق له في الأعيان، فضلاً عن أن يكون خالقاً لها مبدعاً.

ثم يلزم من وجوده المعين الوجود المطلق المطابق للمعين. فإذا تحقق الوجود الواجب تحقق الوجود المطلق المطابق للمعين. وإذا تحقق الفاعل لكل شيء تحقق الفاعل المطلق المطابق. وإذا تحقق القديم الأزلي تحقق القديم المطلق المطابق. وإذا تحقق الغني عن كل شيء تحقق الغني المطابق. وإذا تحقق رب كل شيء تحقق الرب المطابق. كما ذكرنا أنه إذا تحقق هذا الإنسان وهذا الحيوان تحقق الإنسان المطلق المطابق والحيوان المطلق المطابق.

لكن المطلق لا يكون مطلقاً إلا في الأذهان لا في الأعيان. والله تعالى هو الخالق للأمور الموجودة في الأعيان، والمعلم للصور الذهنية المطابقة لما في الأعيان.

ولهذا كان أول ما أنزل على رسوله: {اقرأ باسم ربك الذي خلق، خلق الإنسان من علق، اقرأ وربك الأكرم} إلى قوله {علم الإنسان ما لم يعلم} [سورة العلق: 1-5]. بين في أول ما أنزل أنه خالق الأعيان عموماً وخصوصاً ومعلم العلوم. فكما أنه خالق الموجودات العينية فهو المعلم للماهيات الذهنية. فالموجودات الخارجية آيات له مستلزمة لوجود عينه، وإذا تصورتها الأذهان معينة أو مطلقة فهو المعلم لهذا المتصور. فالصور الذهنية أيضاً من آياته المستلزمة لوجود عينه. لكنها تدل على هدايته وتعليمه، كما قال: {سبح اسم ربك الأعلى، الذي خلق فسوى، والذي قدر فهدى} [سورة الأعلى: 1-3]، وقال موسى: {ربنا الذي أعطى كل شيء خلقه ثم هدى} [سورة طه: 50].
The great difference between proving the Lord by signs and proving Him by syllogistic proof. And it is known that everything other than God among contingent things entails the essence of the Lord Almighty—its existence is impossible without the existence of the essence of the Lord Almighty and Exalted. And if it also entails universal matters common to it and other things, it is because from its existence follows the existence of its concomitants, and those common universals are among the concomitants of the particular—I mean, it is entailed by what distinguishes it: absolute existence from that universal common and the common universal—it is entailed conditionally upon its existence, and the existence of the world that conceives the common aspect. And He, exalted, knows things as they are, so He knows His holy essence by what distinguishes it, and He knows universals as universals. So from the existence of the particular follows the existence of the absolute universal, i.e., the share of the particular from that universal, just as from the existence of this human follows the existence of 'human', and from the existence of this human follows the existence of the 'humanity' and 'animality' subsisting in him. So everything other than the Lord entails His holy essence itself; the existence of anything other than Him is impossible without the existence of His holy essence. For absolute universal existence has no reality in external objects, let alone being their creator and originator. Then from the existence of the particular follows the absolute existence corresponding to the particular. So if the necessary existence is realized, the absolute existence corresponding to the particular is realized. And if the agent of everything is realized, the absolute agent corresponding is realized. And if the eternal ancient is realized, the absolute ancient corresponding is realized. And if the independent of everything is realized, the absolute independent corresponding is realized. And if the Lord of everything is realized, the absolute Lord corresponding is realized. Just as we mentioned that if this human and this animal are realized, the absolute human corresponding and the absolute animal corresponding are realized. But the absolute is only absolute in minds, not in external objects. And God Almighty is the creator of things existing in external objects, and the teacher of mental forms corresponding to what is in external objects. And for this reason, the first thing revealed to His Messenger was: {Read in the name of your Lord who created, created man from a clot, read and your Lord is the most generous} up to His saying {He taught man what he did not know} [Surah al-Alaq: 1-5]. He made clear in the first revelation that He is the creator of external objects generally and specifically and the teacher of sciences. Just as He is the creator of external existents, so He is the teacher of mental essences. So external existents are signs for Him, entailing the existence of His essence, and when minds conceive them as particular or absolute, He is the teacher of that conception. So mental forms are also among His signs entailing the existence of His essence. But they indicate His guidance and teaching, as He said: {Exalt the name of your Lord, the Most High, who created and proportioned, and who determined and guided} [Surah al-A'la: 1-3], and Moses said: {Our Lord is He who gave each thing its form and then guided} [Surah Ta-Ha: 50].
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya contrasting two methods of proving God: through signs (ayat) and through syllogistic proof (qiyas burhani). He argues that every contingent thing (ma siwa Allah) necessarily entails the essence of God—its existence is impossible without God. This entailment is direct and not mediated by universal terms; the particular thing itself entails its Creator. He then discusses the relationship between particular and universal existence: the existence of a particular entails the existence of the absolute corresponding to it (e.g., this human entails 'human'), but the absolute universal has no extramental reality—it exists only in the mind. He applies this to divine attributes: if the Necessary Existent is realized, then absolute existence corresponding to it is realized in the mind, but this absolute existence is not an external reality. He then cites Quranic revelation (Surah al-Alaq) to show that God is both Creator of external objects and Teacher of sciences, confirming that both external and mental realities are signs (ayat) that entail His existence. Thus, the 'sign-argument' (istidlal bi-l-ayat) is more immediate and certain than syllogistic reasoning that relies on universal premises.

Key Terms
الآيات
الوجود المطلق
المستلزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the relationship between particular existents and the existence of God?
A) Particular existents are independent of God.
B) Particular existents necessarily entail God's existence.
C) God is proven only through universal syllogisms.
D) Particular existents are mere illusions.

Key Takeaway
Proving God through signs is direct and certain; syllogistic proofs relying on universals are secondary and less foundational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a sign (aya) in creation (e.g., the alternation of night and day) and explain how it directly entails God's existence, following Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning.
?
Construct a syllogistic proof of God's existence using a universal premise (e.g., 'Every contingent needs a cause') and then show how Ibn Taymiyya would critique it on the grounds that the universal premise is merely mental.

intermediate
12 min
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' concept of absolute existence
Critique of the logicians' claim that absolute existence follows from specific existence and is real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why Ibn Taymiyya argues that the absolute existence deduced by logicians is only a mental construct and not a real external entity, and explain how this undermines their proof for the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Absolute existence is only in the mind, not in reality; the philosophers mistakenly reify a mental universal.

The Peripatetic logicians argued that from the existence of specific entities, an absolute existence corresponding to them necessarily follows, and they took this absolute existence to be a real attribute of the Necessary Being. In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya deconstructs this argument, showing that the absolute concept is merely a mental abstraction with no external reality. By grounding his critique in the nature of universals and Quranic revelation, he demonstrates that the philosophers' proof fails to establish the specific Lord of Islam, yielding only a generic universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
What exactly are the logicians asserting? They claim that from the specific existence of a thing (e.g., a human), an absolute existence (e.g., absolute humanity) follows and is real. Apply this to God: they think absolute necessary existence is a real external attribute.
2
Trace Ibn Taymiyya's deconstruction
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between specific existence (al-wujud al-mu'ayyan) and absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq). He argues that absolute concepts are only in the mind, not in external reality. The philosophers' inference from specific to absolute existence does not give an external real entity.
He uses the example of 'this human' vs 'absolute human' to illustrate.
3
Examine his Quranic alternative
Ibn Taymiyya invokes Quranic verses to show that Allah is the creator of external entities and the teacher of mental concepts. External existents and mental forms are both signs (ayat) that necessitate His specific existence. This framing replaces the philosophers' abstract universal with a concrete, specific Lord.
Surah al-Alaq, al-A'la, and Taha are cited.
4
Understand the conclusion
The philosophers' demonstration yields only a universal concept (e.g., absolute necessary), not the specific Lord. Therefore, their proof does not establish the unique Creator of Islam. This is a fundamental critique of the logical proof for God's existence as formulated by the Peripatetics.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that the universal is not specific to the Lord.

Source Text

ومن وجود هذا الإنسان» وجود «الإنسانية» و «الحيوانية» القائمة به . فكل ما سوى الرب مستلزم لنفسه المقدسة بعينها، يمتنع وجود شيء سواه بدون وجود نفسه المقدّسة. فإن «الوجود المطلق الكلي لا تحقق له في الأعيان، فضلاً عن أن يكون خالقاً لها مبدعاً . ثم يلزم من وجوده المعين الوجود المطلق المطابق للمعين. فإذا تحقق الوجود الواجب تحقق الوجود المطلق المطابق للمعين. وإذا تحقق الفاعل لكل شيء تحقق الفاعل المطلق المطابق. وإذا تحقق القديم الأزلي تحقق القديم المطلق المطابق . وإذا تحقق الغني عن كل شيء تحقق الغني المطابق. وإذا تحقق ربُّ كل شيء تحقق الرب المطابق. كما ذكرنا أنه إذا تحقق هذا الإنسان» و «هذا الحيوان» تحقق «الإنسان المطلق المطابق و الحيوان المطلق المطابق». لكن «المطلق» لا يكون مطلقاً إلا في الأذهان لا في الأعيان. والله تعالى هو المطلق إلا في الأذهان الخالق للأمور الموجودة في الأعيان، والمعلم للصور الذهنية المطابقة لما في الأعيان . خالق الموجودات - الله سبحانه ولهذا كان أول ما أنزل على رسوله : أَقْرَأْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ الَّذِى خَلَقَ خَلَقَ الْإِنسَنَ مِنْ عَلَقٍ اقْرَأْ وَرَبُّكَ الْأَكْرَمُ ) - إلى قوله - ما لريعلَم [سورة العلق : ١ - ٥] . بين في أول ما أنزل أنه ومعلم العلوم خالقُ الأعيان عموماً وخصوصاً. فكما أنه خالق الموجودات العينية فهو المعلم للماهيات الذهنية. فالموجودات الخارجية آيات له مستلزمة لوجود عينه، وإذا تصورتها الأذهانُ معينة أو مطلقة فهو المعلم لهذا المتصوّر. فالصُّوَر الذهنية أيضاً من آياته المستلزمة لوجود عينه. لكنها تدل ذلك على هدايته وتعليمه، كما قال: ﴿سَبِّح اسْمَ رَبِّكَ الْأَعْلَى الَّذِي خَلَقَ فَسَوَّى وَالَّذِى قَدَّرَ فَهَدَى [سورة الأعلى : ١-٣]، وقال موسى : رَبُّنَا الَّذِي أَعْطَى كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقَهُ ثُمَّ هَدَى ) [سورة طه : ٥٠]. المقام الثالث - الوجه السادس : الله سبحانه خالق الموجودات، ومعلم العلوم ۱۹۷ كما أن الموجودات العينية من آيات ،وجوده والصورُ الذهنية من حيث إنها موجودات عينية هي من هذا الباب، كما أنها من جهة مطابقتها للموجودات الخارجية من الباب الأول. لكن إذا علم إنسان وجودَ إنسان مطلق و حيوان «مطلق لم يكن عالماً بنفس لا يعلم الرب المعيَّن. كذلك من علم واجباً مطلقاً» و «فاعلاً مطلقاً» و «غنياً مطلقاً» لم يكن من الوجود عالماً بنفس ربّ العالمين، وما يختص به عن غيره . المطلق وذلك هو مدلول «آياته تعالى فآياته تستلزم عينه التي يمنع تصورها من وقوع إنما الشركة فيها. وكل ما سواه دليل على عينه، وآية له فإنه ملزوم لعينه، وكل ملزوم فإنه يعلم الرب دليل على لازمه ويمتنع تحقق شيء من الممكنات إلا مع تحقق عينه. فكلها ملزوم لنفس الرب، دليل عليه آية له . ودلالتها بطريق قياسهم على الأمر المطلق الكلي الذي لا يتحقق إلا في الذهن. فلم يعلموا بـ «برهانهم ما يختص بالرب تعالى ولهذا ما يُثبتونه من واجب الوجود عند التحقيق إنما هو أمر كلي لا يختص بالربّ تعالى.
And from the existence of this human being, the existence of 'humanity' and 'animality' subsisting in it. So everything other than the Lord is necessarily entailing His holy self per se; it is impossible for anything else to exist without the existence of His holy self. For 'the absolute universal existence' has no realization in external entities, let alone being their creator and originator. Then, from His specific existence, the absolute existence corresponding to the specific necessarily follows. So if the necessary existence is realized, the absolute existence corresponding to the specific is realized. If the agent of everything is realized, the absolute agent corresponding is realized. If the eternal pre-eternal is realized, the absolute eternal corresponding is realized. If the self-sufficient from everything is realized, the absolute self-sufficient corresponding is realized. If the Lord of everything is realized, the absolute Lord corresponding is realized. As we mentioned that if 'this human' and 'this animal' are realized, 'the absolute human corresponding' and 'the absolute animal corresponding' are realized. But 'the absolute' is only absolute in minds, not in external entities. And Allah is the absolute only in minds, the creator of things existing in external entities, and the teacher of mental forms corresponding to what is in external entities. The creator of existents - Allah, and for this reason the first thing revealed to His messenger was: 'Read in the name of your Lord who created, created man from a clot. Read, and your Lord is the Most Generous' - up to His saying - 'taught man what he did not know' [Surah al-Alaq: 1-5]. He made clear in the first revelation that He is the creator of external entities generally and specifically and the teacher of sciences. Just as He is the creator of external existents, He is the teacher of mental essences. So external existents are signs of Him, necessitating the existence of His self; and when minds conceive them as specific or absolute, He is the teacher of what is conceived. So mental forms are also among His signs necessitating the existence of His self, but they indicate His guidance and teaching, as He said: 'Exalt the name of your Lord, the Most High, who created and proportioned, and who determined and guided' [Surah al-A'la: 1-3], and Moses said: 'Our Lord is He who gave each thing its form and then guided [it]' [Surah Taha: 50]. The third station - the sixth aspect: Allah is the creator of existents and the teacher of sciences. Just as external existents are signs of His existence, and mental forms insofar as they are external existents are of this category, and insofar as they correspond to external existents are of the first category. But if a person knows the existence of 'absolute human' and 'absolute animal', he does not know the specific Lord. Similarly, whoever knows 'absolute necessary', 'absolute agent', and 'absolute self-sufficient' does not thereby know the self of the Lord of the worlds and what distinguishes Him from others. That is the meaning of His signs: His signs necessitate His self, the conception of which prevents any sharing in it. And everything other than Him is a proof of His self and a sign for Him, for it is entailing of His self, and every entailing thing is a proof of its consequent; and it is impossible for any possible thing to be realized except with the realization of His self. So all of them are entailing of the self of the Lord, a proof of Him, a sign for Him. And their indication is by way of their syllogism on the absolute universal matter that is only realized in the mind. So they did not know by their 'demonstration' what is specific to the Lord; and therefore what they affirm of the Necessary Existence, upon investigation, is only a universal matter not specific to the Lord.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a key logical argument used by the Peripatetic philosophers. Ibn Taymiyya begins by establishing a principle: the existence of any specific entity (like a particular human) entails the existence of its corresponding universal concept (humanity) in the mind. He then applies this to the Necessary Being: the existence of the specific Necessary Being (Allah) would logically entail the 'absolute existence' corresponding to it, just as a specific human entails absolute human. However, he immediately clarifies that such 'absolute' concepts have no reality outside the mind; they are mental constructs. Thus, the philosophers wrongly assume that the 'absolute Necessary Existence' is an external reality, when in fact it is only a mental universal. Ibn Taymiyya then supports his view with Quranic verses that highlight God as the creator of external entities and the teacher of mental concepts. He explains that external existents are signs of His specific self, and mental forms are also signs, but they indicate His teaching. The key critique is that the philosophers' syllogism only yields a universal concept (like 'necessary existence') which is not specific to the Lord. Their 'demonstration' thus fails to establish the unique, specific Lord; it only affirms a generic concept that does not correspond to the actual reality of Allah. This refutation undermines the entire logical proof for the Necessary Being as formulated by the philosophers.

الوجود المعين
المطلق المطابق

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why does the logicians' proof fail to establish the specific Lord?
A) Because the proof is invalid in form.
B) Because the 'absolute existence' they conclude is only a mental universal, not a real entity.
C) Because they do not use Quranic evidence.
D) Because they ignore the distinction between necessary and contingent.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians by showing that their concept of absolute existence is merely a mental universal, not an external reality, so their proof does not establish the specific Lord.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the specific claim of the logicians that Ibn Taymiyya attacks, and then restate Ibn Taymiyya's counter-argument in your own words. Ensure you explain why the absolute is only mental and how the Quranic framework provides an alternative.
?
Examine a contemporary argument for God's existence that uses terms like 'necessary being' (e.g., the modal cosmological argument). Does it commit the same error of treating a mental universal as an external entity? Write a brief assessment (200-300 words) referencing Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Logical Convolution
Author attacks logicians' method as false or unnecessarily lengthy, contrasting with the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' method as either false or unnecessarily convoluted, and contrast it with the Quranic method.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya attacks logicians for making a simple path convoluted.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya launches a trenchant critique of the logicians' methodology. He argues that their intricate proofs are either outright false or excessively long-winded, when a direct approach would suffice. He uses vivid analogies and contrasts this with the Quran, which he calls the straightest path. This lesson dissects his critique and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities
Ibn Taymiyya states that the logicians' method is either false (bāṭil) or a prolongation (taṭwīl) that distances the seeker from truth. Find this in the Arabic: 'إما أن يكون باطلاً، وإما أن يكون تطويلاً'. Understand that he gives no middle ground.
This sets up a dichotomy: either false or unnecessarily lengthy.
2
Analyze the ear analogy
He gives the example of someone asked where his ear is, who instead of pointing directly raises his hand high and circles it to his ear. This illustrates unnecessary complexity. Reflect on how this mimics logicians' circuitous reasoning.
The analogy shows that a simple task is made difficult.
3
Interpret the poetic quotes
Two lines of poetry are quoted: the first describes someone working days for water that ends up like water; the second about making a strap for a broken sandal. Both depict wasted effort. Consider how they reinforce the critique of futility.
Poetry underscores the absurdity of excessive labor for minimal gain.
4
Contrast with the Quran
Ibn Taymiyya quotes Quran 17:9: 'Indeed this Quran guides to that which is most straight.' He contrasts the Quran's direct guidance with the logicians' crooked and long path. Note that he elevates prophetic guidance over human artifice.
The Quran is presented as the standard of straightness.
5
Assess the conclusion
He concludes that the logicians' path, with its errors and length, does not save from punishment or lead to true happiness or perfection. Reflect on the theological stakes: the method fails both epistemologically and soteriologically.
The ultimate failure is spiritual, not just logical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: هذا الذي قالوه إما أن يكون باطلاً، وإما أن يكون تطويلاً يبعد الطريق على الطالب المستدلّ، فلا يخلو عن خطأ يصدُّ عن الحق، أو طريق طويل يتعب صاحبَه حتى يصل إلى الحق، مع إمكان وصوله بطريق قريب. كما كان يمثله طويل متعب بعض سلفنا بمنزلة من قيل له : أين أذنك؟، فرفع يده فوق رأسه رفعاً شديداً ثم أدارها إلى أذنه اليسرى، وقد كان يمكنه الإشارة إلى اليمني أو اليسرى من طريق مستقيم. وما أشبه هؤلاء بقول القائل : أقام يعمل أياماً رَوِيَّتَهُ وشبه الماء بعد الجُهد بالماء. وقول الآخر : وإني، وإنّي، ثم إنّي، وإنني إذا انقطعت نعلي جعلتُ لها شِسْعاً. ما أحسن ما وصف الله به كتابه بقوله: ﴿ إِنَّ هَذَا الْقُرْآنَ يَهْدِي لِلَّتِي هِيَ أَقْوَمُ ﴾ [سورة الإسراء : ۹] . فأقومُ الطرق إلى أشرف المطالب ما بعث الله به رسوله. وأما طريق هؤلاء فهي مع ضلالهم في البعض، واعوجاج طريقهم وطولها في البعض الأخرى، إنما يوصلهم إلى أمر لا يُنجي من عذاب الله، فضلاً عن أن يوجب لهم السعادة، فضلاً عن حصول الكمال للأنفس البشرية بطريقهم.
So we say: This which they said is either false, or it is a prolongation that distances the path from the seeking student. So it is not free from an error that turns away from the truth, or a long path that tires its owner until he reaches the truth, despite the possibility of reaching it by a near path. Just as some of our predecessors used to liken it to the one who is asked: 'Where is your ear?' So he raised his hand above his head with great raising then circled it to his left ear, while he could have pointed to the right or left directly. And these are like the poet's saying: 'He stayed working days on his deliberation, and after exertion, water is like water.' And the other's saying: 'And I, and I, and then I, and indeed I, when my sandal broke, I made a strap for it.' How excellent is what Allah described His Book with: 'Indeed this Quran guides to that which is most straight.' So the straightest paths to the noblest demands are what Allah sent His Messenger with. As for the path of these (logicians), along with their error in some (parts) and the crookedness and length of their path in other (parts), it only leads them to something that does not save from Allah's punishment, let alone necessitate happiness for them, let alone the attainment of perfection for human souls through their path.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a fierce critique of the logicians' methodology. Ibn Taymiyya argues that their claimed proofs are either outright false or excessively lengthy and convoluted. He gives an analogy: someone asked where his ear is, instead of pointing directly, he raises his hand high and circles it to his ear. This illustrates unnecessary complexity. He quotes two lines of poetry illustrating wasted effort. Then he contrasts this with the Quran, which is the straightest path. He concludes that the logicians' path, with its errors and length, does not lead to salvation or true perfection. This is a rhetorical and theological refutation, not a logical one.

Key Terms
تطويل (taṭwīl): Unnecessary lengthening or prolixity in argumentation. Not a technical term in logic; used here in a rhetorical sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point about the logicians' method in this passage?
A) It is always false.
B) It is either false or unnecessarily long, and the Quran provides the straightest path.
C) It is difficult but necessary for salvation.
D) It is equivalent to the prophetic method.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique warns against mistaking complexity for profundity; the Quran offers a more direct path to truth.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Heretical Sufis Who Elevate Philosophers Over Prophets
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the extreme views of heretical Sufis who claim philosophers like Aristotle were greater than prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key claims of heretical Sufis regarding philosophers and prophets and explain Ibn Taymiyya's refutation based on Islamic revelation.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the extreme deviation of those who place philosophers above prophets, reducing miracles to sorcery.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya confronts a severe deviation within some Sufi circles that elevates philosophers like Aristotle above the prophets. He lists several specific blasphemous claims and traces them to known heretical figures. Understanding this critique helps you recognize the boundaries of orthodox Islamic belief regarding prophethood and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups being criticized
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya targets 'malāḥidat al-mutaṣawwifa' (heretical Sufis) and also mentions Shi'a elements. He names Ibn Sab'in and Ibn Arabi as examples.
2
List the key claims of these heretics
The passage enumerates several claims: (1) Aristotle was al-Khidr; (2) philosophers are superior to prophets in knowledge; (3) Harun was more knowledgeable than Musa; (4) Ali was more knowledgeable than Muhammad; (5) al-Khidr, Ali, and Harun knew intellectual realities better than Musa, Isa, and Muhammad; (6) prophets are superior only in practical power; (7) Pharaoh is the 'Coptic Plato'; (8) Musa's father-in-law is the Greek Plato; (9) Musa was more knowledgeable in sorcery; (10) miracles are just psychic/natural/celestial powers akin to sorcery.
Focus on the core: philosophers over prophets, miracles as sorcery.
3
Identify the basis of Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
Ibn Taymiyya does not provide a detailed argument here but implicitly refutes by labeling these as sayings of malāḥida (heretics) and by associating them with known deviants. The refutation is grounded in Islamic revelation: these claims contradict the clear superiority of prophets in the Qur'an and Sunnah.
The refutation is by showing these views are from those who outwardly profess Islam but are actually heretics.
4
Analyze the implications for 'aqida
The passage underscores that elevating philosophers over prophets undermines the fundamental Islamic belief in the finality and superiority of prophethood. Reducing miracles to sorcery attacks the very basis of prophetic proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ملاحدة المتصوفة من يزعم أن أرسطو كان هو الخضر ـ خضر موسى . وهؤلاء منهم من يفضل الفلاسفة على الأنبياء في العلم، ويقول إن هارون كان أعلم من موسى، وإن علياً كان أعلم من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، كما يزعمون أن الخضر كان أعلم من موسى؛ وأن علياً وهارون ،والخضر، كانوا فلاسفة يَعْلَمون الحقائق العقلية العلمية أكثر من موسى وعيسى، ومحمد، لكن هؤلاء كانوا في القوة العملية أكمل ، ولهذا وضعوا الشرائع العملية . وهؤلاء يفضّلون فرعون على موسى، ويسمونه أفلاطن القبطي»، وقد يقولون : إن صاحب مدين الذي تزوّج موسى بنته هو أفلاطن اليوناني، أستاذ أرسطو . ويقولون إن موسى كان أعلم من غيره بالسِّحر، وإنه استفاد ذلك من حَمْوه، إذ كان عندهم ليست المعجزات إلا قوى نفسانية، أو طبيعية، أو فلكية، من جنس السحر . موسى كان مبرزاً على غيره في ذلك . ولكن موسى إلى أمثال ذلك من المقالات التي تقولها الملاحدة المتفلسفة المنتمون إلى الإسلام في الظاهر من متشيّع ومتصوف كابن سبعين وابن عربي
And among the heretical Sufis are those who claim that Aristotle was al-Khidr - the Khidr of Moses. And among these are those who prefer the philosophers over the prophets in knowledge, and say that Harun was more knowledgeable than Musa, and that Ali was more knowledgeable than the Prophet (peace be upon him), just as they claim that al-Khidr was more knowledgeable than Musa; and that Ali, Harun, and al-Khidr were philosophers who knew the intellectual rational realities more than Musa, Isa, and Muhammad, but these (prophets) were more complete in practical power, and for this they placed practical laws. And these prefer Pharaoh over Musa, and call him 'the Coptic Plato', and they say that the owner of Madyan who married his daughter to Musa is the Greek Plato, the teacher of Aristotle. And they say that Musa was more knowledgeable than others in sorcery, and that he benefited that from his father-in-law, since according to them miracles are nothing but psychic, natural, or celestial powers, of the same genus as sorcery. Musa was prominent over others in that. But Musa... and the like of these sayings which are said by the heretical philosophizing ones who outwardly belong to Islam from among the Shi'a and Sufis like Ibn Sab'in and Ibn Arabi.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a sharp critique by Ibn Taymiyya of extreme Sufi and Shi'a groups who hold philosophers in higher regard than prophets. He lists several blasphemous claims: that Aristotle was al-Khidr, that Harun was more knowledgeable than Musa, that Ali was more knowledgeable than the Prophet Muhammad, that the prophets were inferior in knowledge but superior in practical strength, that Pharaoh was a Coptic Plato, that the father-in-law of Musa was Plato, and that miracles are merely psychological or natural forces akin to sorcery. Ibn Taymiyya attributes these views to heretics like Ibn Sab'in and Ibn Arabi, who outwardly profess Islam.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of False Historical Claims about Aristotle and Khidr
Author refutes false historical claims about Aristotle, Khidr, and Dhul-Qarnayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify false historical claims made by philosophers and refute them using chronological evidence.

Core Idea
Chronological errors expose the ignorance of those who elevate philosophers over prophets.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks two interconnected false claims: that Aristotle was the prophet Khidr, and that Moses studied under Plato. These claims were used by philosophers and their Sufi followers to elevate philosophical knowledge above prophetic revelation. The refutation relies on a simple but devastating chronological fact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the false claims
Locate the specific claims in the passage: that Aristotle was Khidr (al-Khidr) and that Moses' father-in-law was Plato, implying Moses learned from him.
2
Understand the ideological motive
Recognize that these claims serve to prioritize philosophers over prophets in knowledge, reducing miracles to psychic powers similar to magic.
Ibn Taymiyya mentions that some claim Aaron and Ali were more knowledgeable than Moses and Muhammad respectively.
3
Examine the chronological refutation
Ibn Taymiyya points out that Plato (Aristotle's teacher) lived less than 400 years before Christ, long after Moses. Thus, Moses could not have learned from him.
This refutation uses basic historical awareness to show the impossibility of the claim.
4
Evaluate the strength of the refutation
The refutation is decisive: it does not require complex argument, only accurate chronology. The philosophers' own ignorance of history undermines their claims to superior knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسيح بنحو ثلاث مئة سنة . وكثير من الجهال يحسب أن هذا هو ذُو القَرْنَين المذكور في القرآن. ويعظم أرسطو بكونه كان وزيراً له كما ذكر ذلك ابن سينا وأمثاله من الجهال بأخبار الأمم . ومن ملاحدة المتصوفة من يزعم أن أرسطو كان هو الخضر ـ خضر موسى . وهؤلاء منهم من يفضل الفلاسفة على الأنبياء في العلم، ويقول إن هارون كان أعلم من موسى، وإن علياً كان أعلم من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، كما يزعمون أن الخضر كان أعلم من موسى؛ وأن علياً وهارون ،والخضر، كانوا فلاسفة يَعْلَمون الحقائق العقلية العلمية أكثر من موسى وعيسى، ومحمد، لكن هؤلاء كانوا في القوة العملية أكمل ، ولهذا وضعوا الشرائع العملية . وهؤلاء يفضّلون فرعون على موسى، ويسمونه أفلاطن القبطي»، وقد يقولون : إن صاحب مدين الذي تزوّج موسى بنته هو أفلاطن اليوناني، أستاذ أرسطو . ويقولون إن موسى كان أعلم من غيره بالسِّحر، وإنه استفاد ذلك من حَمْوه، إذ كان عندهم ليست المعجزات إلا قوى نفسانية، أو طبيعية، أو فلكية، من جنس السحر . موسى كان مبرزاً على غيره في ذلك . ولكن موسى إلى أمثال ذلك من المقالات التي تقولها الملاحدة المتفلسفة المنتمون إلى (۱) وأصحابه . الإسلام في الظاهر من متشيّع ومتصوف كابن سبعين (۱) وابن عربي . هذا الجنس ما يطول حكايته مما يدل على أنهم من أجهل الناس فإن أفلاطن (۱) أستاذ أرسطو كان قبل المسيح بأقل من أربع مئة سنة، وذلك بعد موسى بمدة طويلة تزيد على (۲) فكيف يتعلّم منه ؟
They venerate Aristotle because he was a minister of him, as mentioned by Ibn Sina and his like among the ignorant of the nations' histories. And among the atheist Sufis are those who claim that Aristotle was al-Khidr—Khidr of Moses. And among these are those who prefer the philosophers over the prophets in knowledge, and say that Aaron was more knowledgeable than Moses, and that Ali was more knowledgeable than the Prophet (peace be upon him), just as they claim that al-Khidr was more knowledgeable than Moses; and that Ali, Aaron, and al-Khidr were philosophers knowing intellectual, scientific truths more than Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad, but these (prophets) were more complete in practical power, and for this they laid down practical laws. And these prefer Pharaoh over Moses, calling him 'the Coptic Plato,' and they say that the man of Midian whose daughter Moses married was the Greek Plato, the teacher of Aristotle. And they say that Moses was more knowledgeable than others in magic, and that he acquired that from his father-in-law, since according to them miracles are only psychic, natural, or celestial powers, akin to magic. Moses was outstanding over others in that. But Moses to similar statements that the atheist philosophers, who outwardly belong to Islam among the Shi'a and Sufis like Ibn Sab'in and Ibn 'Arabi, say. This type (of claim) is too lengthy to recount, indicating that they are among the most ignorant of people. For Plato, the teacher of Aristotle, was less than four hundred years before Christ, and that was after Moses by a long period exceeding (gap) so how could he learn from him?
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the false historical and intellectual claims made by the philosophers and their followers among the Sufis. He begins by mentioning that some ignorant people think that Alexander (the Macedonian) is the Dhul-Qarnayn mentioned in the Quran, and they venerate Aristotle because they think he was Alexander's minister. Then he moves to the more extreme claim that Aristotle was actually al-Khidr, the companion of Moses. He details their beliefs: they prioritize philosophers over prophets in knowledge, claiming that Aaron, Ali, and al-Khidr were more knowledgeable than Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad, but that the prophets were stronger in practical action. They even prefer Pharaoh over Moses, calling him 'the Coptic Plato,' and claim that the father-in-law of Moses was the Greek Plato. They reduce miracles to sorcery. Ibn Taymiyya then exposes their ignorance of chronology: Plato lived less than 400 years before Christ, which is long after Moses, so how could Moses have learned from him? This argument demonstrates their historical errors. The commentary should highlight that Ibn Taymiyya uses simple historical facts to refute these claims, showing that the philosophers' own history contradicts their assertions.

Key Terms
الخضر
أرسطو
ذو القرنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Living al-Khidr: Identifying the Logical Error and Its Cause
Author refutes the belief that al-Khidr is alive and explains the cause of the error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the core epistemological error in the belief that al-Khidr is alive and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the claim that al-Khidr is Aristotle.

Core Idea
The belief that al-Khidr is alive stems from demonic deception, not historical evidence.

Ibn Taymiyya tackles a widespread folk belief with a twofold refutation: the historical claim that al-Khidr died long ago, and the psychological explanation that people are fooled by devils. In this lesson, we will analyse how he identifies the cause of the error and labels the claim as a 'cold lie'. This demonstrates a critical method for evaluating supernatural claims in Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims being refuted
Find in the passage the two distinct statements that Ibn Taymiyya attacks: the claim that al-Khidr is Aristotle and the claim that al-Khidr is still alive.
2
Examine the evidence for al-Khidr's death
Note that Ibn Taymiyya asserts as correct that al-Khidr died long before Aristotle. This is a historical claim used to refute the association with Aristotle and the idea of his continued life.
3
Analyse the cause of the error: demonic impersonation
Observe that Ibn Taymiyya explains why people mistakenly believe al-Khidr is alive: they encounter someone in remote places claiming to be al-Khidr, but that is a devil in human form.
4
Evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critical method
Consider how Ibn Taymiyya combines a historical refutation (al-Khidr died) with a causal explanation (demonic deception) to undermine the belief. This method is applicable to other similar supernatural claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال القول بحياة الخضر . وقولهم إن الخضر هو أرسطو» من أظهر الكذب البارد والخضر على الصواب مات قبل ذلك بزمان طويل والذين يقولون إنه حي - كبعض العباد، وبعض العامة، وكثير من اليهود والنصارى - غالطون في ذلك غلطاً لا ريب فيه . وسبب غلطهم أنهم يرون في الأماكن المنقطعة وغيرها مَنْ يُظَنُّ أنه من الزهاد، ويقول «أنا الخضر»، وقد يكون ذلك شيطاناً قد تمثل بصورة آدمي .
Refutation of the claim of the life of al-Khidr. And their statement that 'al-Khidr is Aristotle' is among the clearest of cold lies, and al-Khidr, correct, died long before that. And those who say he is alive—like some worshippers, some common people, and many of the Jews and Christians—are grossly mistaken in that, without doubt. And the cause of their error is that they see in remote places and elsewhere someone who is thought to be among the ascetics, and he says, 'I am al-Khidr,' and that may be a devil who has manifested in human form.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of two related claims: that al-Khidr is Aristotle, and that al-Khidr is still alive. Ibn Taymiyya states that the claim that al-Khidr is Aristotle is a clear and frigid lie. He asserts as correct that al-Khidr died long before the time of Aristotle. Then he addresses the widespread belief that al-Khidr is still alive, held by some worshippers, common people, and many Jews and Christians. He declares them undoubtedly mistaken. The cause of their error is that in remote places, they encounter someone pretending to be al-Khidr, but it is actually a devil appearing in human form. So, the error arises from being deceived by demonic impersonations.

Key Terms
الخضر
كذب بارد
شيطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ibn Taymiyya's Historical Critique of Philosophers' Ignorance
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes philosophers and mystics for their ignorance of prophethood and historical chronology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's chronological argument against the claim that Moses learned from Plato and explain why it refutes the philosophers' claim of prophetic ignorance.

Core Idea
Prophets preceded philosophers; chronological impossibility proves prophetic knowledge is independent of Greek thought.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks the claim that prophets like Moses learned from philosophers like Plato. He uses a simple but devastating historical fact: Plato lived centuries after Moses. This lesson unpacks his argument and shows how historical knowledge is essential for defending prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the refuted claim
Locate the claim that Moses learned from Plato, which implies that prophetic knowledge derives from philosophy.
This is implicit in the accusation that philosophers and mystics diminish the prophets.
2
Analyze the types of ignorance
Ibn Taymiyya lists three ignorances: ignorance of what prophets brought, ignorance of historical news, and ignorance of rational matters. Focus on the second as it is the basis of his argument.
3
Extract the chronological data
He states that Plato lived less than 400 years before Christ, and Moses lived long before that. The exact number is missing due to a lacuna, but the order is clear.
The chronological gap is the key evidence.
4
Draw the logical conclusion
If Plato came after Moses, then Moses could not have been his student. Therefore, the claim that prophetic knowledge comes from philosophy is false.
This is a simple but powerful refutation.
5
Understand the broader implication
This refutes the notion that prophets are inferior or that their knowledge is derived from Greek philosophy. Prophethood is independent and prior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - تنقيص المتفلسفة والمتصوفة للأنبياء المرسلين. ولم يكفهم جهلهم بما جاءت به النبوات حتى ضَمُّوا إلى ذلك الجهل بأخبار العالم وأيام الناس، والجهل بالعقليات. هذا الجنس ما يطول حكايته مما يدل على أنهم من أجهل الناس. فإن أفلاطن أستاذ أرسطو كان قبل المسيح بأقل من أربع مئة سنة، وذلك بعد موسى بمدة طويلة تزيد على فكيف يتعلَّم منه؟
The third station: Diminishing the philosophers and mystics of the sent prophets. Their ignorance of what the prophecies brought was not enough for them until they added to that ignorance of the news of the world and the days of the people, and ignorance of rational matters. This kind of thing, its narration would be lengthy, indicating that they are among the most ignorant of people. For Plato, the teacher of Aristotle, was less than four hundred years before Christ, and that was after Moses by a long period exceeding... so how could he have learned from him?
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a critique by Ibn Taymiyya of philosophers and mystics (mutafalsifa and mutasawwifa) for their ignorance regarding the prophets. He begins by stating that their ignorance of what the prophets brought is compounded by ignorance of historical facts and rational sciences. He then gives a specific example: the claim that Moses learned from Plato. Ibn Taymiyya points out that Plato lived after Moses by many centuries (the text notes a lacuna where the exact number would be), so it is impossible for Moses to have learned from Plato. This is a direct refutation of those who claim that prophetic knowledge derives from philosophy. The argument is chronological and logical: if Plato came after Moses, then Moses could not have been his student. The passage highlights the importance of sound historical knowledge in defending the integrity of prophethood.

Key Terms
المتفلسفة
المتصوفة
النبوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what historical fact refutes the claim that Moses learned from Plato?
A) Plato lived after Moses
B) Plato was a student of Moses
C) Moses lived after Plato
D) Aristotle taught Plato

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique demonstrates that sound historical knowledge is a necessary tool for defending the primacy of prophetic revelation.

Show the historical timeline between the Prophet (570 CE) and the Greek philosophers like Aristotle (384-322 BCE) or the Neoplatonists. Explain why such a claim is impossible.

Ibn Taymiyya's Conditional Argument Against al-Khidr's Life
Ibn Taymiyya uses a conditional argument to prove al-Khidr is dead.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's modus tollens argument and explain why the absence of al-Khidr from the Prophet's time proves his death.

Core Idea
If al-Khidr were alive, he would have come to the Prophet; he did not; therefore he is dead.

Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that al-Khidr is alive by invoking a conditional argument from the Quranic covenant of prophets (Al-Imran 81). He argues that if al-Khidr were still alive, he would have been obligated to support the Prophet Muhammad. Since there is no record of such an event—indeed, none of the Companions ever saw him—al-Khidr must have died before the Prophet's time. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its epistemological foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
Find the sentence: 'if al-Khidr were alive, he would have come to the Prophet, peace be upon him, to believe in him and to strive alongside him.' This is the major premise of a conditional argument.
The conditional is based on the Quranic covenant (Al-Imran 81).
2
Identify the factual premise (denial of consequent)
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya states that none of the Companions ever saw al-Khidr, and that al-Khidr did not come to the Prophet. This provides the denial of the consequent: 'He did not come to the Prophet.'
The absence of evidence is treated as evidence of absence in this case.
3
Apply modus tollens
From 'if A then B' and 'not B', we infer 'not A'. Here: if alive then comes; he did not come; therefore he is not alive (i.e., dead).
This is a valid deductive form.
4
Evaluate the strength of the conditional premise
Consider whether the conditional is universally true. Ibn Taymiyya grounds it in divine obligation: the covenant of the prophets requires every prophet (including al-Khidr, if alive) to support Muhammad. Thus the conditional is a matter of religious duty, making the argument strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال القول بحياة الخضر. وقولهم إن الخضر هو أرسطو من أظهر الكذب البارد والخضر على الصواب مات قبل ذلك بزمان طويل والذين يقولون إنه حي - كبعض العباد، وبعض العامة، وكثير من اليهود والنصارى - غالطون في ذلك غلطاً لا ريب فيه. وسبب غلطهم أنهم يرون في الأماكن المنقطعة وغيرها مَنْ يُظَنُّ أنه من الزهاد، ويقول «أنا الخضر»، وقد يكون ذلك شيطاناً قد تمثل بصورة آدمي. وهذا مما علمناه في وقائع كثيرة، حتى في مكان الذي كتبت فيه هذا عند الربوة بدمشق رأى شخص بين الجبلين صورة رجل قد سَدَّ ما بين الجبلين، وبلغ رأسه رأسَ الجبل، وقال: «أنا الخضر و أنا نقيب الأولياء». وقال للرجل الرائي: «أنت رجل صالح، وأنت ولي الله». ومد يده إلى فأس كان الرجل نسيه في مكان وهو ذاهب إليه، فناوله إياه، وكان بينه وبين ذلك المكان نحو ميل. ومثل هذه الحكاية كثير. وكل من قال : إنه رأى الخضر، وهو صادق، إما أن يتخيَّل له في نفسه أنه رآه، ويَظُنّ ما في نفسه كان في الخارج، كما يقع لكثير من أرباب الرياضات. وإما أن يكون جنّياً يتصوّر له بصورة إنسان ليُضلّه؛ وهذا كثير جداً قد علمنا منه ما يطول وصفه. وإما أن يكون رأى إنسياً ظن أنه الخضر، وهو غالط في ظنه؛ فإن قال له ذلك الجني أو الإنسي «إنه الخضر» يكون قد كذب عليه. لا يخرج الصدقُ في هذا الباب عن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة. وأما الأحاديث فكثيرة ولهذا لم ينقل عن أحد من الصحابة أنه رأى الخضر ولا اجتمع به لأنهم كانوا أكمل علماً وإيماناً من غيرهم. فلم يكن يمكن الشيطان التلبيس عليهم كما لبس على كثير من العباد ولهذا كثير من الكفار - اليهود والنصارى - رؤية الخضر يأتيهم من يظنون أنه الخضر ويحضر في كنائسهم، وربما حدثهم بأشياء؛ وإنما هو شيطان جاء إليهم، يضلّهم. ولو كان الخضر حيّاً لوجب عليه أن يأتي إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فيؤمن به، ويجاهد معه، كما أخذ الله الميثاق على الأنبياء وأتباعهم بقوله: ﴿وَإِذْ أَخَذَ اللَّهُ مِيثَاقَ النَّبِيِّينَ لَمَا آتَيْتُكُم مِّن كِتَابٍ وَحِكْمَةٍ ثُمَّ جَاءَكُمْ رَسُولٌ مُصَدِّقٌ لِمَا مَعَكُمْ لَتُؤْمِنُنَّ بِهِ وَلَتَنصُرُنَّهُ﴾ [آل عمران: 81]. والخضر قد أصلح السفينة لقوم من عُرض الناس، فكيف لا يكون بين محمد وأصحابه؟
Refutation of the statement about the life of al-Khidr. And their statement that al-Khidr is Aristotle is among the most manifest absurd lies, and al-Khidr, correctly, died a long time before that. And those who say that he is alive—like some worshippers, some common people, and many Jews and Christians—are mistaken in that without doubt. And the reason for their mistake is that they see in remote places and elsewhere someone who is thought to be of the ascetics and says 'I am al-Khidr,' and that may be a devil that has taken the form of a human. This is something we have known in many incidents, even in the place where I wrote this at al-Rabwa in Damascus, a person saw between two mountains the figure of a man who filled the space between the two mountains, and his head reached the top of the mountain, and he said: 'I am al-Khidr and I am the deputy of the saints.' And he said to the seeing man: 'You are a righteous man, and you are a friend of God.' And he extended his hand to an axe that the man had forgotten in a place and was going to it, and he handed it to him, and between him and that place was about a mile. And similar stories are many. And everyone who says that he has seen al-Khidr, being truthful, either imagines in himself that he saw him, and thinks that what was in his mind was in the external, as occurs to many of the practitioners of spiritual exercises. Or it may be a jinn that appears to him in human form to mislead him; this is very frequent, and we have known of it what would be lengthy to describe. Or he may have seen a human being whom he thought was al-Khidr, and he is mistaken in his assumption; so if that jinn or human says to him 'I am al-Khidr', then he has lied to him. The truth in this matter does not go beyond these three categories. As for the hadiths, they are many, and for this reason it is not transmitted from any of the Companions that he saw al-Khidr nor met with him, because they were more perfect in knowledge and faith than others. So the devil could not deceive them as he deceived many worshippers. For this reason many disbelievers—Jews and Christians—see al-Khidr; one whom they think is al-Khidr comes to them and attends their churches, and perhaps speaks to them about things; but it is a devil that has come to them to mislead them. And if al-Khidr were alive, he would have been obliged to come to the Prophet, peace be upon him, to believe in him and to strive alongside him, just as God took the covenant of the prophets and their followers with His saying: 'And (remember) when Allah took the covenant of the prophets, saying: "Whatever I have given you of the Book and wisdom, then later there comes to you a Messenger confirming what is with you, you must believe in him and must help him."' [Al-Imran: 81]. And al-Khidr repaired the ship for a people of ordinary people, so how would he not be with Muhammad and his Companions?
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of two false claims: that al-Khidr is Aristotle and that al-Khidr is still alive. He begins by declaring the claim that al-Khidr is Aristotle to be 'the most manifest absurd lies' and states that al-Khidr in fact died long before that. He then addresses those who believe al-Khidr is alive, labeling them as mistaken without doubt. He explains the reason for their error: they encounter individuals in secluded places who claim to be al-Khidr, but these may actually be devils (shayatin) appearing in human form. He provides a personal anecdote from his experience in Damascus at a place called al-Rabwa, where a man claimed to be al-Khidr and performed an apparent miracle (retrieving an axe from a distance). Ibn Taymiyya then classifies all truthful claims of seeing al-Khidr into three categories: (1) the person imagines the sighting within himself and mistakes an internal image for external reality, common among those who practice spiritual exercises; (2) a jinn appears to him in human form to deceive him; (3) the person sees a human being but mistakenly identifies him as al-Khidr. In all cases, if the being claims to be al-Khidr, it is a lie. He then reinforces this with a historical argument: none of the Companions of the Prophet ever reported seeing or meeting al-Khidr, because they were too perfect in knowledge and faith to be deceived by the devil. By contrast, ignorant Jews and Christians often have such experiences because they are open to demonic deception. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya presents a conditional argument: if al-Khidr were alive, he would have been obligated to come to the Prophet Muhammad, believe in him, and support him, based on the Quranic covenant of prophets (Al-Imran 81). Since he did not do so, he must be dead. The argument is a reductio: the absence of al-Khidr's presence at the time of the Prophet proves he was not alive. This passage combines direct refutation, classification of false claims, empirical testimony, and scriptural argument.

Key Terms
الخضر
شيطان
الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

What logical form does Ibn Taymiyya use to prove al-Khidr is dead?
A) Denying the antecedent
B) Affirming the consequent
C) Modus tollens
D) Hypothetical syllogism

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's argument that al-Khidr died before the Prophet is a valid modus tollens grounded in the prophetic covenant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reconstruct a similar argument using modus tollens to evaluate the claim that the Companion Zayd ibn Thabit lived until the Caliphate of Uthman based on the absence of his participation in the compilation of the Quran after Abu Bakr.
?

The Three Categories of False Claims of Seeing al-Khidr
Ibn Taymiyya classifies the three possible explanations for claimed sightings of al-Khidr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any claim of seeing al-Khidr into one of Ibn Taymiyya's three categories (imagination, jinn deception, mistaken identity) and identify the epistemological error in each.

Core Idea
Every claim of seeing al-Khidr falls into one of three epistemic errors: imagination, demonic deception, or mistaken identity.

Ibn Taymiyya provides a comprehensive classification of why people mistakenly think they have seen al-Khidr. He identifies three possible causes: internal imagination (especially in practitioners of spiritual exercises), a jinn appearing in human form to mislead, or simply misidentifying another human. This lesson helps you apply this taxonomy to evaluate similar claims and recognize that none constitutes genuine proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first category: internal imagination
Look for the part where Ibn Taymiyya says the person 'imagines in himself that he saw him, and thinks that what was in his mind was in the external.' This occurs in practitioners of spiritual exercises (arbab al-riyadat).
This is a psychological error: conflating internal image with external reality.
2
Identify the second category: jinn deception
Find where he says 'a jinn appears to him in human form to mislead him.' This is a common demonic tactic, especially among those prone to superstition.
Ibn Taymiyya claims personal knowledge of many such incidents.
3
Identify the third category: mistaken identity of a human
Find where he says the person 'sees a human being whom he thought was al-Khidr, and he is mistaken.' If that human claims to be al-Khidr, it is a lie.
This is a simple case of misidentification, perhaps due to expectation.
4
Apply the classification to a test case
Given a story (e.g., someone claims to have met al-Khidr at a mountain retreat), decide which category best fits. Consider: Was the person an ascetic? Did the figure perform supernatural acts? Could it be psychological or demonic?
Practice with the anecdote Ibn Taymiyya himself gives at al-Rabwa.

Classifying Claims of Seeing Khidr: Three Categories
Author classifies all truthful claims of seeing Khidr into three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any claim of seeing al-Khidr into one of Ibn Taymiyya's three categories and explain why each category is epistemologically flawed.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya reduces all truthful Khidr sightings to three forms of error—imagination, demonic impersonation, or mistaken identity.

Claims of seeing the prophet/saint al-Khidr are common in Islamic mystical circles. Ibn Taymiyya, in his critique of logicians and speculative theology, offers a sharp epistemological tool: every truthful report falls into exactly three categories, none of which constitute a genuine encounter. This classification helps us critically assess supernatural claims using rational and revealed criteria.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Ibn Taymiyya is addressing people who say they have seen al-Khidr and are truthful (ṣādiq). Find this phrase in the Arabic: 'وكل من قال : إنه رأى الخضر، وهو صادق'.
He is not discussing liars, only those who believe they are truthful.
2
Extract the three categories
Locate the three 'إما أن' clauses. The first is internal imagination mistaking inner thoughts for external reality. The second is a jinn taking human form to mislead. The third is seeing an actual human and mistakenly thinking it is Khidr.
The categories are exhaustive: 'لا يخرج الصدقُ في هذا الباب عن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة'.
3
Analyze the epistemological flaw in each
Category 1: confuses inner perception with outer reality. Category 2: relies on a deceiving entity. Category 3: based on mistaken identity. None provide verifiable external evidence.
Each category involves a failure of reliable perception or testimony.
4
Understand the implication for reported stories
Ibn Taymiyya notes that despite many stories, no Companion (ṣaḥāba) reported such a sighting. Their superior 'ilm and īmān prevented satanic deception. This strengthens the argument that all such claims are false.
This is an argument from silence with a theological rationale.
5
Apply to a contemporary claim
Now take a modern claim of seeing Khidr (or a similar figure like the Mahdi) and determine which category it fits. Ask: Did the person imagine it? Could it be a jinn? Did they mistake someone else?
This tool protects against accepting miraculous claims uncritically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل من قال : إنه رأى الخضر، وهو صادق، إما أن يتخيَّل له في نفسه أنه رآه ، لا يخلو عن ويَظُنّ ما في نفسه كان في الخارج، كما يقع لكثير من أرباب الرياضات . وإما أن يكون جنّياً يتصوّر له بصورة إنسان ليُضلّه ؛ وهذا كثير جداً قد علمنا منه ما يطول وصفه. وإما أن يكون رأى إنسياً ظن أنه الخضر، وهو غالط في ظنه ؛ فإن قال له ذلك الجني أو الإنسي «إنه الخضر» يكون قد كذب عليه . لا يخرج الصدقُ في هذا الباب عن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة . وأما الأحاديث فكثيرة ولهذا لم ينقل عن أحد من الصحابة أنه رأى الخضر ولا لم ينقل عن اجتمع به لأنهم كانوا أكمل علماً وإيماناً من غيرهم. فلم يكن يمكن الشيطان التلبيس عليهم كما لبس على كثير من العباد ولهذا كثير من الكفار - اليهود والنصارى - رؤية الخضر يأتيهم من يظنون أنه الخضر ويحضر في كنائسهم، وربما حدثهم بأشياء؛ وإنما هو شيطان جاء إليهم، يضلّهم.
And everyone who says that he saw al-Khidr, and is truthful, either imagines in himself that he saw him, not free from [؟] and thinks that what was in himself was external, as occurs to many people of spiritual exercises. Or it is a jinn that appears to him in the form of a human to mislead him; and this is very frequent, we have known of it much that would take long to describe. Or he saw a human and thought that he was al-Khidr, being mistaken in his assumption; for if that jinn or human says to him 'I am al-Khidr', he would have lied to him. Truth in this matter does not go beyond these three categories. As for the stories, they are numerous, and for this reason nothing has been reported from any Companion that he saw al-Khidr, nor that any met with him, because they were more complete in knowledge and faith than others. Satan could not deceive them as he deceived many worshippers, and for this reason many disbelievers – Jews and Christians – [claim] seeing al-Khidr, there comes to them one they think is al-Khidr, and he attends their churches, and perhaps tells them things; but it is a devil that came to them, misleading them.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a critical classification by Ibn Taymiyya of all purported sightings of al-Khidr. He begins by stating that any truthful claim of seeing Khidr must fall into one of three categories, and no truthful claim goes beyond these. The first category is that the person imagines seeing him internally, mistakenly believing that internal perception is external reality—a common occurrence among those engaged in spiritual exercises. The second is that a jinn (spirit) appears in human form to mislead the person, which is very common. The third is that the person actually sees a human being and mistakenly thinks it is Khidr; if that human or jinn claims to be Khidr, they are lying. After laying out these categories, the author notes that despite many stories, no Companion of the Prophet is reported to have seen Khidr, because their knowledge and faith were superior and prevented satanic deception. In contrast, many Jews and Christians are deceived by devils appearing as Khidr in their churches. The passage thus refutes any claim of a genuine encounter with Khidr.

Key Terms
الخضر
الصدق
الجني

A) The person imagines seeing him internally and mistakes inner perception for external reality.
B) A jinn appears in human form to mislead the person.
C) The person sees a prophet in a dream and interprets it as Khidr.
D) The person sees a human being and mistakenly thinks it is Khidr.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's three-category classification of Khidr sightings is a powerful epistemological tool for critically evaluating supernatural claims, rooted in the principle that any truthful report must fall into one of three flawed forms.

Ibn Taymiyya's Conditional Argument Against Khidr's Life
Ibn Taymiyya refutes Khidr's continued life via conditional reasoning from Quranic obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's conditional argument against Khidr's continued life and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
If Khidr were alive, he would have followed the Prophet; he didn't, so he isn't.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin presents a crisp logical refutation of the belief that al-Khidr is still alive. Using a conditional syllogism derived from a Quranic covenant, Ibn Taymiyya shows that the obligation on previous prophets to follow Muhammad proves Khidr cannot be alive. We will dissect this argument step by step, understanding how a conditional inference rooted in revelation can decisively settle a theological claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional claim (if-then)
The passage begins with 'if al-Khidr were alive, he would have to come to the Prophet, believe in him, and strive with him.' This is the conditional premise: if P then Q, where P = 'Khidr is alive' and Q = 'Khidr follows the Prophet'.
The verb 'وجب' indicates obligation, so the consequent is an obligation that would be realized if the antecedent were true.
2
Identify the missing premise: consequent false
Ibn Taymiyya implicitly asserts that Khidr did not come to the Prophet and believe (since he is absent from history). This is the negation of the consequent (not Q), forming a modus tollens argument.
The passage uses rhetorical questions and the fact that Khidr helped ordinary people but not Muhammad to imply the consequent is false.
3
Recognize the Quranic evidence supporting the conditional
The conditional premise is supported by Quran 3:81, which states that God took a covenant from the prophets (and their followers) that if a messenger comes confirming what they have, they must believe in and help him.
This scriptural backing transforms the logical conditional into a theological obligation.
4
Determine the logical form
The argument is a modus tollens conditional syllogism: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. P = 'Khidr is alive', Q = 'Khidr follows the Prophet'. Since he did not follow (not Q), we conclude he is not alive (not P).
Modus tollens is a valid form of inference.
5
Evaluate the argument's strength
The argument is logically valid (formally sound) provided the conditional premise is true. The truth of the premise rests on the correct interpretation of the Quranic covenant and the historical claim that Khidr did not appear in Medina.
Ibn Taymiyya's overall case is stronger if the conditional premise is accepted; critics might challenge the scope of the covenant (does it include Khidr specifically?).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان الخضر حيّاً لوجب عليه أن يأتي إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فيؤمن به، كان الواجب ويجاهد معه، كما أخذ الله الميثاق على الأنبياء وأتباعهم بقوله : ﴿وَإِذْ أَخَذَ اللَّهُ مِشَقَ النَّبِيِّينَ على الخضر لَمَاءَ اتَيْتُكُم مِّن كِتَبٍ وَحِكْمَةٍ ثُمَّ جَاءَ كُمْ رَسُولٌ مُصَدِّقُ لِمَا مَعَكُمْ لَتُؤْمِنُنَّ بِهِ، وَلَتَنصُرُنَّهُ له [سورة آل عمران : ۸۱] . والخضر قد أصلح السفينة لقوم من عُرض الناس، فكيف لا يكون بين محمد وأصحابه؟

المقام الثالث - إبطال القول بحياة الخضر
وهو إن كان نبيا فنبينا أفضل منه ؛ وإن لم يكن نبياً فأبو بكر وعمر أفضلُ منه . وهذا مبسوط في موضعه.
And if al-Khidr were alive, he would have to come to the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, and believe in him, it would be obligatory, and strive with him, as God took the covenant from the prophets and their followers by His saying: 'And when God took the covenant of the prophets: for what I have given you of the Book and wisdom, then a messenger comes to you confirming what is with you, you shall believe in him and help him' [Surah Al-Imran: 81]. And al-Khidr repaired the ship for some ordinary people, so how could he not be with Muhammad and his Companions?

The third station – Refuting the claim of al-Khidr's life
And if he was a prophet, then our prophet is better than him; and if he was not a prophet, then Abu Bakr and Umar are better than him. And this is elaborated in its place.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a logical refutation of the belief that al-Khidr is still alive. Ibn Taymiyya uses a conditional argument: if Khidr were alive, he would be obligated to come to Prophet Muhammad, believe in him, and support him, based on the Quranic verse (3:81) which commands previous prophets and their followers to believe in and help the final prophet. The author then points out that Khidr once helped some ordinary people by repairing a ship, so how could he not help Muhammad and his Companions if he were alive? The implication is that Khidr did not come, so he is not alive. The passage then introduces the 'third station' (possibly a section heading) which adds another argument: even if Khidr were a prophet, Muhammad is superior; if he were not a prophet, then Abu Bakr and Umar are superior, so Khidr's life is not significant. The argument reinforces that belief in Khidr's continued life is baseless.

Key Terms
الخضر (al-Khidr): The figure allegedly still alive; Ibn Taymiyya argues he cannot be alive because he would have had to follow Muhammad. [Note: In some traditions, Khidr is believed to be alive; here Ibn Taymiyya rejects that using a conditional argument from Quranic obligation.]
الميثاق (al-mīthāq): The covenant taken from prophets to believe in and support the final messenger; used as evidence for Khidr's obligation. [Note: No significant shift—this term is stable in Quranic exegesis.]
المقام الثالث (al-maqām al-thālith): The third station or section of the discussion, here titled 'Refuting the claim of Khidr's life'. [Note: This is a structural marker in Ibn Taymiyya's text, not a standard term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what would al-Khidr be obligated to do if he were alive?
A) He would have to wander the earth performing miracles.
B) He would have to come to the Prophet, believe in him, and strive with him.
C) He would have to remain hidden to avoid fitna.
D) He would have to oppose the Prophet to test the believers.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's refutation demonstrates how a conditional argument rooted in a clear Quranic text can decisively eliminate a baseless belief through modus tollens.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar conditional argument for the claim that the Dajjal (Antichrist) is already alive. Use the premise that if the Dajjal were alive, he would have openly claimed divinity and deceived people, but such a figure has not appeared in history. Evaluate whether this argument is valid modus tollens.

Distinguishing Dhul-Qarnayn from Alexander the Great
Author refutes the philosophers' claim that Aristotle was vizier of Dhul-Qarnayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Quranic figure Dhul-Qarnayn from the historical Alexander the Great (son of Philip) using Ibn Taymiyya's criteria of belief and historical timeline.

Core Idea
Not every Alexander in history is the Quranic Dhul-Qarnayn; theology and history must align.

Ibn Taymiyya confronts a common error among philosophers who conflate the prophet-like king Dhul-Qarnayn with the polytheist conqueror Alexander the Great. He shows that this confusion stems from relying on false transmissions and ignorance. The key distinction lies in belief (monotheism vs. polytheism) and chronology. Understanding this distinction is crucial for preserving the integrity of Quranic narratives against historical misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Locate the claim that Aristotle was the vizier of Dhul-Qarnayn. Note that this relies on equating two different figures, leading to a historical and theological error.
2
Separate the two Alexanders
Identify two figures named Alexander: one is the polytheist son of Philip (known as Alexander the Great), the other is a monotheist sometimes called Alexander son of Darius, who is identified as Dhul-Qarnayn.
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that the Alexander who had Aristotle as vizier is the polytheist son of Philip.
3
Compare their beliefs and deeds
Note that the historical Alexander (son of Philip) worshipped idols and never reached the barrier (the wall of Gog and Magog), whereas Dhul-Qarnayn was a monotheist and built the barrier according to the Quran.
4
Assess the theological implication
The philosophers' error would attribute a prophet-like figure to the service of a pagan, which is theologically impossible. The conclusion is that the two are distinct individuals with incompatible beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلامنا هنا في ضلال هؤلاء المتفلسفة الذين يبنون ضلالهم بضلال غيرهم، فيتعلقون بالكذب في المنقولات، وبالجهل في المعقولات كقولهم: «إن أرسطو وزير ذي القرنين» - المذكور في القرآن - لأنهم سمعوا أنه كان وزير الإسكندر، وذو القرنين " يقال له «الإسكندر) . وهذا من جهلهم. فإن الإسكندر الذي وَزَر له أرسطو هو ابن فيلبس المَقْدُوني الذي يؤرخ له تاريخ الروم، المعروف عند اليهود والنصارى. وهو إنما ذهب إلى أرض الفرس، لم يصل إلى السدّ عند من يعرف أخباره. وكان مشركاً يعبد الأصنام. وكذلك أرسطو وقومه كانوا مشركين يعبدون الأصنام.
وذو القرنين كان موحداً مؤمناً ،بالله ، وكان متقدماً على هذا. ومن يسميه كان «الإسكندر» يقول : هو الإسكندر بن دارا .
And our speech here is about the misguidance of these philosophers who build their misguidance on the misguidance of others, clinging to falsehood in transmitted reports and ignorance in rational matters, such as their saying: 'Aristotle is the vizier of Dhul-Qarnayn' – mentioned in the Quran – because they heard that he was the vizier of Alexander, and Dhul-Qarnayn is called 'Alexander'. And this is from their ignorance. For Alexander, whom Aristotle served as vizier, is the son of Philip the Macedonian, by whose era the Romans date, known among the Jews and Christians. He only went to the land of Persia, he did not reach the barrier according to those who know his history. And he was a polytheist worshipping idols. Likewise, Aristotle and his people were polytheists worshipping idols.
And Dhul-Qarnayn was a monotheist, a believer in God, and he came before this one. And those who call him 'Alexander' say: he is Alexander, son of Darius.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' confusion between Alexander the Great and the Quranic figure Dhul-Qarnayn. Ibn Taymiyya explains that the philosophers rely on false transmitted reports and ignorance to claim that Aristotle was the vizier of Dhul-Qarnayn, whom they equate with Alexander. The author distinguishes two Alexanders: the known Alexander (son of Philip) was a polytheist who never reached the barrier (the wall of Gog and Magog), while Dhul-Qarnayn (some say Alexander son of Darius) was a monotheist and earlier in time. The author points out that the polytheistic nature of Aristotle and his patron Alexander contradicts the piety of Dhul-Qarnayn. Thus, the philosophers' historical claim is both factually wrong and theologically problematic, as it attributes a prophet-like figure to the service of a pagan.

أرسطو
الإسكندر
ذو القرنين

C) Alexander was a polytheist, Dhul-Qarnayn was a monotheist.

Key Takeaway
