The Sufficiency of a Single Premise: Ibn Taymiyya's Example of Intoxicants
Author introduces the third station: further demonstration that a single premise may suffice, using the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that a single premise can suffice for a conclusion, and identify the conditions under which it does so.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shows that when a premise is clear and accepted, one premise can be enough.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya introduces the third station of his discussion: further demonstrating that a single premise can be sufficient. He uses the example of the hadith prohibiting every intoxicating drink to illustrate that if the opponent accepts that hadith, then from that single premise they can conclude that a specific intoxicant is forbidden. This challenges the Aristotelian requirement for two premises in a syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Ibn Taymiyya claims that a single premise can suffice. This is the central thesis of this passage.
Look for the phrase 'المقدمة الواحدة قد تكفي'.
2
Find the example
He gives the hadith that the Prophet prohibited every intoxicating drink (كل شراب أسكر). This is the single premise.
The premise is general: all intoxicants are forbidden.
3
Understand the condition
The premise suffices only if the opponent accepts the premise and the entailment is clear (i.e., the specific drink is intoxicating).
Ibn Taymiyya imagines an opponent who knows a drink is khamr but might dispute the ruling.
4
Analyze the dialectical scenario
If the opponent disputes the premise (e.g., does not accept that khamr is forbidden), then additional premises are needed. The single premise suffices only when the premise is accepted.
This shows Ibn Taymiyya's practical epistemology.
5
Contrast with standard logic
In Aristotelian logic, a categorical syllogism requires two premises. Here, the minor premise (this drink is intoxicating) is obvious, so only the major premise is stated.
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the strict two-premise requirement is unnecessary when entailment is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - مزيد البيان أن المقدمة الواحدة قد تكفي

وهذه الأحاديث في الصحيح. وهي وأضعافها معروفة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تدل على أنه حرّم كل شراب أسكر.

فإن قال: أنا أعلم أنه خمر، لكن لا أسلّم أن الخمر حرام أو لا أسلم أنه حرام مطلقاً أثبت هذه المقدمة الثالثة. وهلم جراً.
The third station - further demonstration that the single premise may suffice. These hadith are in the Sahih. They and their many equivalents are well-known from the Prophet, peace be upon him, indicating that he prohibited every intoxicating drink. If he says: I know it is khamr, but I do not accept that khamr is forbidden, or I do not accept that it is absolutely forbidden, then this third premise is established. And so on.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a critical demonstration that a single premise can suffice for a conclusion. Ibn Taymiyya provides an example from the hadith: the Prophet forbade every intoxicating drink. This single premise (that every intoxicant is forbidden) is sufficient to conclude that a specific intoxicating drink is forbidden, provided the opponent accepts the authority of the hadith. The dialectical exchange shows that if someone disputes the premises, additional premises may be needed, but the point is that in principle, one premise can be enough if its entailment is clear and accepted.

Key Terms
المقدمة الواحدة
الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the Prophet said so without need for evidence.
D) Because the opponent does not dispute the premise.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that a single premise can be sufficient when the entailment is clear and the premise is accepted, challenging the rigid requirement for two-premise syllogisms.

Is Knowledge of Two Premises Enough? The Need for Attention
Author discusses the sufficiency of knowledge of two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why knowledge of two premises is not always sufficient for knowledge of the conclusion, using Ibn Sina's mule example.

Core Idea
Knowing premises is not enough; attention to the link is the third factor.

Ibn Taymiyya engages with a classic epistemological question: if you know both premises, do you automatically know the conclusion? He presents Ibn Sina's example of the mule to show that mere knowledge is insufficient without attentiveness (tafattun). This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Premises in the Mule Example
The person knows that the animal is a mule (first premise) and that mules do not give birth (second premise). These are known facts.
2
Recognize the Gap Between Premises and Conclusion
Despite knowing both premises, the person sees a mule with a swollen belly and asks if it is pregnant. The conclusion ('this mule is not pregnant') is not drawn automatically.
This shows that knowledge of premises does not guarantee the conclusion is derived.
3
Understand the Role of Attention (al-tafattun)
The third factor is the act of becoming attentive to the entailment. When reminded, the person suddenly realizes the implication. Ibn Sina argues that this attentiveness is necessary.
Ibn Taymiyya agrees with Ibn Sina on this point.
4
Apply to the Islamic Example of Intoxicating Nabidh
A believer may know that 'every intoxicant is forbidden' and that 'nabidh is intoxicating', yet fail to conclude that nabidh is forbidden if unmindful. The reminder (middle term) triggers attention.
This illustrates how the same logic applies in fiqh discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول:

إذا كانت اللوازم منها ما لُزُومه للملزوم بين بنفسه لا يحتاج إلى «دليل» يتوسط بينهما، فهذا نفس تصوره تصور الملزوم يكفي في العلم بثبوته له. وإذا كان بينهما «وسط»، فذاك «الوسط» إذا كان لزومه للملزوم الأول ، ولزوم الثاني له، بيناً، لم يفتقر إلى «وسط» ثان .

وإن كان أحد الملزومين غير بين بنفسه احتاج إلى وسط. وإن لم يكن واحد منهما بيناً احتاج إلى وسطين. وهذا «الوسط» هو حد تكفي فيه مقدمة واحدة . فإذا طلب الدليل على تحريم النبيذ المسكر فقيل : لأنه قد صح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: كل مسكر خمر أو كل مسكر حرام . فهذا «الوسط»، وهو قول النبي ، لا يفتقر عند المؤمن لزوم تحريم المسكر له إلى «وسط»، ولا يفتقر لزوم تحريم النبيذ المتنازع فيه لتحريم المسكر إلى وسط. فإن كل أحد يعلم أنه إذا حُرّم كل مسكر حرم النبيذ المسكر المتنازع فيه، وكل مؤمن يعلم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا حرم شيئاً حرم . ولو قال : الدليل على تحريمه أنه مسكر فالمخاطَب إن كان يعرف أن ذلك مسكر، والمسكر محرّم سَلَّم له التحريم ولكنه كان غافلاً عن كونه مسكراً، أو جاهلاً بكونه مسكراً.

وكذلك إذا قال : لأنه خمر فإن أقرّ أنه خمر ثبت التحريم. وإذا أقر بعد إنكاره فقد يكون كان جاهلاً فعلم، أو غافلاً فذكر . فليس كل من علم شيئاً كان ذاكراً له .

الخلاف في أن العلم بالمقدمتين كافٍ في العلم بالنتيجة أم لا؟

ولهذا تنازع هؤلاء المنطقيون في العلم بالمقدمتين هل هو كاف في العلم بالنتيجة، أم لا بد من التفطُن لأمر ثالث؟ وهذا الثاني هو قول ابن سينا وغيره. قالوا بالاحتياج إلى مقدمة ثالثة لأن الإنسان قد يكون عالماً بـ «أن البغلة لا تلد»، ثم يغفل عن ذلك ويرى بغلة منتفخة البطن، فيقول: «أهذه حامل أم لا؟ فيقال له : «أما تعلم أنها بغلة؟» فيقول : «بلى، ويقال له : أما تعلم أن البغلة لا تلد؟ فيقول : بلى». قال: فحينئذ يتفطن لكونها لا تلد.
We say: If among entailed consequences are those whose entailment to the entailing thing is clear in itself, not needing an evidence that mediates between them, then the mere conception of the entailing thing suffices for knowledge of its existence (attribution). If there is a 'middle' between them, then that 'middle', if its entailment to the first entailing thing and the entailment of the second to it are clear, does not require a second middle. If one of the two entailments is not clear in itself, it requires a middle. If neither is clear, it requires two middles. And this 'middle' is a boundary in which a single premise suffices. So if one seeks evidence for the prohibition of intoxicating nabidh, and it is said: because it has been authentically reported from the Prophet that he said: every intoxicant is khamr, or every intoxicant is forbidden. So this 'middle' — which is the Prophet's statement — does not require, in the case of the believer, for the entailment of the prohibition of the intoxicant to him, a 'middle', nor does it require for the entailment of the prohibition of the contested intoxicating nabidh to the prohibition of the intoxicant a 'middle'. For everyone knows that if every intoxicant is forbidden, the intoxicating nabidh in question is forbidden, and every believer knows that if the Prophet has prohibited something, it is prohibited. And if one says: the evidence for its prohibition is that it is intoxicating — then the addressee, if he knows that it is intoxicating and that the intoxicating is prohibited, he will accept the prohibition, but he was either unaware of its being intoxicating or ignorant. Similarly, if he says: because it is khamr, and if he acknowledges that it is khamr, the prohibition is established. If he acknowledges after denying, he may have been ignorant and came to know, or unaware and was reminded. For not everyone who knows something is mindful of it.

The disagreement is about whether knowledge of the two premises is sufficient for knowledge of the conclusion or not? Hence these logicians disputed about 'knowledge of the two premises': is it sufficient for knowledge of the conclusion, or is a third matter necessary for attention? And this latter is the view of Ibn Sina and others. They said: there is need for a third premise because a person may know that 'the mule does not give birth', then he is unmindful of that and sees a mule with a swollen belly, and says: 'Is this pregnant or not?' He is told: 'Do you not know that it is a mule?' He says: 'Yes.' And he is told: 'Do you not know that the mule does not give birth?' He says: 'Yes.' He said: then at that point he becomes attentive to the fact that it does not give birth.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
Ibn Taymiyya continues his discussion on the sufficiency of premises. He explains that if an entailment is clear, mere conception of the entailing thing suffices. If a middle term is needed, if its entailment is clear, no further middle is required. He applies this to the prohibition of intoxicating nabidh: the Prophet's statement 'every intoxicant is forbidden' serves as a clear middle term that does not need further proof for a believer. The believer knows that if the Prophet said it, it is true. He also addresses scenarios where the opponent may be unaware or ignorant. He then brings up the logicians' disagreement about whether knowledge of two premises is sufficient for knowledge of the conclusion. Ibn Sina's example of the mule illustrates that mere knowledge of two premises (that this is a mule and that mules do not give birth) is not sufficient; one must also be attentive to the connection. The third factor is the act of attention (al-tafattun). Thus, Ibn Taymiyya engages with the epistemological requirement of awareness in inference.

Key Terms
العلم بالمقدمتين
التفطن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's example of the mule, why is knowledge of the two premises not sufficient for knowledge of the conclusion?
A) Because the premises are false.
B) Because one may be unmindful of the premises.
C) Because the conclusion requires a third premise.
D) Because the mule example is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of premises alone is insufficient; attentiveness (tafattun) is a necessary third factor for deriving the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following scenario: A student knows that 'All khamr is forbidden' (from the hadith 'every intoxicant is khamr') and that 'Nabidh is intoxicating', yet does not immediately conclude that nabidh is forbidden. Using Ibn Sina's mule example, explain why this can happen and what is needed for the conclusion to be drawn.

Classification of Consequences and Middle Terms
Classification of consequences and middle terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify consequences as self-evident or needing a middle term, and determine the number of middle terms required in a proof.

Core Idea
Some consequences are self-evident; others require a middle term, and sometimes multiple.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logician's assumption that all proofs require complex syllogisms by distinguishing consequences that are self-evident from those that need mediation. In this lesson, we analyze his classification and its implications for structuring proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of consequences
Find in the Arabic where Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes consequences that are self-evident (bayan bi-nafsihi) from those that require a mediating proof. Note the phrase 'لا يحتاج إلى دليل يتوسط'.
The self-evident case is when mere conception of the first gives knowledge of the second.
2
Understand the condition for self-evidence
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'if the consequence of the consequent for the consequent is self-evident, the mere conception of the consequent suffices'. This means no additional premise is needed.
3
Determine the number of middle terms needed
If both consequences (first to middle and middle to second) are self-evident, one middle term suffices. If one is not self-evident, one middle term is needed. If neither is self-evident, two middle terms are needed.
The logic: each non-self-evident step requires a mediating premise.
4
Relate to the concept of 'hadd'
Ibn Taymiyya says this middle term is a 'hadd' (boundary) for which one premise suffices. That means a single authoritative proposition can serve as the mediating link if its implications are clear.
This is a departure from Aristotelian syllogisms requiring two premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا كانت اللوازم منها ما لُزُومه للملزوم بين بنفسه لا يحتاج إلى دليل يتوسط بينهما، فهذا نفس تصوره تصور الملزوم يكفي في العلم بثبوته له. وإذا كان بينهما وسط، فذاك الوسط إذا كان لزومه للملزوم الأول، ولزوم الثاني له، بيناً، لم يفتقر إلى وسط ثان. وإن كان أحد الملزومين غير بين بنفسه احتاج إلى وسط. وإن لم يكن واحد منهما بيناً احتاج إلى وسطين. وهذا الوسط هو حد تكفي فيه مقدمة واحدة. فإذا طلب الدليل على تحريم النبيذ المسكر فقيل : لأنه قد صح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: كل مسكر خمر أو كل مسكر حرام. فهذا الوسط، وهو قول النبي، لا يفتقر عند المؤمن لزوم تحريم المسكر له إلى وسط، ولا يفتقر لزوم تحريم النبيذ المتنازع فيه لتحريم المسكر إلى وسط. فإن كل أحد يعلم أنه إذا حُرّم كل مسكر حرم النبيذ المسكر المتنازع فيه، وكل مؤمن يعلم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا حرم شيئاً حرم. ولو قال : الدليل على تحريمه أنه مسكر فالمخاطَب إن كان يعرف أن ذلك مسكر، والمسكر محرّم سَلَّم له التحريم ولكنه كان غافلاً عن كونه مسكراً، أو جاهلاً بكونه مسكراً. وكذلك إذا قال : لأنه خمر فإن أقرّ أنه خمر ثبت التحريم. وإذا أقر بعد إنكاره فقد يكون كان جاهلاً فعلم، أو غافلاً فذكر. فليس كل من علم شيئاً كان ذاكراً له.
If among the consequences there are those whose consequence for the consequent is self-evident and does not require a proof that mediates between them, then the mere conception of the consequent suffices for knowledge of its attribution. And if there is a middle term between them, and that middle term's consequence for the first consequent and its consequence for the second are self-evident, it does not need a second middle term. If one of the two consequents is not self-evident, it needs a middle term. If neither is self-evident, it needs two middle terms. And this middle term is a boundary (hadd) for which one premise suffices. If proof is sought for the prohibition of intoxicating nabidh, it is said: Because it is authentically reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' or 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' So this middle term, which is the saying of the Prophet, does not require for the believer that the consequence of the prohibition of the intoxicant for it needs a middle term, nor does the consequence of the prohibition of disputed nabidh for the prohibition of the intoxicant need a middle term. For everyone knows that if every intoxicant is forbidden, then the disputed intoxicating nabidh is forbidden, and every believer knows that if the Prophet forbade something, it is forbidden. And if he says: The proof of its prohibition is that it is intoxicating, then the addressee, if he knows that it is intoxicating and the intoxicating is forbidden, he concedes the prohibition, but he was heedless of its being intoxicating, or ignorant of its being intoxicating. Likewise if he says: Because it is khamr, then if he acknowledges that it is khamr, the prohibition is established. If he acknowledges after denial, he may have been ignorant then learned, or heedless then remembered. So not everyone who knows something is mindful of it.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological explanation of the role of middle terms (awasit) in proofs. Ibn Taymiyya begins by classifying consequences: some are self-evident, requiring no mediation; others need a middle term. If both premises are self-evident, one middle term suffices; if one is not, more may be needed. He then applies this to the example of proving the prohibition of intoxicating nabidh. The middle term is the prophetic statement 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (or 'Every intoxicant is khamr'). For a believer, the implication is self-evident—if the Prophet forbade something, it is forbidden, and if all intoxicants are forbidden, then nabidh as an intoxicant is included. However, the addressee may be ignorant or heedless of the fact that nabidh is intoxicating or that it falls under the category of khamr. Thus, the proof works by reminding or informing, not by constructing a complex syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya stresses that knowledge does not guarantee constant mindfulness; a person may know something but forget it. This is a key point against logicians who assume that possessing premises automatically yields the conclusion.

Key Terms
الوسط
اللزوم
المقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, if one of the two consequents is not self-evident, how many middle terms are needed?
A) None
B) One
C) Two
D) It depends on the case

Key Takeaway
Knowing when a consequence is self-evident saves the need for unnecessary premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the Qur'anic verse 'Forbidden to you are carrion, blood, and pork' (5:3), is the prohibition of consuming blood self-evident or does it require a middle term? Explain using Ibn Taymiyya's framework.

The Example of Nabidh and Prophetic Authority
Application to the prohibition of intoxicating nabidh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Taymiyya's framework to the specific case of nabidh and explain why a believer does not need multiple premises when relying on a prophetic statement.

Core Idea
The Prophet's statement serves as a self-evident middle term for believers.

Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of proving that intoxicating nabidh is forbidden to illustrate his theory of middle terms. The middle term is the Prophet's hadith. For a believer, the implications are self-evident. This lesson dissects the reasoning and its assumptions about faith and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in the nabidh example
Ibn Taymiyya says the proof for the prohibition of nabidh is 'because it is authentically reported from the Prophet that he said: Every intoxicant is forbidden.' So the middle term is the prophetic statement itself.
This is a textual authority, not a logical abstraction.
2
Understand why the consequence from the hadith to the ruling is self-evident
For a believer, the Prophet's command is binding. So if the Prophet says 'every intoxicant is forbidden', the believer immediately accepts that intoxicants are forbidden. No further proof is needed.
This relies on the epistemological principle that prophetic statements are certain for a believer.
3
Understand why the consequence from that ruling to nabidh is self-evident
If all intoxicants are forbidden, and nabidh is intoxicating, then nabidh is forbidden. This is a simple class inclusion that is self-evident once the major premise and the fact of intoxication are known.
The minor premise (nabidh is intoxicating) may need to be established, but that is a factual matter, not a logical middle term.
4
Discuss the role of ignorance and heedlessness
Ibn Taymiyya notes that the addressee may be ignorant that nabidh is intoxicating, or may know but be heedless. The proof works by reminding or informing, not by constructing a multi-step syllogism. The key is that the middle term (the hadith) is sufficient.
This highlights a practical dimension: proofs often serve to awaken knowledge already possessed in principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا كانت اللوازم منها ما لُزُومه للملزوم بين بنفسه لا يحتاج إلى دليل يتوسط بينهما، فهذا نفس تصوره تصور الملزوم يكفي في العلم بثبوته له. وإذا كان بينهما وسط، فذاك الوسط إذا كان لزومه للملزوم الأول، ولزوم الثاني له، بيناً، لم يفتقر إلى وسط ثان. وإن كان أحد الملزومين غير بين بنفسه احتاج إلى وسط. وإن لم يكن واحد منهما بيناً احتاج إلى وسطين. وهذا الوسط هو حد تكفي فيه مقدمة واحدة. فإذا طلب الدليل على تحريم النبيذ المسكر فقيل : لأنه قد صح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: كل مسكر خمر أو كل مسكر حرام. فهذا الوسط، وهو قول النبي، لا يفتقر عند المؤمن لزوم تحريم المسكر له إلى وسط، ولا يفتقر لزوم تحريم النبيذ المتنازع فيه لتحريم المسكر إلى وسط. فإن كل أحد يعلم أنه إذا حُرّم كل مسكر حرم النبيذ المسكر المتنازع فيه، وكل مؤمن يعلم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا حرم شيئاً حرم. ولو قال : الدليل على تحريمه أنه مسكر فالمخاطَب إن كان يعرف أن ذلك مسكر، والمسكر محرّم سَلَّم له التحريم ولكنه كان غافلاً عن كونه مسكراً، أو جاهلاً بكونه مسكراً. وكذلك إذا قال : لأنه خمر فإن أقرّ أنه خمر ثبت التحريم. وإذا أقر بعد إنكاره فقد يكون كان جاهلاً فعلم، أو غافلاً فذكر. فليس كل من علم شيئاً كان ذاكراً له.
If among the consequences there are those whose consequence for the consequent is self-evident and does not require a proof that mediates between them, then the mere conception of the consequent suffices for knowledge of its attribution. And if there is a middle term between them, and that middle term's consequence for the first consequent and its consequence for the second are self-evident, it does not need a second middle term. If one of the two consequents is not self-evident, it needs a middle term. If neither is self-evident, it needs two middle terms. And this middle term is a boundary (hadd) for which one premise suffices. If proof is sought for the prohibition of intoxicating nabidh, it is said: Because it is authentically reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' or 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' So this middle term, which is the saying of the Prophet, does not require for the believer that the consequence of the prohibition of the intoxicant for it needs a middle term, nor does the consequence of the prohibition of disputed nabidh for the prohibition of the intoxicant need a middle term. For everyone knows that if every intoxicant is forbidden, then the disputed intoxicating nabidh is forbidden, and every believer knows that if the Prophet forbade something, it is forbidden. And if he says: The proof of its prohibition is that it is intoxicating, then the addressee, if he knows that it is intoxicating and the intoxicating is forbidden, he concedes the prohibition, but he was heedless of its being intoxicating, or ignorant of its being intoxicating. Likewise if he says: Because it is khamr, then if he acknowledges that it is khamr, the prohibition is established. If he acknowledges after denial, he may have been ignorant then learned, or heedless then remembered. So not everyone who knows something is mindful of it.

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological explanation of the role of middle terms (awasit) in proofs. Ibn Taymiyya begins by classifying consequences: some are self-evident, requiring no mediation; others need a middle term. If both premises are self-evident, one middle term suffices; if one is not, more may be needed. He then applies this to the example of proving the prohibition of intoxicating nabidh. Thus, the proof works by reminding or informing, not by constructing a complex syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya stresses that knowledge does not guarantee constant mindfulness; a person may know something but forget it. This is a key point against logicians who assume that possessing premises automatically yields the conclusion.

According to Ibn Taymiyya, why does the believer not need a second middle term in the nabidh proof?
A) Because the hadith has multiple transmission chains

C) Because nabidh is explicitly mentioned in the hadith

Ibn Sina's Third Condition and al-Razi's Objection
Ibn Taymiyya presents the dispute between Ibn Sina and al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Sina's argument for a third condition (attention) and al-Razi's objection that it leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
The dispute over a third condition exposes a hidden assumption that only two premises suffice.

Ibn Sina argued that knowing both premises does not automatically give the conclusion unless one 'attends' to their relation. Al-Razi objected that if this attention is a separate premise, it leads to infinite regress. Ibn Taymiyya presents this debate to set up his own critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Sina's claim
Find the passage where Ibn Sina states that knowledge of the two premises is not sufficient; a third thing (attention) is needed. Note the mule example he uses to illustrate forgetfulness.
The key term is 'tafattun' (attention).
2
Extract al-Razi's objection
Locate al-Razi's response. He argues that if the subsumption (indiraj) is different from the premises, it becomes an additional premise, leading to infinite regress. If it is not different, it cannot be a condition.
This is a dilemma: either way, the third condition fails.
3
Analyze the logical structure of the objection
Break down al-Razi's reasoning: He presents two conditional statements. First: if subsumption is different → infinite regress. Second: if not different → not a valid condition. The conclusion is that the third condition is untenable.
This form is a dilemma (qiyas munfasil).
4
Identify the hidden assumption
Notice that both Ibn Sina and al-Razi assume that a syllogism must have exactly two premises. Ibn Taymiyya later challenges this assumption.
This assumption is the root of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا تنازع هؤلاء المنطقيون في العلم بالمقدمتين هل هو كاف في العلم بالنتيجة، أم لا بد من التفطُن لأمر ثالث؟ وهذا الثاني هو قول ابن سينا وغيره. قالوا : بالاحتياج إلى مقدمة ثالثة لأن الإنسان قد يكون عالماً بأن البغلة لا تلد، ثم يغفل عن ذلك ويرى بغلة منتفخة البطن، فيقول: أهذه حامل أم لا؟ فيقال له : أما تعلم أنها بغلة؟ فيقول : بلى، ويقال له : أما تعلم أن البغلة لا تلد؟ فيقول : بلى. قال: فحينئذ يتفطن لكونها لا تلد. ونازعه الرازي وغيره وقالوا هذا ضعيف؛ لأن اندراج إحدى المقدمتين تحت الأخرى إن كان مغايراً للمقدمتين كان ذلك مقدمة أخرى لا بد فيها من الإنتاج، ويكون الكلام في كيفية التيامها مع الأوليين كالكلام في كيفية التيام الأوليين، ويفضي ذلك إلى اعتبار ما لا نهاية له من المقدمات. وإن لم يكن ذلك معلوماً مغايراً للمقدمتين استحال أن يكون شرطاً في الإنتاج، لأن الشرط مغاير للمشروط، وهنا لا مغايرة، فلا يكون شرطاً. وأما حديث البغلة فذلك إنما يمكن إذا كان الحاضِرُ في الذهن إحدى المقدمتين فقط، إما الصغرى وإما الكبرى. وأما عند اجتماعهما في الذهن فلا نسلّم أنه يمكن الشك أصلاً في النتيجة. قلت : وحقيقة الأمر أن هذا النزاع لزمهم في ظَنّهم الحاجة إلى مقدمتين فقط، وليس الأمر كذلك، بل المحتاج إليه هو ما به يُعْلَم المطلوب، سواء كانت مقدمة، أو ثنتين أو ثلاثاً. والمغفول عنه ليس بمعلوم حال الغفلة، فإذا تذكر صار معلوماً بالفعل. وهنا الدليل هو العلم بأن البغلة لا تحبل، وهذه المقدمة كان ذاهلاً عنها، فلم يكن عالماً بها العلم الذي تحصل به الدلالة. فإن المغفول عنه لا يدل حين ما يكون مغفولاً عنه، بل إنما يدل حال كونه مذكوراً، إذ هو بذلك يكون معلوماً علماً حاضراً. والرب تعالى منزّه عن الغفلة والنسيان، لأن ذلك يناقض حقيقة العلم، كما أنه تنزيه الرب منزه عن السِّنَة والنوم، لأن ذلك يناقض كمال الحياة والقيوميّة، فإن النوم أخو الموت. ولهذا كان أهل الجنة لا ينامون كما لا يموتون، وكانوا يُلْهَمُون التسبيحَ كما يُلهم أحدنا النَفَس. والمقصود هنا أن وجه الدليل العلم بلزوم المدلول له سواء سُمّى استحضاراً أو تفطُّناً أو غير ذلك. فمتى استحضر في ذهنه لزوم المدلول له علم أنه دال عليه وهذا اللزوم إن كان بيناً له، وإلا فقد يحتاج في بيانه إلى مقدمة، أو ثنتين، أو ثلاثة، أو أكثر.
And for this reason these logicians disputed regarding knowledge of the two premises: is it sufficient for knowledge of the conclusion, or must there be attention to a third thing? The second is the statement of Ibn Sina and others. They said: there is need for a third premise because a person may know that the mule does not give birth, then he neglects that and sees a mule with a swollen belly, and says: Is this pregnant or not? It is said to him: Do you not know that it is a mule? He says: Yes. And it is said to him: Do you not know that the mule does not give birth? He says: Yes. He said: Then at that moment he becomes attentive to the fact that it does not give birth. Al-Razi and others disputed him and said: This is weak, because the subsumption of one premise under the other, if it is different from the two premises, then that would be another premise that is necessary for production, and the discussion about how it combines with the first two is similar to the discussion about how the first two combine, and that leads to positing an infinite number of premises. If it is not known to be different from the two premises, it cannot be a condition for production, because a condition is different from the conditioned, and here there is no difference, so it is not a condition. As for the mule story, that is only possible if only one of the two premises is present in the mind, either the minor or the major. As for when both are present together in the mind, we do not concede that doubt in the conclusion is possible at all. I say: The truth of the matter is that this dispute arose from their assumption that only two premises are needed, but it is not so. Rather, what is needed is that by which the sought knowledge is known, whether it is one premise, two, or three. And what is neglected is not known during the state of neglect. When it is remembered, it becomes actually known. Here the proof is the knowledge that the mule does not conceive, and this premise he was heedless of, so he was not knowledgeable of it with the knowledge that yields indication. For what is neglected does not indicate when it is neglected, but only indicates when it is mentioned, because by that it becomes known as present knowledge. And the Lord, exalted, is free from heedlessness and forgetfulness, because that contradicts the reality of knowledge, just as the Lord is free from slumber and sleep, because that contradicts the perfection of life and self-subsistence, for sleep is the brother of death. For this reason, the people of Paradise do not sleep just as they do not die, and they are inspired with glorification as one of us is inspired with breath. And the intended point here is that the aspect of the proof is knowledge of the implication of the thing indicated, whether it is called 'summoning' or 'attention' or something else. So whenever one summons to his mind the implication of the thing indicated, he knows that it indicates it. And this implication, if it is self-evident to him, then it is fine; otherwise, it may need in its clarification one premise, or two, or three, or more.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of a dispute among logicians, particularly Ibn Sina and al-Razi, over whether knowing both premises automatically yields the conclusion. Ibn Sina argues that a third condition—attention (tatabbur)—is needed, citing the example of a person who knows that mules do not give birth but is momentarily heedless and doubts a mule's pregnancy. Al-Razi objects that if the subsumption (indiraj) of the minor under the major is different from the premises, it becomes an infinite regress; if not, it cannot be a condition. Ibn Taymiyya resolves the debate by rejecting the premise that only two premises are needed. He states that what is required is the knowledge that indicates the sought conclusion, whether from one premise or more. Forgetfulness negates actual knowledge; when remembered, the premise becomes active. Thus, the mule example works because the major premise was forgotten. Ibn Taymiyya then relates this to divine omniscience, noting that God is free from forgetfulness. He concludes that the key is the presence of the implication (luzum) in the mind, whether immediate or requiring further premises.

Key Terms
التفطن
اندراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what happens if the subsumption (indiraj) is different from the two premises?
A) It becomes a valid condition for production.
B) It becomes another premise leading to infinite regress.
C) It eliminates the need for attention.
D) It proves that only two premises are sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's third condition leads to a dilemma: either infinite regress or it cannot be a condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proof for God's existence: 'The world is originated. Everything originated has an originator.' If a person knows both premises but forgets the second while looking at a complex phenomenon, does the proof hold? Use Ibn Sina's and al-Razi's arguments to discuss.

intermediate

Ibn Taymiyya's Resolution: Knowledge Without Fixed Premise Count
Ibn Taymiyya offers his resolution of the dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's solution to the dispute by rejecting the assumption that only two premises are needed.

Core Idea
The number of premises is not fixed; what matters is knowledge of the implication.

Ibn Taymiyya cuts through the debate by arguing that the logicians' dispute rests on a false assumption: that exactly two premises are necessary. He shows that the required knowledge may come from one premise or many, and forgetfulness affects the actual presence of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption Ibn Taymiyya rejects
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that the dispute arose from assuming only two premises are needed. He says 'ليس الأمر كذلك' (it is not so).
This is the key to his critique.
2
Understand his alternative
Read his statement: 'بل المحتاج إليه هو ما به يُعْلَم المطلوب، سواء كانت مقدمة، أو ثنتين أو ثلاثاً' — what is needed is whatever yields the sought knowledge, whether one, two, or three premises.
This removes the rigid premise count.
3
Apply his analysis to the mule example
Ibn Taymiyya explains that the doubt occurred because the major premise (mules do not give birth) was forgotten (مغفول عنه). When remembered, it becomes active knowledge. The forgetfulness means it wasn't actually known at that moment.
Thus, the example does not prove need for a third premise, only that memory is fallible.
4
Identify the core concept: luzum (implication)
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the key is the presence of the implication (luzum) in the mind: 'فمتى استحضر في ذهنه لزوم المدلول له علم أنه دال عليه'.
This is his positive account.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya's resolution, why did the person in the mule example doubt the conclusion?
A) He was unaware of the minor premise (that the animal is a mule).
B) He did not know the major premise (that mules do not give birth).
C) He had forgotten the major premise at the moment of doubt.
D) He lacked a third premise to subsume the minor under the major.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya resolves the dispute by rejecting fixed premise count and emphasizing the presence of implication (luzum).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a qiyas (analogical reasoning) in fiqh: the prohibition of wine (khamr) is due to intoxication; consider a new intoxicating substance. If the jurist knows the original rule and the 'illa but forgets the 'illa at the moment of ruling, does the analogy hold? Use Ibn Taymiyya's resolution to explain why remembering the 'illa is necessary for the ruling to be valid.

Divine Omniscience and the Absence of Forgetfulness
Ibn Taymiyya applies his analysis to divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to connect the logical concept of forgetfulness with Islamic theological attributes of God.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is perfect and thus free from forgetfulness, just as He is free from sleep.

Ibn Taymiyya extends his epistemological analysis to theology. Since forgetfulness negates actual knowledge, and God's knowledge is perfect, God cannot be subject to forgetfulness. He parallels this with the attribute of life: God does not sleep because sleep is akin to death.

Guided Path
1
Note the transition from logic to theology
After discussing the mule example, Ibn Taymiyya says 'والرب تعالى منزّه عن الغفلة والنسيان' – God is free from heedlessness and forgetfulness. Recognize that he is applying the same analysis to God.
This shows the integration of logic and theology.
2
Identify the parallel drawn
He compares forgetfulness contradicting knowledge to sleep contradicting life: 'كما أنه تنزيه الرب منزه عن السِّنَة والنوم'. Understand the analogy: as sleep is incompatible with perfect life, forgetfulness is incompatible with perfect knowledge.
Both are negations of perfection.
3
Understand the implication for divine knowledge
God's knowledge is always actual and present; there is no need for 'attention' or 'summoning'. This reinforces Ibn Taymiyya's earlier point that forgetfulness is a human limitation.
Divine knowledge is a model of perfect knowledge.
4
Reflect on the eschatological aspect
He mentions that people of Paradise do not sleep (and by extension do not forget), showing that this perfection extends to the blessed in the afterlife.
This connects logical perfection to spiritual reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا تنازع هؤلاء المنطقيون في العلم بالمقدمتين هل هو كاف في العلم بالنتيجة، أم لا بد من التفطُن لأمر ثالث؟ وهذا الثاني هو قول ابن سينا وغيره. قالوا : بالاحتياج إلى مقدمة ثالثة لأن الإنسان قد يكون عالماً بأن البغلة لا تلد، ثم يغفل عن ذلك ويرى بغلة منتفخة البطن، فيقول: أهذه حامل أم لا؟ فيقال له : أما تعلم أنها بغلة؟ فيقول : بلى، ويقال له : أما تعلم أن البغلة لا تلد؟ فيقول : بلى. قال: فحينئذ يتفطن لكونها لا تلد. ونازعه الرازي وغيره وقالوا هذا ضعيف؛ لأن اندراج إحدى المقدمتين تحت الأخرى إن كان مغايراً للمقدمتين كان ذلك مقدمة أخرى لا بد فيها من الإنتاج، ويكون الكلام في كيفية التيامها مع الأوليين كالكلام في كيفية التيام الأوليين، ويفضي ذلك إلى اعتبار ما لا نهاية له من المقدمات. وإن لم يكن ذلك معلوماً مغايراً للمقدمتين استحال أن يكون شرطاً في الإنتاج، لأن الشرط مغاير للمشروط، وهنا لا مغايرة، فلا يكون شرطاً. وأما حديث البغلة فذلك إنما يمكن إذا كان الحاضِرُ في الذهن إحدى المقدمتين فقط، إما الصغرى وإما الكبرى. وأما عند اجتماعهما في الذهن فلا نسلّم أنه يمكن الشك أصلاً في النتيجة. قلت : وحقيقة الأمر أن هذا النزاع لزمهم في ظَنّهم الحاجة إلى مقدمتين فقط، وليس الأمر كذلك، بل المحتاج إليه هو ما به يُعْلَم المطلوب، سواء كانت مقدمة، أو ثنتين أو ثلاثاً. والمغفول عنه ليس بمعلوم حال الغفلة، فإذا تذكر صار معلوماً بالفعل. وهنا الدليل هو العلم بأن البغلة لا تحبل، وهذه المقدمة كان ذاهلاً عنها، فلم يكن عالماً بها العلم الذي تحصل به الدلالة. فإن المغفول عنه لا يدل حين ما يكون مغفولاً عنه، بل إنما يدل حال كونه مذكوراً، إذ هو بذلك يكون معلوماً علماً حاضراً. والرب تعالى منزّه عن الغفلة والنسيان، لأن ذلك يناقض حقيقة العلم، كما أنه تنزيه الرب منزه عن السِّنَة والنوم، لأن ذلك يناقض كمال الحياة والقيوميّة، فإن النوم أخو الموت. ولهذا كان أهل الجنة لا ينامون كما لا يموتون، وكانوا يُلْهَمُون التسبيحَ كما يُلهم أحدنا النَفَس. والمقصود هنا أن وجه الدليل العلم بلزوم المدلول له سواء سُمّى استحضاراً أو تفطُّناً أو غير ذلك. فمتى استحضر في ذهنه لزوم المدلول له علم أنه دال عليه وهذا اللزوم إن كان بيناً له، وإلا فقد يحتاج في بيانه إلى مقدمة، أو ثنتين، أو ثلاثة، أو أكثر.

He said: Then at that moment he becomes attentive to the fact that it does not give birth. Al-Razi and others disputed him and said: This is weak, because the subsumption of one premise under the other, if it is different from the two premises, then that would be another premise that is necessary for production, and the discussion about how it combines with the first two is similar to the discussion about how the first two combine, and that leads to positing an infinite number of premises. If it is not known to be different from the two premises, it cannot be a condition for production, because a condition is different from the conditioned, and here there is no difference, so it is not a condition. As for the mule story, that is only possible if only one of the two premises is present in the mind, either the minor or the major. As for when both are present together in the mind, we do not concede that doubt in the conclusion is possible at all. I say: The truth of the matter is that this dispute arose from their assumption that only two premises are needed, but it is not so. Rather, what is needed is that by which the sought knowledge is known, whether it is one premise, two, or three. And what is neglected is not known during the state of neglect. When it is remembered, it becomes actually known. Here the proof is the knowledge that the mule does not conceive, and this premise he was heedless of, so he was not knowledgeable of it with the knowledge that yields indication. For what is neglected does not indicate when it is neglected, but only indicates when it is mentioned, because by that it becomes known as present knowledge. And the Lord, exalted, is free from heedlessness and forgetfulness, because that contradicts the reality of knowledge, just as the Lord is free from slumber and sleep, because that contradicts the perfection of life and self-subsistence, for sleep is the brother of death. For this reason, the people of Paradise do not sleep just as they do not die, and they are inspired with glorification as one of us is inspired with breath. And the intended point here is that the aspect of the proof is knowledge of the implication of the thing indicated, whether it is called 'summoning' or 'attention' or something else. So whenever one summons to his mind the implication of the thing indicated, he knows that it indicates it. And this implication, if it is self-evident to him, then it is fine; otherwise, it may need in its clarification one premise, or two, or three, or more.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of a dispute among logicians, particularly Ibn Sina and al-Razi, over whether knowing both premises automatically yields the conclusion. Ibn Sina argues that a third condition—attention (tatabbur)—is needed, citing the example of a person who knows that mules do not give birth but is momentarily heedless and doubts a mule's pregnancy. Al-Razi objects that if the subsumption (indiraj) of the minor under the major is different from the premises, it becomes an infinite regress; if not, it cannot be a condition. Ibn Taymiyya resolves the debate by rejecting the premise that only two premises are needed. He states that what is required is the knowledge that indicates the sought conclusion, whether from one premise or more. Forgetfulness negates actual knowledge; when remembered, the premise becomes active. Thus, the mule example works because the major premise was forgotten. Ibn Taymiyya then relates this to divine omniscience, noting that God is free from forgetfulness. He concludes that the key is the presence of the implication (luzum) in the mind, whether immediate or requiring further premises.

Key Terms
الغفلة
السِّنَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is God free from forgetfulness?
A) Because God does not need to remember.
B) Because forgetfulness contradicts the reality of knowledge.
C) Because God is too powerful to forget.
D) Because forgetfulness is a human trait only.

Key Takeaway
God's perfect knowledge excludes forgetfulness, just as His perfect life excludes sleep.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Qur'an says: 'Your Lord is not forgetful' (19:64). How does Ibn Taymiyya's logical analysis of forgetfulness support this verse? Write a brief explanation connecting the definition of knowledge to the negation of forgetfulness.

Angels Are Not the Philosophers' Intellects
Rejects identification of angels with the philosophers' intellects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of equating angels with Neoplatonic intellects and explain why he considers it a gross falsehood.

Core Idea
Angels are real created beings, not abstract philosophical concepts.

Did you know that some philosophers claimed the angels mentioned in the Qur'an are actually the 'intellects' of Neoplatonic emanation? Ibn Taymiyya fiercely rejects this syncretism. In this lesson, we will dissect his critique and see why he calls it 'the most false of falsehoods.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the philosophical claim that Ibn Taymiyya is attacking. It is the assertion that the angels of revelation are identical to the 'intellects' ('uqul) of Neoplatonic philosophy.
This claim was made by those attempting to harmonize revelation with philosophy, such as al-Farabi and Ibn Sina.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's verdict
Look at the phrase 'min abtal al-batil' (among the most false of falsehood). This is an extremely strong condemnation. What does that indicate about the seriousness of the error?
3
Identify the grounds of the critique
Ibn Taymiyya says the differences between Qur'anic angels and philosophical intellects are obvious, except to those blinded. He does not list them here but points to the Qur'anic descriptions as the source of truth.
This is a revelation-based epistemological objection.
4
Assess the force of the critique
Consider why Ibn Taymiyya does not engage in detailed argument here. He dismisses the equation as self-evidently false to anyone with true insight (basira). This is a common rhetorical move in critiques.
The critique depends on the primacy of revelation over speculative reason.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage rejects the common philosophical claim that the angels of revelation are identical to the intellects of Neoplatonic philosophy. Ibn Taymiyya states that those who try to harmonize revelation and philosophy by equating them are committing the worst falsehood. He insists that the angels described in the Qur'an have attributes that are fundamentally different from the abstract intellects. The differences are so clear that only a person whose spiritual sight is blinded would miss them.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of al-Ghazali's Two-World Division
Criticizes al-Ghazali's division of creation into two worlds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection to al-Ghazali's distinction between 'alam al-khalq and 'alam al-amr and state why he considers it un-Islamic.

Core Idea
Everything other than God is created — no exceptions.

Al-Ghazali, influenced by philosophical traditions, divided existence into a physical world of bodies ('alam al-khalq) and a non-physical world of souls and intellects ('alam al-amr) that comes from God's command without matter. Ibn Taymiyya sharply criticizes this distinction, calling it contrary to Islamic creed. This passage shows how he grounds his critique in the absolute creatorship of God.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Ghazali's doctrine
Al-Ghazali separates existence into two realms: 'alam al-khalq (bodies) and 'alam al-amr (souls and intellects). The latter is from God's command, not matter.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's rejection
Find the phrase 'laysa min din al-Muslimin' — Ibn Taymiyya declares this distinction is not part of the religion of Muslims.
3
Understand the positive counter-claim
Ibn Taymiyya asserts 'kullu ma siwa Allah makhluq' — everything besides God is created. This includes both worlds.
4
Theological implication
The distinction implies something uncreated (the world of command), which contradicts God as Creator of all things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبو حامد يفرق بين عالم الخلق وعالم الأمر، فيجعل الأجسام عالم الخلق، والنفوس والعقول عالم الأمر . وهذا أيضاً ليس من دين المسلمين، بل كلُّ ما سوى الله مخلوق عند المسلمين والله تعالى خالق كل شيء .
And Abu Hamid (al-Ghazali) distinguishes between the world of creation ('alam al-khalq) and the world of command ('alam al-amr), making bodies the world of creation, and souls and intellects the world of command. And this too is not from the religion of the Muslims. Rather, everything other than God is created according to the Muslims, and God Most High is the Creator of everything.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage targets al-Ghazali's philosophical distinction. Ibn Taymiyya notes that al-Ghazali separates existence into the world of creation (bodies) and the world of command (souls and intellects), the latter being directly from God's command without matter. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this as un-Islamic, asserting that everything other than God is created (makhluq) and God is the Creator of all things. This affirms the absolute creatorship of God without any mediating eternal entities.

Key Terms
عالم الخلق
عالم الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is derived from the Qur'an.
C) It is not from the religion of the Muslims.
D) It only applies to angels.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya rejects any division of existence that implies something beyond God's creation, affirming that everything other than God is created.

Using Hadith to Refute Philosophical Claims about Angels
Proves from hadith that angels are created from light, refuting philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the philosophers' identification of their intellects with angels by citing the hadith on the creation of angels from light.

Core Idea
Angels are created from light, so they cannot be eternal intellects.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya confronts the philosophers who claim that the 'intellects' they posit correspond to the angels mentioned in revelation. He counters this by citing an authentic hadith that explicitly states God created the angels from light. This refutes the philosophers' view that the intellects are eternal or originated without creation. We will analyze how he uses textual evidence to uphold the Islamic doctrine of creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical claim being refuted
Locate in the Arabic where Ibn Taymiyya states the philosophers' assertion: 'وإذا ادعو أن «العقول التي اثبتوها هي ) الملائكة»' (And if they claim that the intellects which they affirm are the angels). Note that they also consider these intellects as either eternal (قديمة أزلية) or originated without matter (مبتدعة لا مخلوقة).
The philosophers' claim conflates rational abstractions with revealed beings.
2
Cite the prophetic evidence
Ibn Taymiyya brings a hadith from Sahih Muslim: 'خلق الله الملائكة من نور' (God created the angels from light). This is explicit textual proof that angels have a material origin (light), which is a created substance.
The hadith also mentions jinn from fire and Adam from clay, emphasizing creation from pre-existing matter.
3
Understand the implication
If angels are created from light, they are not eternal (they have a beginning) and are not 'mubtada'a' (originated without creation). The philosophers' claim that the intellects are eternal or without creation directly contradicts this revelation.
Ibn Taymiyya uses the term 'فأين هذا من قول...' (So where is this from the statement of...) to highlight the stark opposition.
4
Evaluate the refutation's strength
The hadith is authentic (sahih) and explicit. The philosophers' claim is based on speculative reasoning, but revelation provides certain knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya's method prioritizes transmitted evidence (naql) over rational speculation when they conflict.
This illustrates a key principle in Islamic theology: revelation corrects reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ادعو أن «العقول التي اثبتوها هي ) الملائكة» في كلام الأنبياء، فقد ثبت بالنص والإجماع أن الله خلق الملائكة، بل خلقهم من مادّة، كما ثبت في صحيح مسلم، عن عائشة، أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : خلق الله الملائكة من نور، وخلق الجان من مارج من نار، وخلق آدم مما وُصف لكم. فبين أن الملائكة مخلوقون من مادة موجودة قبلهم فأين هذا من قول مَنْ ينفي الخلق عنها؟ ويقول : إنها مبتدعة لا مخلوقة أو يقول : إنها قديمة أزلية لم تكن من مادة أصلاً.
And if they claim that 'the intellects which they affirm are the angels' in the speech of the prophets, then it has been established by text and consensus that God created the angels; rather, He created them from matter, as is established in Sahih Muslim, from Aisha, that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'God created the angels from light, and created the jinn from a smokeless flame of fire, and created Adam from what was described to you.' So it is clear that the angels are created from matter that existed before them. So where is this from the statement of one who denies creation from them? And says: They are originated (mubtada'a) not created, or says: They are eternal, pre-eternal, not from matter at all.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that the philosophical intellects are the angels of revelation. Ibn Taymiyya cites a sahih hadith from Sahih Muslim that the angels are created from light (nur). This establishes that they have a material origin—light is a created substance. Thus, the philosophers who claim the intellects are eternal (qadima azaliyya) and not created from matter are contradicted by explicit revelation. The hadith shows that even the angels are contingent beings, created from pre-existing light.

Key Terms
الملائكة
مبتدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses explicit hadith evidence to decisively refute the philosophers' claim that angels are eternal intellects, affirming that they are created beings from light.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Logicians' Language
Critique of logicians' awkward language and al-Kindi's example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of logicians' language and evaluate his argument using his own criteria.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya judges logicians by their own confused speech, not just their theory.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya moves beyond theoretical objections to attack the very language of logicians. He argues that their style is inherently awkward and weak, and that any eloquence they possess is borrowed from Muslims. This is a key move in his overall critique: if the tools of logic produce unclear speech, they cannot be reliable guides to truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya is not critiquing the content of logical arguments here, but the linguistic style of logicians. He uses terms like 'ruka' and 'iyy' to describe their speech.
2
Extract the criteria of critique
Ibn Taymiyya claims that logicians' language narrows the path of knowledge both intellectually and linguistically. Their words and meanings are of the same deficient kind.
3
Examine the al-Kindi example
Al-Kindi, despite being called the philosopher of Islam, uses awkward constructions like 'for the lack of existence of such-and-such'. This illustrates the claimed weakness.
4
Analyze the borrowed eloquence claim
Ibn Taymiyya argues that any eloquence in al-Kindi or Ibn Sina comes from their exposure to Muslim intellect and language, not from their logical training.
5
Assess the strength of the critique
Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya provides sufficient evidence. He gives one example from al-Kindi but does not systematically analyze many logicians. This is more of a rhetorical attack than a logical proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - وجود الركة والعي في كلام أهل المنطق
بأبين العبارات مِنْ استعمال المقدمتين في كلامهم ما يوجَدُ في كلام أهل المنطق. بل من سلك طريقهم كان من المضيقين لطريق العلم عقولاً والسنة، ومعانيهم من جنس ألفاظهم ، تجد فيها من الرّكة والعِيّ ما لا يرضاه عاقل .
وكان يعقوب بن إسحاق الكندي (۱) فيلسوفُ الإسلام في وقته ـ أعنى الفيلسوف العيب على ، من أثبت فلاسفة الذي في الإسلام وإلا فليس الفلاسفة من المسلمين كما قالوا لبعض أعيان القضاة الذين كانوا في زماننا ابن سينا من فلاسفة الإسلام، فقال: ليس للإسلام فلاسفة للإسلام كان يعقوب يقول في أثناء كلامه لعدم فقد وجود كذا»، وأنواع هذه الإضافات .
ومن وجد في بعض كلامه فصاحة أو بلاغة كما يوجد في بعض كلام ابن سينا وغيره فلما استفاده من المسلمين - من عقولهم وألسنتهم وإلا فلو مشى على طريقة سلفه وأعرض عما تعلمه من المسلمين لكان عقله ولسانه يشبه عقولهم وألسنتهم.
The third station - The existence of awkwardness and weakness in the speech of the people of logic. With the clearest expressions from the use of the two premises in their speech is what is found in the speech of the people of logic. Rather, whoever follows their way is among those who narrow the path of knowledge intellectually and linguistically, and their meanings are of the same kind as their words; you find in them such awkwardness and weakness that no rational person would approve. And Ya'qub ibn Ishaq al-Kindi, the philosopher of Islam in his time - meaning the philosopher criticized for [something] - from those who affirmed the philosophers of [Islam]? Actually, the philosophers are not from the Muslims, as they said to some prominent judges of our time: Ibn Sina is among the philosophers of Islam. He said: Islam has no philosophers. Ya'qub used to say during his speech 'for the lack of existence of such-and-such', and varieties of these additions. And whoever finds in some of his speech eloquence or rhetoric, as is found in some of the speech of Ibn Sina and others, it is due to what he acquired from the Muslims - from their intellects and tongues. Otherwise, if he had followed the way of his predecessors and turned away from what he learned from the Muslims, his intellect and tongue would resemble theirs.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a critique. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the third station of his argument, which focuses on the awkwardness (ruka) and weakness ('iyy) in the speech of logicians. He asserts that the clearest expressions of their use of premises are found in their own words, yet following their method narrows the path to knowledge both intellectually and linguistically. Their meanings match their words, and their speech contains such deficiency that no rational person would accept it. He then cites al-Kindi as an example, noting that despite being called the philosopher of Islam, al-Kindi's speech is weak and filled with awkward constructions like 'for the lack of existence of such-and-such'. Ibn Taymiyya explains that any eloquence found in al-Kindi or Ibn Sina is borrowed from Muslims; left to their own tradition, their intellect and language would be equally weak. The point is to show that logicians' language is inherently flawed, undermining their claim to precision.

Key Terms
الركة
العي
فيلسوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because al-Kindi was a naturally gifted orator.
B) Because al-Kindi learned from the Muslims' intellect and tongues.
C) Because al-Kindi followed the Greek philosophical tradition strictly.
D) Because al-Kindi avoided using logical terminology.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya attacks logicians' language as inherently awkward, using al-Kindi as an example, to undermine the claim that logical methods produce clear knowledge.

Exposing the Contradictions in al-Wahid la Yasdur
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the philosophers' claim that the One only produces one, exposing its contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the philosophers' claim that the one only issues one, and identify the contradictions he exposes.

Core Idea
The philosophers' tawhid collapses distinctions, yielding absurdities that rational people reject.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks a cornerstone of Islamic philosophy: the principle that from the One only one can proceed. He shows that the philosophers' notion of divine oneness, stripped of all positive attributes, leads to logical contradictions and is universally rejected by sound reason. This lesson unpacks his critical method and its implications for theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find the statement 'al-wāḥid lā yaṣdur ʿanhu illā wāḥid' in the Arabic. Note that it rests on a specific concept of 'one' as devoid of all positive attributes.
This is not the Islamic tawhid but a philosophical construct.
2
Extract the consequences they draw
List the implications: creation is non-existential, knowledge=power=care=love=pleasure, and all are reduced to negations.
These follow from stripping God of positive attributes.
3
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's objection
Find the sentence starting with 'fa-in qaddara thubūt...'. He argues that even if their concept is granted, rational people know it is false and contradictory.
The objection is based on rational necessity (ḍarūrat al-ʿaql).
4
Evaluate the argument's force
Assess whether Ibn Taymiyya's appeal to 'majority of rational people' is a valid refutation. Consider how he exposes internal inconsistencies without constructing a formal syllogism.
This is a dialectical critique, not a syllogistic proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

مما يتضمنه قولهم الذي يسمونه توحيد واجب الوجود».
ردّ قولهم : «الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد»
فإن قدر ثبوت هذا المعنى الذي يسمونه «توحيداً»، مع أن جماهير العقلاء من جميع الأمم إذا تصوروا ذلك علموا بضرورة العقل أن هذا قول باطل متناقض .
The reality of the philosophers' tawhid. And this is like their statement: The one does not issue from him except one. For if it is established for them that the Lord, exalted, is in the sense of the one that they claim, that is, that He has no positive attribute at all, but rather He is stripped of every positive thing, not described except by pure negation, or by what does not entail except negation, like addition which is in the meaning of negation. And they made His creation of the world a non-existential matter because it is 'addition' according to them. And they made knowledge, the known, the knower, love, the lover, the beloved, and pleasure, all of that non-existential matters having no positive thing in them. And they claimed that knowledge itself, care, and power are the same as the knowing, powerful, willing world, and knowledge itself is power itself, and power itself is care itself, and all that is pleasure, love, and love and pleasure are the loving enjoyer, and love and pleasure are the same as knowledge and power, and His knowledge of Himself is His knowledge of known things. And so on, from what their statement entails, which they call 'the tawhid of the necessary existent'. Refutation of their statement: 'The one does not issue from him except one'. For if the reality of this meaning which they call 'tawhid' is assumed to be established, while the majority of rational people from all nations, when they imagine that, know by necessity of reason that this is a false and contradictory statement.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' (falāsifa) doctrine that 'the one only issues one' (al-wāḥid lā yaṣdur ʿanhu illā wāḥid). Ibn Taymiyya begins by exposing the implications of their concept of divine oneness (tawḥīd al-falāsifa). According to them, God is one in the sense of having no positive attributes whatsoever; He is described only through pure negation or relational addition (iḍāfa) which is also negative in meaning. They make His creation of the world a non-existential (ʿadamī) affair because it is merely an addition. Similarly, they reduce knowledge, the known, the knower, love, the lover, the beloved, and pleasure to non-existential matters. They collapse these concepts into each other: knowledge equals power equals care equals love equals pleasure, and God's knowledge of Himself is His knowledge of all things. Ibn Taymiyya finds this self-defeating. He then delivers his refutation: even if we suppose their meaning of 'tawḥīd' is established, the vast majority of rational people across nations, upon understanding it, would know by rational necessity that such a statement is false and contradictory. The argument is not a formal syllogism but a dialectical exposure of absurd consequences.

Key Terms
توحيد الفلاسفة
الواحد
سلوب
إضافة

C) God's attributes are unknown
D) God has only relational attributes that are positive

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the philosophers' claim by showing that their concept of divine oneness leads to logical contradictions and is rejected by sound reason.

Refutation of the Emanation Principle
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the emanation principle by logical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the principle 'from one only one proceeds' and identify its reliance on a flawed ontology.

Core Idea
If action is a mere negation, multiplicity of effects does not compromise divine unity.

Ibn Taymiyya takes on a cornerstone of Neoplatonic emanationism: the claim that from a simple one, only one thing can proceed. He shows that this principle depends on a specific ontology where all divine attributes and actions are reduced to negations. Once you accept his analysis, the principle collapses under the weight of its own contradictions.

Guided Path
1
State the philosophers' principle
Identify the principle 'al-wahid la yasduru 'anhu illa wahid' (from one only one proceeds). The philosophers use it to argue that God can directly create only one thing, leading to a chain of emanations.
This principle is linked to their concept of tawhid as absolute simplicity without positive attributes.
2
Expose the ontology of negation
The philosophers claim God has no positive attributes; all descriptions are negations or relations (which they treat as non-existent). They say creation (ibda') is a non-existent relation.
This is key: they redefine a positive act as a mere negation.
3
Apply the logic of negations to multiplicity
Ibn Taymiyya argues: if creation is a mere negation, then even if a thousand things are created, it is like saying 'God is not this, not that' – many negations do not multiply the essence.
Thus the principle that one cannot produce many is false on their own terms.
4
Find the contradiction in their categories
The philosophers themselves divide existence into substance and accident, and place 'to act' (an yaf'ala) under accident, which is a positive category. Yet they claim creation is non-existent. This is a direct contradiction.
Ibn Taymiyya uses their own ontological framework against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقيقة توحيد الفلاسفة

وهذا كقولهم : الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد فإنه إن ثبت لهم أن الرب تعالى بمعنى الواحد الذي يدّعونه، وهو أنه ليس له صفة ثبوتية أصلاً، بل هو مسلوب لكل أمر ثبوتي، لا يوصف إلا بالسَّلْب المحض، أو بما لا يتضمن إلا السلب، كالإضافة التي هي في معنى السلب وجعلوا إبداعه للعالم أمراً عدمياً لكونه إضافة عندهم. وجعلوا العلم والعالم والمعلوم، والعشق، والعاشق والمعشوق، واللذة، كل ذلك أموراً عدمية ليس فيها أمر ثبوتي. وادعوا أن نفس العلم، والعناية، والقدرة، هو نفس العالم القادر المريد ونفس العلم هو نفس القدرة، ونفس القدرة هو نفس العناية، وهذا كله هو اللذة. والعشق هو اللذة، والعشق واللذة هو العاشق الملتذ والعشق واللذة هو نفس العلم ونفس القدرة وعلمه بنفسه هو علمه بالمعلومات. إلى أمثال ذلك مما يتضمنه قولهم الذي يسمونه توحيد واجب الوجود.

ردّ قولهم : «الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد»

فإن قدر ثبوت هذا المعنى الذي يسمونه «توحيداً»، مع أن جماهير العقلاء من جميع الأمم إذا تصوروا ذلك علموا بضرورة العقل أن هذا قول باطل متناقض . فإن قدر ثبوته قيل حينئذ : الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد يقررونه بقولهم : لأنه لو صدر عنه اثنان لكان مصدرُ (الألف) غير مصدر (الجيم)، وكان المصدر هذا الصادر مخالفاً لهذا المصدر مع هذا الصادر، فيكون في المصدر جهتان، وذلك ينافي الوحدة. وبهذا أثبتوا أن «الواحد لا يكون فاعلاً» و «قابلاً» ، لئلا يكون فيه جهتان، جهة فعل، وجهة قبول، فيكون مركباً. فيقال لهم : إذا كان صدور الصادرات عنه هو فعله لها، و الفعل إضافة محضة لا توجب كثرة إلى أمر ثبوتية، وهو عندكم لا يوصف بصفة ثبوتية، بل لا يوصف إلا بما هو سَلْب، وقلتم إن الإضافة هنا سَلْب، لم يكن ولو صدر عنه ألف صادر إلا بمنزلة سلب الأشياء عنه. وإذا قلتم ليس هو بعَرَض، ولا ممكن، ولا محدث، ونحو ذلك، لم تكن كثرة السلوب توجب أمراً ثبوتياً. والإبداع عندكم لا يوجب له وصفاً ثبوتياً، فكثرة الإبداعات منه لا توجب له وصفاً ثبوتياً. هذا مع أنهم متناقضون في جعلهم الإبداع أمراً عدمياً بل وفي قولهم هو إضافة أمراً علمياً. قد قرروا في العلم الأعلى عندهم القاسم للوجود أن الوجود ينقسم إلى جوهر وعرض، ومن الأعراض أن يفعل، ومنها الإضافة. و الإبداع هو من مقولة «أن يفعل»، وهو أمر وجودي. وإبداع الباري أكمل من كل إبداع، فكيف يكون أكمل أنواع «أن يفعل» عدمياً؟ ثم هم جعلوا الإضافة جنساً غير أن يفعل، فإن ثبت هذا بَطَل جعل إبداعه للعالم مجرد إضافة. وإن سُلّم أنه إضافة فـ الإضافة عندهم من جملة الأجناس الوجودية. وهذا وأمثاله مما يبيّن فساد ما قالوه في الإلهيات من التعطيل مما يطول وصفه. ثم إذا سُلِّم هذا، وسُلِّم أن الإضافة عدمية، فكثرة العدميات له لا توجب تكثر المبدعات إذ أمور ثبوتية فيه مثل تكثر سائر السلوب. فإذا قدر أنه أبدع كل شيء بلا واسطة، لم يكن كثرة في ذاته في هذا إلا كثرة أمور عدمية يتصف بها، وتلك لا توجب كثرة في ذاته مثل سلب جميع صفات المبدعات عنه. فإذا قيل : ليس بفلك، ولا كوكب ولا شمس ولا جنة، ولا نار، ولا ...
The reality of the philosophers' monotheism: And this is like their saying: from one only one proceeds. For if they establish that the Lord, exalted, is according to the meaning of the One that they claim — that He has no positive attribute at all, but is stripped of every positive thing, described only by pure negation, or by what implies only negation, like relation which is in the meaning of negation — and they made His creation of the world a non-existent thing because it is, according to them, a relation. And they made knowledge, the known object, the lover and the beloved, pleasure, all of them non-existent things with no positive aspect. And they claimed that knowledge itself, care, and power are the knowing, powerful, willing essence itself, and knowledge is power, and power is care, and all this is pleasure. And love is pleasure, and love and pleasure are the lover enjoying, and love and pleasure are knowledge and power, and His knowledge of Himself is His knowledge of known things. And so on, including their statement they call 'the monotheism of the Necessary Existent'. Response to their saying: 'From one only one proceeds.' If it is assumed that this meaning they call 'monotheism' is established — while the masses of rational people from all nations, if they conceive it, know by necessity of reason that it is a false and contradictory statement — then if its establishment is assumed, one says then: from one only one proceeds, they establish it by their saying: because if two proceeded from him, then the source of (A) would be other than the source of (B), and the source would be different from this source with this source, so there would be two aspects in the source, which contradicts unity. And by this they proved that 'the one cannot be an agent' and 'receiver', lest there be in him two aspects — an aspect of action and an aspect of reception — so he would be composite. So it is said to them: if the emanation of effect from Him is His action upon them, and action is a pure relation that does not necessitate multiplicity in a positive thing — and He according to you is not described by a positive attribute, but only by negation, and you said that relation here is negation — then even if a thousand things emanated from Him, it would be only like the negation of things from Him. And if you say He is not a substance, nor possible, nor temporally originated, and so on, then the multiplicity of negations does not necessitate a positive thing. And creation according to you does not necessitate a positive attribute for Him, so the multiplicity of creations from Him does not necessitate a positive attribute for Him. This is despite their contradiction in making creation a non-existent thing, and in their saying it is a relation, a negative thing. They have established in their highest science, the divider of existence, that existence is divided into substance and accident, and among accidents is 'to act', and among them is relation. And creation is from the category of 'to act', and it is a positive thing. And the creation of the Creator is more perfect than every creation, so how can the most perfect type of 'to act' be non-existent? Then they made relation a genus other than 'to act', and if this is established, their making His creation of the world a mere relation becomes invalid. And if it is conceded that it is a relation, then relation according to them is among the positive genera. This and its like shows the corruption of what they said in theology by way of negation (ta'til), the description of which would be lengthy. Then if this is conceded, and it is conceded that relation is non-existent, then the multiplicity of non-existents for Him does not necessitate multiplicity of creations because there are positive things in Him like the multiplicity of other negations. So if it is supposed that He created everything without mediation, there would be no multiplicity in His essence in this except a multiplicity of non-existent attributes, and these do not necessitate multiplicity in His essence, like the negation of all attributes of created things from Him. So if it is said: He is not a sphere, nor a planet, nor the sun, nor paradise, nor fire, nor...
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' (falasifa) principle 'from one only one proceeds' (الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد), which they derive from their concept of monotheism (توحيد). Ibn Taymiyya begins by summarizing the philosophers' position: they define God as a pure one with no positive attributes, only negations and relations. They consider creation (إبداع) a non-existent relation and reduce all attributes (knowledge, love, power) to non-existents. Then, they argue that if multiple things emanated from this one, there would be multiple aspects in the source, violating its unity. Ibn Taymiyya responds by pointing out a contradiction: if action and creation are mere negations or relations (which they consider non-existent), then the emanation of many things does not imply any multiplicity in the divine essence — it is like negating many things from Him. He then shows that their own ontology accuses creation as a category of 'to act' (أن يفعل), which is a positive accident, so their claim that creation is non-existent is self-contradictory. Moreover, even if relation is non-existent, multiple negations do not multiply the essence. Thus, the principle 'from one only one proceeds' is baseless. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that this reveals the corruption of their theology.

إضافة
إبداع
سلب
توحيد

B) They deny God's existence but affirm creation.
C) They say creation is from nothing but also use matter.
D) They affirm divine attributes but deny creation.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that the emanation principle is not a necessary truth but an artifact of a particular ontological framework that collapses under scrutiny.
