Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Philosophical Emanation as Shirk
Ibn Taymiyya quotes Quran and accuses philosophers of inventing sons and daughters for God through their emanationist doctrines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the philosophers' emanation doctrine is a form of shirk worse than Arab polytheism, and apply his method of scriptural comparison to evaluate other doctrines.

Core Idea
Philosophers replace God's exclusive creation with a male-female emanation, making the Intellect a creator.

This passage exemplifies Ibn Taymiyya's method of using Quranic revelation to critique philosophical theology. He directly applies the Quranic condemnation of those who ascribe children to God (Q 6:100-101) to the falasifa who posit the Intellect and Soul as emanated intermediaries. By comparing their doctrine to the polytheism of pre-Islamic Arabs, he demonstrates that the philosophers' shirk is even more severe because they attribute creative power to these emanated beings and deny God's exclusive role as Creator and Originator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic foundation
Read the verse Q 6:100-101 that Ibn Taymiyya quotes. What sin does it condemn? It condemns those who invent sons and daughters for God without knowledge.
This verse sets the standard: any attribution of offspring to God is baseless shirk.
2
Extract the philosophers' claim
Ibn Taymiyya states that philosophers claim the Intellects and Souls 'emanated' (sadir) from God. They explicitly describe the Intellect as male and the Soul as female, implying a generative process.
The terms 'aql (intellect) and nafs (soul) are key. The philosopher's emanation is likened to reproduction.
3
Compare the two forms of shirk
The polytheistic Arabs called angels daughters of God but did not claim those daughters created anything. In contrast, philosophers claim the First Intellect (one of the 'sons') is the originator (mubdi') of everything else besides God.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes the difference in scale: attributing creative power to a creature is more grievous.
4
Identify the additional error
Ibn Taymiyya notes that the polytheistic Arabs at least acknowledged that God created the heavens and earth ex nihilo (after they were not). The philosophers, however, assert the pre-eternity (qidam) of the universe, denying creation ex nihilo.
This compounds the shirk: not only do they attribute creation to an intellect, they also deny God's creatorship of the world.
5
Assess the critique's method
Ibn Taymiyya uses a comparative analysis between the two forms of shirk, measuring them against the Quranic criterion. He does not reject all logic but shows how philosophical premises contradict revelation.
This is a 'critical refinement': he refines the understanding of shirk to include philosophical emanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الفلاسفة خرقوا الله بنين وبنات ﴿ وَخَلَقَهُمْ وَخَرَقُوا لَهُمُ بَنِينَ وَبَنَاتِ بِغَيْرِ عِلْمٍ سُبْحَنَهُ وَتَعَالَى عَمَّا يَصِفُونَ ﴾ [سورة /٢١٩ الأنعام: 100 - 101]. والذين قالوا: إن العقول والنفوس صدرت عنه خَرَقُوا له بنين وبنات بغير علم الفلاسفة لكن أقوالهم أعظم فساداً في العقل وكفراً في الملل من قول أولئك العرب الذين خرقوا خرقوا الله له بنين وبنات بغير علم فإن أولئك لم يكونوا يجعلون شيئاً من البنين والبنات مبدعة لكل ما سواه، وهؤلاء يجعلون أحد البنين، وهو «العقل»، أبدع كل ما سواه، ويجعلون «العقل كالذكر، والنفس كالأنثى». وهذا مما صرَّحوا به. وكانت العرب تقرُّ بأنه خلق السموات والأرض وأحدثهما بعد أن لم تكونا، ولم يكونوا يقولون إنها قديمة أزلية لم تزل معه. وهذا مبسوط في موضع آخر.
The Third Station - The philosophers invented for God sons and daughters. "And they invented for Him sons and daughters without knowledge. Exalted is He and high above what they describe" [Surah al-An'am 6:100-101]. And those who said that the intellects and souls emanated from Him invented for Him sons and daughters without knowledge - the philosophers - but their statements are more corrupt in reason and more blasphemous in religion than the statement of those Arabs who invented for God sons and daughters without knowledge. For those (Arabs) did not make any of the sons and daughters an originator of everything other than Him, while these (philosophers) make one of the sons, namely the 'Intellect', the originator of everything other than Him, and they make the 'Intellect like the male and the soul like the female.' And this is something they explicitly stated. And the Arabs acknowledged that He created the heavens and the earth and brought them into existence after they were not, and they did not say that they are eternal, pre-eternal, always existing with Him. This is elaborated elsewhere.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' emanationist cosmology. Ibn Taymiyya begins by quoting the Quranic verse (6:100-101) which condemns those who ascribe children to God. He then applies this to the philosophers who claim that the intellects and souls proceed from God. He argues that the philosophers' doctrine is even worse than the polytheistic Arabs who called angels daughters of God. The polytheists did not claim that those 'daughters' created everything else, whereas the philosophers claim that the First Intellect (al-'aql) is the creator of all that exists besides God, and they explicitly describe the intellect as male and the soul as female in their emanative processes. Moreover, the polytheistic Arabs at least acknowledged that God created the heavens and earth ex nihilo, while the philosophers assert the pre-eternity of the universe. Ibn Taymiyya thus exposes the philosophical departure from Islamic monotheism (tawhid).

Key Terms
العقول والنفوس
مبدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the philosophers affirmed that God created everything directly.
D) Because the polytheists accepted that the universe is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique exposes how philosophical emanationism entails a hidden polytheism by assigning divine creative acts to created intellects, which is more severe than the explicit shirk of the Arabs.

Write a short paragraph explaining how Ibn Taymiyya's critique of emanation could be applied to the modern scientific claim that the universe came into existence through natural laws alone, without a creator. Identify the key Quranic principles involved.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Free Agent vs Natural Agent
Ibn Taymiyya argues that God, acting with knowledge and will, is more appropriately compared to human agents than to inanimate natural forces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that God is more appropriately compared to free agents (fā‘il bi al-ikhtiyār) than to natural agents (fā‘il bi aṭ-ṭab‘).

Core Idea
The philosophers' tanzih leads to a worse tashbih by likening God to inanimate nature.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the philosophical principle that God, being one, can only produce one effect (al-wahid la yasduru illa wahid). He exposes a flaw in their analogy: they compare God to natural agents like fire, which act uniformly without knowledge or will, whereas human free agents produce diverse effects. This lesson will dissect his argument and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agents
Extract from the Arabic the characteristics of the natural agent (fā‘il bi aṭ-ṭab‘) and the free agent (fā‘il bi al-ikhtiyār). Note that natural agents act without knowledge or will, while free agents act with knowledge and choice.
2
Observe the diversity of effects
Notice that the free agent (human) produces diverse actions, while the natural agent produces uniform effects. The philosophers themselves accept this distinction.
3
Recognize the perfection hierarchy
Understand that acting with knowledge and will is more perfect. Therefore, God, being the most perfect, should be compared to the free agent, not the natural agent.
4
Uncover the inconsistency
The philosophers compare God to inanimate objects (natural agents) while avoiding comparing Him to living beings, which is actually a worse comparison (tashbih). Ibn Taymiyya calls this 'khidhlān' – divine abandonment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً، فهذا الحار الذي يفعل حرارة والبارد الذي يفعل برودة إنما يفعل ذلك بغير علم وإرادة، بخلاف ما يفعل بعلم واختيار كالإنسان، فإن هذا يفعل أفعالاً متنوعة وتصدر عنه أمور مختلفة. وهم يسلّمون ذلك ويقولون: «إن الفاعل بالطبع يتحد فعله والفاعلُ بالاختيار يتنوع فعله. وإذا كان كذلك فمعلوم أن الفاعل بالعلم والإرادة أكمل مما يفعل بلا علم ولا إرادة؛ فالإنسان أكمل من الجماد. وحينئذ، فإن كان باب «القياس» صحيحاً فقياس الرب بما يفعل بعلم واختيار أولى من قياسه بما يفعل بلا علم ولا اختيار فما بالهم شبَّهوا ربَّ العالمين بالجمادات، ونزّهوه أن يشبهوه بالأحياء الناطقين! وهذا الخذلان أصابهم في باب صفاته وأفعاله.
Also, this hot thing that produces heat and the cold thing that produces cold only do so without knowledge and will, unlike that which acts with knowledge and choice like the human being, for this one performs diverse actions and various things proceed from him. And they (philosophers) affirm that and say: 'The natural agent has a uniform action, and the free agent has diverse action.' If that is so, then it is known that the agent with knowledge and will is more perfect than that which acts without knowledge and will; so the human being is more perfect than the inanimate object. Then, if the door of 'syllogism' (qiyas) is sound, then comparing the Lord to that which acts with knowledge and choice is more appropriate than comparing Him to that which acts without knowledge and choice. So what is wrong with them that they likened the Lord of the worlds to inanimate objects, and deemed it farfetched to liken Him to living rational beings? And this abandonment (khidhlān) afflicted them in the matter of His attributes and actions.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage contains a powerful critique of the philosophers' method of reasoning about God. Ibn Taymiyya begins by giving examples: a hot object causes heat naturally, without knowledge or will; a cold object causes cold similarly. In contrast, a human being acts with knowledge and choice, producing diverse effects. The philosophers themselves accept the distinction: the natural agent produces uniform effects, while the free agent produces diverse effects. Ibn Taymiyya then argues that since acting with knowledge and will is more perfect than acting without, God, being the most perfect, should be compared to the free agent, not the natural agent. Yet the philosophers compare God to inanimate objects (like fire) when they apply the principle 'al-wahid la yasduru illa wahid' (which assumes uniform effect). They consider it tanzih (transcendence) to avoid likening God to living beings, but Ibn Taymiyya says this actually results in a worse form of tashbih (anthropomorphism) by likening Him to lifeless matter. The term 'khidhlān' (abandonment) indicates that God has left them to error due to their misguided reasoning.

Key Terms
فاعل بالطبع
فاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because natural agents produce uniform effects, and God produces diverse effects.
B) Because humans are more perfect than inanimate objects, and God is the most perfect.
C) Because philosophers themselves accept that free agents are more perfect.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes that the philosophers' analogy comparing God to natural agents is less perfect than comparing Him to free agents, undermining their own principle of tanzih.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of Denying Divine Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya argues that denying God's attributes results in likening Him to inanimate objects, which is worse than affirming attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that denying God's attributes leads to a worse form of tashbih than affirming them, and identify the internal inconsistency in the philosophers' position.

Core Idea
Denying God's attributes likens Him to dust and water, which is worse than the tashbih you flee from.

The philosophers claim that affirming God's attributes of life, knowledge, and power likens Him to celestial or human souls. Ibn Taymiyya turns their own weapon against them: if that is tashbih, then denying those attributes likens God to inanimate matter like dust and water—a far more degrading comparison. He then exposes a deeper inconsistency using the philosophers' own ideal of tashabbuh (assimilation to God). This lesson sharpens your ability to detect self-refuting arguments in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
State the philosophers' objection
Identify the claim that affirming attributes like 'living, knowing, powerful' amounts to likening God to celestial or human souls.
This is the philosophers' charge of tashbih.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's counter-objection
Note that he replies by saying that negating those same attributes likens God to inanimate objects like dust and water, which is a worse likeness.
This turns the tables: the philosophers' own fear of tashbih leads them to a more degrading comparison.
3
Trace the internal inconsistency using tashabbuh
The philosophers claim that philosophy is tashabbuh bi al-ilah (becoming like God) and that the celestial sphere does so. If creatures can resemble God, then God must have attributes that can be resembled. But they deny attributes, so either tashabbuh is impossible or God has attributes.
Ibn Taymiyya uses their own principle to derive a contradiction.
4
Assess the conclusion about 'al-wahid la yasduru illa wahid'
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the principle 'the one does not issue from it except one' cannot be used as a valid argument about God, regardless of the syllogistic form.
This undermines a key Neoplatonic axiom used to deny divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهم في باب الصفات يقولون: شبهوا الرب بالجمادات إذا قلنا إنه حي عالم قادر، فقد شبَّهناه بـ «النفس الفلكية أو الإنسانية». فيقال لهم: إذا نفيتم عنه العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة، فقد شبَّهتموه بالجمادات، كالتراب والماء. فإن كنتم إنما هربتم من التشبيه فالذي هربتم إليه شرٌّ مما هربتم منه. ثم إنكم تزعمون أن الفلسفة التشبه بالإله على قدر الطاقة، وأن الفلك يتشبه به بحسب الإمكان فتجعلون مخلوقاته قادرة على التشبه به من بعض الوجوه. فإن كان بالصفات من مخلوقاته التشبه به منتفياً عنه من كل وجه امتنع أن يكون مقدوراً للمخلوقات. وإن جاز أو وجب إثباته من بعض الوجوه كان هو أقدر عليه من مخلوقاته. فكان - إذا كان التشبه من بعض الوجوه ممكناً - أن يخلق ما فيه من صفات الكمال ما يشبهه من بعض الوجوه أَوْلى من أن يقدر ذلك المخلوقُ على أن يُحدث لنفسه ما به مشابهاً له يصير به من بعض الوجوه، سواءً قيل إنه خالق أفعال المخلوقات أو لم يُقَل بذلك. فإنه على الأول يكون هو الخالق لما فيه شَبَه له وحينئذ فيبطل قولهم. وعلى الثاني فيكون المخلوق بدون إعانة الخالق له يقدر على أن يُحدِث ما يشبه به الربَّ والربّ لا يقدر على ذلك من بعض الوجوه؟ فتبين أن قولهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد لا يصح استدلالهم به في حق الله تعالى، بأي قياس استدلوا.
So in the matter of attributes they say: 'You liken the Lord to inanimate objects when we say that He is living, knowing, powerful, for we have likened Him to the 'celestial or human soul'.' So it is said to them: If you negate from Him knowledge, life, power, and will, then you have likened Him to inanimate objects, like dust and water. For if you are fleeing from tashbih (likening), what you flee to is worse than what you flee from. Moreover, you claim that philosophy is to resemble the God as much as possible, and that the celestial sphere resembles Him to the extent possible, so you make His creatures capable of resembling Him in some respects. If resemblance through attributes of His creatures were completely denied of Him in every respect, it would be impossible for it to be within the capacity of creatures. And if it is permissible or obligatory to affirm it in some respects, then He is more capable of it than His creatures. So it would be, if resemblance in some respects is possible, that He creates in Himself perfect attributes by which He resembles Himself in some respects is more appropriate than that creature be able to produce in itself something by which it resembles Him, becoming like Him in some respects, whether one says that He is the creator of the acts of creatures or not. For in the first case, He is the creator of that in which there is resemblance to Him, and then their statement is invalid. And in the second case, the creature without the Creator's assistance would be able to produce that by which it resembles the Lord, while the Lord is not capable of that in some respects? So it is made clear that their statement 'the one does not issue from it except one' is not valid as their argument in respect to God the Most High, by whatever syllogism they argued.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated dialectical refutation of the philosophers' position on divine attributes. Ibn Taymiyya first presents the philosophers' objection: if we affirm that God is living, knowing, powerful, they claim we are likening Him to celestial or human souls (which they consider less than divine). Ibn Taymiyya counters that negating these attributes results in likening God to inanimate matter (dust, water), which is a more debasing comparison. He then invokes the philosophers' own doctrine of 'tashabbuh bi al-ilah' (assimilation to God): they claim that the goal of philosophy is to become like God as much as possible, and that the celestial sphere does so. Ibn Taymiyya points out an inconsistency: if resemblance to God is possible for creatures, then it must be even more possible for God Himself to possess those attributes. If creatures can acquire some attributes of perfection, God is more deserving of them. The philosophers' denial of attributes implies that creatures can achieve something God cannot, which is irrational. Ultimately, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the principle 'al-wahid la yasduru illa wahid' cannot be used as an argument concerning God, regardless of the method of reasoning employed.

Key Terms
التشبه بالإله
النفس الفلكية أو الإنسانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Denying God's attributes, likening Him to inanimate objects
C) Affirming the existence of celestial souls
D) Denying the eternity of the universe

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that denying God's attributes is self-refuting: it contradicts the philosophers' own principle of tashabbuh and results in a more degrading tashbih than the one they sought to avoid.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Negating Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya argues that negating divine attributes leads to a worse form of anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that negating divine attributes leads to a worse anthropomorphism and identify the logical inconsistency in the philosophers' position.

Core Idea
Negating God's attributes is a worse anthropomorphism than affirming them.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the philosophers' attempt to avoid anthropomorphism by denying God's attributes. He argues that this denial actually likens God to inanimate objects, which is a more severe form of tashbih. Furthermore, he exposes a contradiction in their view that creatures can 'imitate' God while negating attributes from Him entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' position
The philosophers claim to flee from tashbih (likening God to creatures) by negating attributes like life, knowledge, power, and will from God. This is their starting point.
Remember that in Kalam, affirming attributes is seen as necessary for tawhid.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counterargument
Ibn Taymiyya retorts that by negating these attributes, they have likened God to inanimate objects (e.g., dust and water), which is worse than the tashbih they fled from.
He uses a 'a fortiori' argument: if fleeing from one tashbih leads to a worse one, the flight is self-defeating.
3
Analyze the tashabbuh (imitation) inconsistency
The philosophers claim that philosophy is imitation of God to the extent possible. If creatures can imitate God in some respects, then God must possess those attributes more perfectly; otherwise, imitation would be impossible.
This highlights a logical contradiction: they cannot both negate attributes and claim creatures imitate them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا قلنا إنه حيّ، عالم قادر، فقد شبَّهناه بـ «النفس الفلكية أو الإنسانية». فيقال لهم : إذا نفيتم عنه العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة، فقد شبّهتموه بالجمادات، كالتراب والماء. فإن كنتم إنما هربتم من التشبيه فالذي هربتم إليه شرٌّ مما هربتم منه . ثم إنكم تزعمون أن الفلسفة التشبه بالإله على قدر الطاقة، وأن الفلك يتشبه به بحسب الإمكان فتجعلون مخلوقاته قادرة على التشبه به من بعض الوجوه. فإن كان بالصفات من مخلوقاته التشبه به منتفياً عنه من كل وجه امتنع أن يكون مقدوراً للمخلوقات. وإن جاز أو وجب إثباته من بعض الوجوه كان هو أقدر عليه من مخلوقاته . فكان ـ إذا كان التشبه من بعض الوجوه ممكناً ـ أن يخلق ما فيه من صفات الكمال ما يشبهه من بعض الوجوه أَوْلى من أن يقدر ذلك المخلوقُ على أن يُحدِث لنفسه ما به مشابهاً له يصير به الوجوه، سواءً قيل إنه خالق أفعال المخلوقات أو لم يُقَل بذلك. فإنه على الأول يكون هو الخالق لما فيه شَبَه ،له وحينئذ فيبطل قولهم. وعلى الثاني فيكون المخلوق بدون إعانة الخالق له يقدر على أن يُحدِث ما يشبه به الربَّ والربّ لا يقدر على ذلك
If we say that He is living, knowing, powerful, then we have likened Him to the 'psychic or human soul'. It is said to them: When you negate from Him knowledge, life, power, and will, you have likened Him to inanimate objects, like dust and water. If you have fled from anthropomorphism, then what you have fled to is worse than what you fled from. Then you claim that philosophy is the imitation of God to the extent of capacity, and that the celestial sphere imitates Him as much as possible, so you make His creatures capable of imitating Him in some respects. If the imitation of Him by His creatures in attributes is negated from Him entirely, it is impossible that it be within the capacity of creatures. And if its affirmation in some respects is permissible or obligatory, then He is more capable of it than His creatures. So—if imitation in some respects is possible—it is more fitting that He create in Himself the perfect attributes that resemble Him in some respects than that the creature be able to produce for itself that by which it resembles Him, regardless of whether it is said that He is the creator of the actions of creatures or not. For on the first view, He is the creator of that in it which resembles Him, and then their statement is invalidated. On the second view, the creature without the aid of the Creator can produce that by which it resembles the Lord, while the Lord cannot do that.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the philosophers' attempt to avoid anthropomorphism (tashbih) by negating God's attributes. Ibn Taymiyya argues that by denying God's knowledge, life, power, and will, they liken Him to inanimate objects like dust and water, which is a worse form of anthropomorphism than the one they fled. He then examines their claim that philosophy is 'imitation of God' (tashabbuh) to the extent possible. If creatures can imitate God in some respects, then God must have those attributes in a more perfect manner; otherwise imitation would be impossible. This inconsistency shows that the philosophers' position is self-defeating.

Key Terms
التشبيه
التشبه
الصفات

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Emanation Principle
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the philosophers' emanation principle and shows it leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's conditional argument against the emanation principle and identify its internal logic.

Core Idea
The philosophers' principle 'from one only one proceeds' is contradicted by the obvious multiplicity of creation.

Ibn Taymiyya takes on a core Neoplatonic dogma: that from a simple First Principle only one thing can directly emanate. He argues that this principle, if applied to God, actually proves the opposite of what the philosophers intend—it forces us to affirm multiple attributes in God to account for the many creatures. In this lesson, we dissect his critique and see how he turns their own premise against them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target principle
Locate the philosophers' claim that 'from the one only one proceeds'. Ibn Taymiyya states it explicitly and then attacks it.
This is a Neoplatonic axiom the philosophers apply to God.
2
Extract the conditional reasoning
Note the 'if... then' structure: If from one aspect only one proceeds, then multiple creatures cannot proceed from a single aspect. The Lord has created many creatures, so either the principle is false or God has multiple aspects.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to force the philosophers into a dilemma.
3
Identify the contradiction with reality
The observable multiplicity of creation directly contradicts the principle. Ibn Taymiyya states 'the Lord has produced many creatures' as a fact.
This is an empirical refutation grounded in observable reality.
4
Turn the defense against the philosophers
The philosophers might say emanation is from multiple aspects. Ibn Taymiyya shows this admits diversity in God, which contradicts their denial of attributes.
He uses their own premise to prove the necessity of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتبين أن قولهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد لا يصح استدلالهم به في حق الله تعالى، بأي قياس استدلوا . وإن قالوا : إن الواحد من الوجه الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد، وإذا صدر عنه اثنان فمن وجهين ، أو قالوا : هذا معلوم بالضرورة فلا يحتاج إلى دليل، فإنه لا بد بين من توحيدهم المصدر والصادر من مناسبة، والوجه الواحد لا يناسب اثنين قيل لهم : هذا يبطل قولكم في نفي الصفات . فإن الرب قد صدر عنه مخلوقات كثيرة ، وإذا كان الواحد لا يصدر عنه من الوجه الواحد إلا واحد امتنع أن تصدر هذه المخلوقات عن خالقها من وجه واحد . فدل ذلك على أنه متصف بأمور متنوعة من صفات متنوعة وأفعال متنوعة صدر عنه باعتبارها ما وجد من المخلوقات. فكان أصل ضلالهم توهمهم إمكان صدور المخلوقات عما قدروه من «الواحد» الذي لا يوجد إلا في الأذهان، لا في الأعيان ولقد أحسن بعضُ الفضلاء إذ قال : «الصَّقْعُ أحسن من توحيد الفلاسفة» (١) ، بل قصر فيما قال . (١) في الأصل الصمع» من غير نقط ، ولعله الصقع» بالقاف من قولهم: «صقع الحمارُ بضرطة» أي : أخرج ريحاً مصوّتاً من دبره في رطوبة وانتشار - معجم لين المستشرق» (E. W. Lane)
Thus it becomes clear that their statement 'from the One only one proceeds' is not valid when used as evidence concerning God, by whatever syllogism they reason. And if they say: 'From the one from one aspect only one proceeds, and if two proceed from it then from two aspects', or they say: 'This is known by necessity and needs no proof, for there must be a suitability between the source and the emanated, and one aspect is not suitable for two' — it is said to them: This invalidates your statement negating attributes. For the Lord has produced many creatures, and if from the one from one aspect only one proceeds, it is impossible that these creatures proceed from their Creator from one aspect. So that indicates that He is described with diverse things from diverse attributes and diverse actions, by virtue of which the creatures that exist proceed from Him. The root of their error was their delusion that it is possible for creatures to proceed from that 'one' which they conceive, which exists only in minds, not in external reality. And one of the virtuous people did well when he said: 'A coarse sound (al-saq') is better than the tawhid of the philosophers.' But he fell short in what he said. (1) In the original: 'al-sama' (?) without dots; likely 'al-saq'' with qaf, from their saying: 'the donkey let out a saq' (i.e., a noisy fart)' meaning: he emitted a resonant sound from his anus with moisture and spread — Lane's Lexicon.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' principle that 'from the One only one proceeds' (a Neoplatonic emanation doctrine). Ibn Taymiyya argues that this principle, when applied to God, contradicts observable reality: the multiplicity of creatures. He then engages with a possible objection: the philosophers might say that the emanation is from different aspects. Ibn Taymiyya responds that this actually proves that God must have multiple attributes, thus contradicting their own denial of attributes. He concludes that the philosophers' 'one' is a mental fiction, not a real entity. The crude statement 'a coarse sound is better than the philosophers' tawhid' is quoted to emphasize the absurdity of their concept. The footnote explains the meaning of 'saq'. Ibn Taymiyya further notes that even this vulgar comparison falls short of adequately criticizing their error.

Key Terms
الواحد
توحيد الفلاسفة
الصقع

Key Takeaway
The emanation principle fails because it cannot account for the obvious plurality of creation without forcing the admission of divine attributes.

The Philosophers’ ‘One’ as a Mental Fiction
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the philosophers' emanation principle and shows it leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers the philosophers' concept of divine unity to be a mental fiction and contrast it with Islamic tawhid.

Core Idea
The philosophers' First Principle is not God but a mental abstraction that cannot account for real multiplicity.

Ibn Taymiyya identifies the root of the philosophers' error: they mistake a mental construct for a real entity. Their 'One' exists only in their minds, not in external reality. This lesson explores that epistemological critique and its implications for the concept of God. We will see how distinguishing between mental existence and external reality is crucial for sound theology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the claim of mental existence
Ibn Taymiyya states that the philosophers' 'One' exists only in minds (al-adhhan), not in external reality (al-a'yan). Find this in the text.
This is a key epistemological distinction.
2
Understand the distinction
In Islamic thought, something can exist in the mind (as a concept) or externally (as a real object). The philosophers confuse the two.
Their principle is based on an abstract concept, not on the actual nature of God.
3
See how this undermines the principle
If their 'One' is only mental, it has no causal power to produce a real world. The multiplicity of real creatures cannot proceed from a mental fiction.
This explains why their emanation scheme collapses.
4
Appreciate the rhetorical force of the quote
The vulgar comparison (al-saq') is crude but underscores the absurdity of their concept. Ibn Taymiyya notes it still falls short.
Ibn Taymiyya uses strong language to dismiss their tawhid as worthless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتبين أن قولهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد لا يصح استدلالهم به في حق الله تعالى، بأي قياس استدلوا . وإن قالوا : إن الواحد من الوجه الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد، وإذا صدر عنه اثنان فمن وجهين ، أو قالوا : هذا معلوم بالضرورة فلا يحتاج إلى دليل، فإنه لا بد بين من توحيدهم المصدر والصادر من مناسبة، والوجه الواحد لا يناسب اثنين قيل لهم : هذا يبطل قولكم في نفي الصفات . فإن الرب قد صدر عنه مخلوقات كثيرة ، وإذا كان الواحد لا يصدر عنه من الوجه الواحد إلا واحد امتنع أن تصدر هذه المخلوقات عن خالقها من وجه واحد . فدل ذلك على أنه متصف بأمور متنوعة من صفات متنوعة وأفعال متنوعة صدر عنه باعتبارها ما وجد من المخلوقات. فكان أصل ضلالهم توهمهم إمكان صدور المخلوقات عما قدروه من «الواحد» الذي لا يوجد إلا في الأذهان، لا في الأعيان ولقد أحسن بعضُ الفضلاء إذ قال : «الصَّقْعُ أحسن من توحيد الفلاسفة» (١) ، بل قصر فيما قال . (١) في الأصل الصمع» من غير نقط ، ولعله الصقع» بالقاف من قولهم: «صقع الحمارُ بضرطة» أي : أخرج ريحاً مصوّتاً من دبره في رطوبة وانتشار - معجم لين المستشرق» (E. W. Lane)
Thus it becomes clear that their statement 'from the One only one proceeds' is not valid when used as evidence concerning God, by whatever syllogism they reason. And if they say: 'From the one from one aspect only one proceeds, and if two proceed from it then from two aspects', or they say: 'This is known by necessity and needs no proof, for there must be a suitability between the source and the emanated, and one aspect is not suitable for two' — it is said to them: This invalidates your statement negating attributes. For the Lord has produced many creatures, and if from the one from one aspect only one proceeds, it is impossible that these creatures proceed from their Creator from one aspect. So that indicates that He is described with diverse things from diverse attributes and diverse actions, by virtue of which the creatures that exist proceed from Him. The root of their error was their delusion that it is possible for creatures to proceed from that 'one' which they conceive, which exists only in minds, not in external reality. And one of the virtuous people did well when he said: 'A coarse sound (al-saq') is better than the tawhid of the philosophers.' But he fell short in what he said. (1) In the original: 'al-sama' (?) without dots; likely 'al-saq'' with qaf, from their saying: 'the donkey let out a saq' (i.e., a noisy fart)' meaning: he emitted a resonant sound from his anus with moisture and spread — Lane's Lexicon.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' principle that 'from the One only one proceeds' (a Neoplatonic emanation doctrine). Ibn Taymiyya argues that this principle, when applied to God, contradicts observable reality: the multiplicity of creatures. He then engages with a possible objection: the philosophers might say that the emanation is from different aspects. Ibn Taymiyya responds that this actually proves that God must have multiple attributes, thus contradicting their own denial of attributes. He concludes that the philosophers' 'one' is a mental fiction, not a real entity. The crude statement 'a coarse sound is better than the philosophers' tawhid' is quoted to emphasize the absurdity of their concept. The footnote explains the meaning of 'saq'. Ibn Taymiyya further notes that even this vulgar comparison falls short of adequately criticizing their error.

الواحد
توحيد الفلاسفة
الصقع

C) They denied the existence of God.
D) They used faulty syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' mistake is ontological: they treat a mental abstraction as if it were a real agent, leading to a false concept of God.

Ibn Taymiyya's Destruction of the 'Pure Existence' Concept
Ibn Taymiyya dismantles the Avicennan notion of wajib al-wujud as existence conditioned by negation of quiddities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the philosophers' Necessary Being is ontologically inferior to contingent beings.

Core Idea
A being defined only by what it lacks cannot be perfect.

Ibn Taymiyya delivers a devastating critique of the Avicennan notion of the Necessary Being as 'existence conditioned by the negation of all quiddities.' He shows that on the philosophers' own principles, such a being is less perfect than any creature that possesses a positive quiddity. This argument strikes at the heart of philosophical theology by turning its own terms against it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' definition
Locate in the Arabic where Ibn Taymiyya states the philosophers' definition: that the Necessary Being is existence conditioned by the negation of all quiddities (al-wujud al-muqayyad bi-salbi jami' al-mahiyyat). Understand that this means His existence is not 'accidental' to any quiddity.
This definition is meant to avoid composition (tarkib) and causation (ta'lil) in God.
2
Find the point of comparison
Ibn Taymiyya compares two types of existents: (1) the Necessary Being as defined, and (2) any other existent that has a positive quiddity. Note the shared element: both exist. The distinguishing factor: one is distinguished by a negation (no quiddity), the other by a positive attribute (a specific quiddity).
The philosophers admit existence is more perfect than non-existence.
3
Apply the perfection principle
Since existence is perfective, a being that shares existence and is further distinguished by a positive quiddity has more existence (specific existence) than one distinguished only by negation. Therefore, the latter is less perfect. This is a simple comparative argument.
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'the one distinguished by a positive matter is more perfect than the one distinguished by a negative matter'.
4
Draw the conclusion
Thus, by the philosophers' own criteria, every contingent being (which has a quiddity) is more perfect than their Necessary Being. This reduces their doctrine to absurdity: they have made God inferior to His creation.
Ibn Taymiyya caps this with a dismissive remark: 'Sah safi' (shut up, liar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث ـ الصقع أحسن من توحيدهم
وإذا قالوا: «هو واحد ليس له صفات وأفعال تقوم به فلو صدر عنه أكثر من واحد لكان قد صدر عن الواحد من الوجه الواحد أكثر من واحد»، قيل لهم : فذلك الأول إن كان واحداً من كل وجه لزم أن لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد من كل وجه، وهذا خلافُ المشاهدة. وإن كان فيه كثرة بوجه ما فقد صدر عن الواحد من الوجه الواحد أكثر من واحد. وإن قالوا تلك الوجوه التي في الصادر الأول أمور عَدَمية»، قيل : فقد صدر عنه باعتبارها كثرة، وإذا جاز هذا جاز أن تجعل الأمور الإضافية الكثيرة في الأول مبدأ الكثرة . فكيفما أدير قولهم تبين أنه أفسد من قول النصارى في التثليث .
وحقيقة قولهم الذي قرره ابن سينا وأمثاله أنه أيّ موجود فُرِضَ في الوجود كان حقيقة قولهم أن كل موجود أكمل من رب العالمين. وذلك أنه قرر أنه وجود مشروط بسلب جميع الأمور الثبوتية عنه . وهو معنى قولهم : هو الوجود المقيَّد بسلب جميع الماهيات»، وقولهم «الوجود الذي لا يعرض له شيء من الماهيات فإن هذا بناه على قوله : إن وجود الماهيات عارض لها بناءً على أن في الخارج لكل ممكن وجوداً وماهية غير الوجود، وأن ذلك الوجود عَرَض لتلك الماهية وإن كان لازماً لها. ولهذا قالوا: إن واجب الوجود» وجوده لا يعرض لشيء من الماهيات، لئلا يلزم التركيب» و «التعليل»، فيكون وجوداً مقيداً بأن لا يعرض لشيء من الماهيات، فلا يجوز أن يكون له حقيقة في نفسه غير الوجود المحض الذي لا يتقيد بأمر ثبوتي . فيقال له فعلى هذا التقدير قد شارك جميع الموجودات في مسمى الوجود، وامتاز عنها بقيد عدمي، وهو سلب كل ثبوت، وامتاز كلّ منها عنه بما يخصه من الحقيقة الموجودة. ومعلوم أن الوجود أكمل من العدم، وهم يسلمون ذلك. فإذا اشترك اثنان في الوجود، وامتاز أحدهما عن الآخر بأمر وجودي، والآخر لم يمتز إلا بأمر عدمي كان الممتاز بأمر وجودي أكمل من الممتاز بأمر عدمي، لأنه شارك هذا في الوجود المشتَرَك، وامتاز عنه بالوجود المختص، وذاك لم يمتز عنه إلا بعدم كل وجود خاص. وسواء جعل الوجود المشترك (جنساً) أو عرضاً عاماً وجُعل المميّز بينهما «فصلاً» أو «خاصة» فعلى كل تقدير يلزم أن يكون ما لم يتميّز إلا بعدم دون ما تميز بوجود.
أو هو من قولهم : «صه صافع» أي : اسكت يا كذاب»، والصاقع: الكذاب.
The Third Station: A coarse sound is better than their tawhid. And if they say: 'He is one, having no attributes and actions subsisting in Him; if more than one were to proceed from Him, then more than one would have proceeded from the one from one aspect' — it is said to them: That first [principle], if it is one from every aspect, then only one from every aspect can proceed from it, and this contradicts observation. And if it has multiplicity in some aspect, then more than one has proceeded from the one from one aspect. And if they say: 'Those aspects in the first emanation are negative matters' — it is said: Then multiplicity has proceeded from it by virtue of them, and if this is permissible, then it is permissible to make the many additional matters in the first principle the source of multiplicity. However their statement is turned, it becomes evident that it is more corrupt than the Christians' statement about the Trinity. And the reality of their statement, as established by Ibn Sina and his likes, is that any existent assumed in existence — the reality of their statement is that every existent is more perfect than the Lord of the worlds. That is because he established that He is existence conditioned by the negation of all positive matters from Him. This is the meaning of their statement: 'He is existence conditioned by negation of all quiddities,' and their statement 'existence which is not affected by any quiddity.' For this is built on his statement that existence is an accident of quiddities, based on the view that in the external world every contingent has an existence and a quiddity distinct from existence, and that existence is an accident for that quiddity even if it is necessary for it. For this reason they said: The existence of the Necessary Existent is not an accident of any quiddity, to avoid composition and causation. So His existence is conditioned by not being an accident of any quiddity, so it is not permissible that He have a quiddity in Himself other than pure existence which is not conditioned by any positive matter. So it is said to him: On this assumption, He shares with all existents in the meaning of existence, and is distinguished from them by a negative condition, which is the negation of every positive [attribute], while each of them is distinguished from Him by its specific existing quiddity. And it is known that existence is more perfect than non-existence, and they admit that. So if two [existents] share in existence, and one is distinguished from the other by a positive matter, and the other is distinguished only by a negative matter, then the one distinguished by a positive matter is more perfect than the one distinguished by a negative matter, because it shares with this one in the common existence and is distinguished from it by specific existence, while that one is distinguished from it only by the absence of all specific existence. And whether the common existence is considered a genus or a general accident, and the distinguishing factor between them is considered a differentia or a proprium, in every case it follows that that which is distinguished only by negation is inferior to that which is distinguished by existence. Or it is from their saying: 'Sah safi' meaning 'Be quiet, O liar!' and al-saqi' is the liar.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the Avicennan concept of the Necessary Being as pure existence conditioned by the negation of all quiddities. Ibn Taymiyya presents several arguments. First, he examines the claim that God is one without attributes; if He were one in every respect, only one thing could proceed from Him, but we observe multiplicity. If He has multiplicity in some aspect, then that aspect itself explains the multiplicity, which contradicts their premise. Second, he points out that their concept makes every other existent more perfect than God, because other existents have positive quiddities in addition to existence, while God's existence is constrained by negations. Since existence is good, a being with additional positive existence (like creatures with specific quiddities) is more perfect than one that only has existence minus all positives. This is a devastating argument, showing that the philosophers' 'Necessary Being' is ontologically inferior. He then adds a dismissive remark equating their doctrine to saying 'Shut up, liar!' to emphasize its absurdity.

الوجود المقيد بسلب جميع الماهيات
واجب الوجود
التركيب
التعليل
سلب جميع الأمور الثبوتية

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is the philosophers' definition of the Necessary Being as 'existence conditioned by negation of all quiddities' self-defeating?
A) It implies that God has no attributes, which contradicts the Quran.
B) It makes contingent beings more perfect than God because they have positive quiddities in addition to existence.
C) It leads to the Trinity, which is worse than Christianity.
D) It denies that God is the cause of all things.

Key Takeaway
Defining God as 'pure existence' with negation of all quiddities, on the philosophers' own terms, makes every other existent more perfect than God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument in your own words, identifying the shared premise and the distinguishing factors. Then, evaluate whether the conclusion follows from the premises.
?
Apply the same comparative logic to another theological claim: Suppose someone defines God as 'being without any attributes'. Use Ibn Taymiyya's method to show that an entity with positive attributes would be more perfect.

The Illusion of Rational Composition
Ibn Taymiyya rejects rational composition and reviews three opinions on body composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's three views on body composition and explain why rational composition (tarkīb ʿaqlī) is mind-dependent, not external.

Core Idea
Rational composition is a mental construct, not a real feature of beings.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets the logicians' claim that a being with multiple attributes is a 'compound'. He argues that this 'rational composition' (genus+differentia) is merely a mental abstraction, not something found in the external world. He then reviews three competing views on what a body is composed of: sensory atoms, rational matter-form, or neither. Understanding this helps clarify that the real dispute is about the meaning of composition itself.

Guided Path
1
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's denial of rational composition
Find where he says rational parts exist only in minds, not in external existents. Note his contrast: real compounds are sensory.
2
Identify the three opinions on body composition
List the three views: (1) composed of sensory parts (atoms), (2) composed of rational parts (matter-form), (3) neither. Note Ibn Taymiyya's ranking: third is correct, second next, third most corrupt.
3
Examine his argument for the third view
What reason does he give for preferring the view that body is neither? (He says it's elaborated elsewhere, but we can infer his rejection of both atomism and hylomorphism.)
4
Apply the distinction to the attribute debate
How does this relate to the question of God's attributes? The logicians' claim that God is 'compound' because He has attributes is based on rational composition, which Ibn Taymiyya rejects as unreal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تسمية الواحد الموصوف بصفاته مركباً كتسمية الحي العالم القادر، لا يسمى الموصوف بالحياة والعلم والقدرة مركباً، فهذا اصطلاح لهم؛ لا يعرف في شيء من الشرائع ولا اللغات ولا عقول جماهير العقلاء جَعْلُ هذا تركيباً ولا تسميته بصفاته مركباً. فإذا قالوا: نحن نسميه تركيباً لأن فيه إثبات معان متعددة لذات واحدة، ونحن نسمى ذلك تركيباً، ونقيم الدليل العقلي على امتناعه قيل: إذا كان الأمر كذلك فالنظر في المعاني المعقولة لا في الألفاظ السمعية، ونحن لا نوافقكم على جعل الإنسان مركباً من الحيوانية والناطقية، ولا أن في الوجود شيئاً مركباً من أجزاء عقلية. بل المركب من الأجزاء العقلية إنما يكون في الأذهان، لا في الأعيان. وكل ما في الوجود من المركبات فإنما هو مركب من أجزاء حسية، موجودة في الخارج. والناس قد تنازعوا في الجسم، هل هو مركب من أجزاء حسية، وهي الجواهر المفردة، أو من أجزاء عقلية، وهي المادة والصورة، أو لا من هذا ولا من هذا، على ثلاثة أقوال، والصحيح عندنا هو القول الثالث. ثم يليه قول من جعله مركباً من الأجزاء الحسية. وأفسد الثلاثة قول من جعله مركباً من الأجزاء العقلية، كما قد بسط في موضعه. وحينئذ فمن قال: إن الباري جسم، وإن الجسم مركب من الأجزاء الحسية أو العقلية، كان الاستدلال على بطلان هذا التركيب استدلالاً مقبولاً ممن يقوله، فإن عليه تعالى مطلوبه صحيح، لكن يبقى النظر هل يُحسِن هذا المستدل الاستدلال عليه أو لا يحسنه. وأما من قال: إنه ليس بمركب، لا هذا التركيب ولا هذا التركيب، وإنما أسميه جسماً أو جوهراً، لأن الجسم والجوهر عندي اسم لكل موجود قائم بنفسه، فهذا النزاع معه في اسم الجسم والجوهر نزاع لفظي، لا عقلي ولا شرعي. فإن الشرع لم ينطق بهذا الاسم لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً، والعقل إنما ينظر في المعاني لا في مجرد اللفظ. فالنظر مع هذا إما في إثبات كون الجسم مركباً أحد التركيبين، وهذا بحث عقلي معروف؛ وإما في كون لفظ الجسم في اللغة لكل مركب، وهذا بحث لغوي له موضع آخر. وهؤلاء ليس مقصودهم بنفي التركيب هذا المعنى فقط، فإن هذا يوافقهم عليه كثير من مثبتة الصفات، لكن مقصودهم أنه لا يتصف بصفة ثبوتية أصلاً. وأخذوا لفظ التركيب الذي وافقهم بعضُ أهل الكلام على نفي معناه، وتوسعوا فيه حتى جعلوه أعمّ مما وافقهم عليه أولئك المتكلمون ونفوه، وصاروا كالجهمية المحضة التي تنفي الأسماء والصفات، أو تثبتها على وجه المجاز. والمقصود هنا أن نقول قولهم: الموصوف بالحياة والعلم والقدرة مركب من هذا وهذا، يقال لهم: سَمُّوا هذا تركيباً، أو تجسيماً، أو ما شئتم من الأسماء، فما الدليل على نفي هذا عقلاً أو سمعاً؟ قالوا: الدليل على ذلك أن كل مركب مفتقر
As for calling the one described by His attributes a 'compound'—like calling the living, knowing, powerful one 'compound'—the one described with life, knowledge, and power is not called 'compound'. This is a convention of theirs; it is not known in any of the religious laws, nor languages, nor the minds of the majority of rational people to make this 'composition' nor to call him by His attributes 'compound'. So if they say: We call it 'composition' because it involves affirming multiple meanings for one essence, and we call that 'composition', and we provide the rational proof for its impossibility, it is said: If that is the case, then consideration is of the intelligible meanings, not of the transmitted words. And we do not agree with you in making man a compound of animality and rationality, nor that there exists in reality anything composed of rational parts. Rather, the compound of rational parts is only in minds, not in external existents. And every compound that exists in reality is only composed of sensory parts that exist externally. People have disputed concerning the body: whether it is composed of sensory parts—which are indivisible atoms—or of rational parts—which are matter and form—or neither this nor that, on three opinions. The correct according to us is the third opinion. Then next is the opinion of those who make it composed of sensory parts. And the most corrupt of the three is the opinion of those who make it composed of rational parts, as has been elaborated in its place. So then, whoever says that the Creator is a body, and that the body is composed of sensory or rational parts, the inference to the falsity of this composition is an acceptable inference from whoever says it, for his objective regarding the Most High is correct, but the question remains whether this inferrer performs the inference well or not. As for whoever says: He is not compound, neither this composition nor that composition, but rather I call Him 'body' or 'substance' because 'body' and 'substance' in my view are names for every existent that is self-subsistent, then this dispute with him over the name 'body' and 'substance' is a verbal dispute, not rational nor religious. For the Law has not uttered this name, neither negating nor affirming; and the intellect only considers meanings, not mere words. So the consideration with him is either regarding the affirmation that the body is one of the two compositions—this is a known rational investigation; or regarding whether the word 'body' in language applies to every composite—this is a linguistic investigation with another place. And these people's purpose in negating composition is not merely this meaning, for many attribute-affirmers agree with them on that, but their purpose is that He is not described by any positive attribute at all. They took the word 'composition' which some theologians agreed with them in negating its meaning, and they extended it until they made it more general than what those theologians agreed with them on, and they negated it, becoming like the pure Jahmiyya who negate the names and attributes, or affirm them metaphorically. The intended point here is to say: Their statement that the one described with life, knowledge, and power is a compound of this and that—it is said to them: Call this 'composition', or 'incarnation', or whatever you wish of names, so what is the proof for negating this rationally or by revelation? They said: The proof for that is that every compound is dependent
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by Ibn Taymiyya targeting the logicians' technical use of 'compound'. He begins by rejecting the logicians' convention of calling a single essence with multiple attributes a 'compound', arguing that this is unknown in religious law, languages, or common rationality. He then challenges the logicians: even if they call it 'composition', the issue is about intelligible meanings, not terminology. Ibn Taymiyya denies that a human is a 'compound' of animality and rationality (the genus and differentia of the definition), asserting that rational composition (tarkīb 'aqlī) exists only in the mind, not in external reality. Real compounds are only composed of sensory parts. He then discusses the dispute over whether a body is composed of atoms, matter and form, or neither. He favors the third view (neither), followed by the atomic view, and considers the matter-form view the most corrupt. From this, he draws a distinction: if someone claims God is a body composed of parts, the argument against that is rationally acceptable, but it depends on whether that person argues correctly. However, if someone calls God 'body' or 'substance' merely as names for any self-subsistent being, the dispute is purely verbal—neither religious nor rational, since the Law does not use these terms. The real issue, Ibn Taymiyya reveals, is that those who negate composition actually intend to negate all positive attributes of God altogether. They took the term 'composition' that some theologians agreed to negate and expanded it to include any attribute, thus falling into Jahmī-style negation. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya states the argument of the attribute-negators: that a being described with life, knowledge, and power is a compound, and every compound is dependent. He asks for the proof of this negation, and the passage cuts off with the beginning of their response. The passage weaves together criticism of logical terminology, a defense of divine attributes, and a clarification that many disputes are merely linguistic.

Verbal Dispute or Real Issue? The Negation of Divine Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes verbal from real disputes and exposes the attribute-negators' agenda.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a verbal dispute (niza' lafzi) and a genuine rational dispute, and identify Ibn Taymiyya's claim that the attribute-negators' real aim is to deny all positive attributes.

Core Idea
Disputes over calling God 'body' are often verbal; the real issue is attribute negation.

In this continuation, Ibn Taymiyya sharpens his critique. He distinguishes between those who argue that God is a body composed of parts (a real dispute) and those who merely use the term 'body' to mean any self-subsistent being (a verbal dispute). He then reveals that the attribute-negators have hijacked the term 'composition' to deny all divine attributes, making them akin to the Jahmiyya. This lesson helps you see how logicians extended a concept to serve a heretical agenda.

Guided Path
1
Find the two types of disputants
Identify the two groups: those who claim God is a body composed of parts (real rational dispute) and those who call God 'body' or 'substance' as a name for any self-subsistent being (verbal dispute).
2
Analyze the distinction between real and verbal disputes
What makes a dispute verbal? The Law does not speak of these terms, and the intellect considers meanings, not words. So if both parties agree on the meaning but differ on the word, it's verbal.
3
Uncover the attribute-negators' real motive
Ibn Taymiyya says their purpose is not merely to negate this specific composition, but to deny that God has any positive attribute at all. How did they extend the term 'composition'?
4
Connect to the Jahmiyya comparison
They have become like the Jahmiyya who negate names and attributes or affirm them metaphorically.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تسمية الواحد الموصوف بصفاته مركباً كتسمية الحي العالم القادر، لا يسمى الموصوف بالحياة والعلم والقدرة مركباً، فهذا اصطلاح لهم؛ لا يعرف في شيء من الشرائع ولا اللغات ولا عقول جماهير العقلاء جَعْلُ هذا تركيباً ولا تسميته بصفاته مركباً. فإذا قالوا: نحن نسميه تركيباً لأن فيه إثبات معان متعددة لذات واحدة، ونحن نسمى ذلك تركيباً، ونقيم الدليل العقلي على امتناعه قيل: إذا كان الأمر كذلك فالنظر في المعاني المعقولة لا في الألفاظ السمعية، ونحن لا نوافقكم على جعل الإنسان مركباً من الحيوانية والناطقية، ولا أن في الوجود شيئاً مركباً من أجزاء عقلية. بل المركب من الأجزاء العقلية إنما يكون في الأذهان، لا في الأعيان. وكل ما في الوجود من المركبات فإنما هو مركب من أجزاء حسية، موجودة في الخارج. والناس قد تنازعوا في الجسم، هل هو مركب من أجزاء حسية، وهي الجواهر المفردة، أو من أجزاء عقلية، وهي المادة والصورة، أو لا من هذا ولا من هذا، على ثلاثة أقوال، والصحيح عندنا هو القول الثالث. ثم يليه قول من جعله مركباً من الأجزاء الحسية. وأفسد الثلاثة قول من جعله مركباً من الأجزاء العقلية، كما قد بسط في موضعه. وحينئذ فمن قال: إن الباري جسم، وإن الجسم مركب من الأجزاء الحسية أو العقلية، كان الاستدلال على بطلان هذا التركيب استدلالاً مقبولاً ممن يقوله، فإن عليه تعالى مطلوبه صحيح، لكن يبقى النظر هل يُحسِن هذا المستدل الاستدلال عليه أو لا يحسنه. وأما من قال: إنه ليس بمركب، لا هذا التركيب ولا هذا التركيب، وإنما أسميه جسماً أو جوهراً، لأن الجسم والجوهر عندي اسم لكل موجود قائم بنفسه، فهذا النزاع معه في اسم الجسم والجوهر نزاع لفظي، لا عقلي ولا شرعي. فإن الشرع لم ينطق بهذا الاسم لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً، والعقل إنما ينظر في المعاني لا في مجرد اللفظ. فالنظر مع هذا إما في إثبات كون الجسم مركباً أحد التركيبين، وهذا بحث عقلي معروف؛ وإما في كون لفظ الجسم في اللغة لكل مركب، وهذا بحث لغوي له موضع آخر. وهؤلاء ليس مقصودهم بنفي التركيب هذا المعنى فقط، فإن هذا يوافقهم عليه كثير من مثبتة الصفات، لكن مقصودهم أنه لا يتصف بصفة ثبوتية أصلاً. وأخذوا لفظ التركيب الذي وافقهم بعضُ أهل الكلام على نفي معناه، وتوسعوا فيه حتى جعلوه أعمّ مما وافقهم عليه أولئك المتكلمون ونفوه، وصاروا كالجهمية المحضة التي تنفي الأسماء والصفات، أو تثبتها على وجه المجاز. والمقصود هنا أن نقول قولهم: الموصوف بالحياة والعلم والقدرة مركب من هذا وهذا، يقال لهم: سَمُّوا هذا تركيباً، أو تجسيماً، أو ما شئتم من الأسماء، فما الدليل على نفي هذا عقلاً أو سمعاً؟ قالوا: الدليل على ذلك أن كل مركب مفتقر
As for calling the one described by His attributes a 'compound'—like calling the living, knowing, powerful one 'compound'—the one described with life, knowledge, and power is not called 'compound'. This is a convention of theirs; it is not known in any of the religious laws, nor languages, nor the minds of the majority of rational people to make this 'composition' nor to call him by His attributes 'compound'. So if they say: We call it 'composition' because it involves affirming multiple meanings for one essence, and we call that 'composition', and we provide the rational proof for its impossibility, it is said: If that is the case, then consideration is of the intelligible meanings, not of the transmitted words. And we do not agree with you in making man a compound of animality and rationality, nor that there exists in reality anything composed of rational parts. Rather, the compound of rational parts is only in minds, not in external existents. And every compound that exists in reality is only composed of sensory parts that exist externally. People have disputed concerning the body: whether it is composed of sensory parts—which are indivisible atoms—or of rational parts—which are matter and form—or neither this nor that, on three opinions. The correct according to us is the third opinion. Then next is the opinion of those who make it composed of sensory parts. And the most corrupt of the three is the opinion of those who make it composed of rational parts, as has been elaborated in its place. So then, whoever says that the Creator is a body, and that the body is composed of sensory or rational parts, the inference to the falsity of this composition is an acceptable inference from whoever says it, for his objective regarding the Most High is correct, but the question remains whether this inferrer performs the inference well or not. As for whoever says: He is not compound, neither this composition nor that composition, but rather I call Him 'body' or 'substance' because 'body' and 'substance' in my view are names for every existent that is self-subsistent, then this dispute with him over the name 'body' and 'substance' is a verbal dispute, not rational nor religious. For the Law has not uttered this name, neither negating nor affirming; and the intellect only considers meanings, not mere words. So the consideration with him is either regarding the affirmation that the body is one of the two compositions—this is a known rational investigation; or regarding whether the word 'body' in language applies to every composite—this is a linguistic investigation with another place. And these people's purpose in negating composition is not merely this meaning, for many attribute-affirmers agree with them on that, but their purpose is that He is not described by any positive attribute at all. They took the word 'composition' which some theologians agreed with them in negating its meaning, and they extended it until they made it more general than what those theologians agreed with them on, and they negated it, becoming like the pure Jahmiyya who negate the names and attributes, or affirm them metaphorically. The intended point here is to say: Their statement that the one described with life, knowledge, and power is a compound of this and that—it is said to them: Call this 'composition', or 'incarnation', or whatever you wish of names, so what is the proof for negating this rationally or by revelation? They said: The proof for that is that every compound is dependent
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by Ibn Taymiyya targeting the logicians' technical use of 'compound'. He begins by rejecting the logicians' convention of calling a single essence with multiple attributes a 'compound', arguing that this is unknown in religious law, languages, or common rationality. He then challenges the logicians: even if they call it 'composition', the issue is about intelligible meanings, not terminology. Ibn Taymiyya denies that a human is a 'compound' of animality and rationality (the genus and differentia of the definition), asserting that rational composition (tarkīb 'aqlī) exists only in the mind, not in external reality. Real compounds are only composed of sensory parts. He then discusses the dispute over whether a body is composed of atoms, matter and form, or neither. He favors the third view (neither), followed by the atomic view, and considers the matter-form view the most corrupt. From this, he draws a distinction: if someone claims God is a body composed of parts, the argument against that is rationally acceptable, but it depends on whether that person argues correctly. However, if someone calls God 'body' or 'substance' merely as names for any self-subsistent being, the dispute is purely verbal—neither religious nor rational, since the Law does not use these terms. The real issue, Ibn Taymiyya reveals, is that those who negate composition actually intend to negate all positive attributes of God altogether. They took the term 'composition' that some theologians agreed to negate and expanded it to include any attribute, thus falling into Jahmī-style negation. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya states the argument of the attribute-negators: that a being described with life, knowledge, and power is a compound, and every compound is dependent. He asks for the proof of this negation, and the passage cuts off with the beginning of their response. The passage weaves together criticism of logical terminology, a defense of divine attributes, and a clarification that many disputes are merely linguistic.

The Necessary Existent and the Four Causes
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the Necessary Existent and His attributes require none of the four causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's claim that the Necessary Existent and His attributes do not require any of the four causes, and explain why this refutes the logicians' framework.

Core Idea
God's essence and attributes are self-sufficient; no efficient, final, material, or formal cause applies to them.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a core Aristotelian doctrine: that every existing thing requires four causes. Here he specifically targets the application to the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) and His attributes. This is a critical refinement that protects tawhid from philosophical contamination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that the Necessary Existent does not need any of the four causes. Note that he includes both the essence and the attributes.
The phrase 'لا يفتقر إلى فاعل ولا علة فاعلة' is key.
2
Understand the four causes enumerated
Ibn Taymiyya mentions the efficient cause (al-ʿilla al-fāʿila), the final cause (al-ʿilla al-ghāʾiyya), and the formal cause (al-ʿilla al-ṣūriyya). The material cause is implied in the discussion of substrate (maḥall).
He focuses on efficient, final, and formal; the material is addressed indirectly.
3
Distinguish two meanings of 'necessarily existent' for attributes
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that if 'Necessary Existent' is taken in a restricted sense, attributes are necessary; if it includes having no substrate, then attributes are not necessary in that sense because they inhere in the essence.
This distinction prevents equivocation and shows that his critique targets a specific interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - مجموع الذات والصفة لا يفتقر إلى العلل الأربع

و واجب الوجود الذي دلت الممكنات عليه هو الموجود بنفسه، القائم بنفسه، رب العالمين، الذي لا يفتقر إلى فاعل ولا علة فاعلة، بل هو نفسه نفسه وصفاته لا يفتقر إلى شيء من العلل الأربع.

كون صفاته تعالى واجبة الوجود

وأما نفس صفاته فليس لها فاعل ولا علة فاعلة ولا علة غائية، ولا صورية.

فهي واجبة الوجود إذا عُني بـ «واجب الوجود» أحد هذه المعاني.

وإن عنى بـ «واجب الوجود» ما هو أعم من ذلك حتى يدخل فيه ما ليس له محل كون الذات محلا للصفات يقوم به فليست واجبة الوجود بهذا التفسير، بل هي ممكنة الوجود، والذات مستلزمة لها، وهي محل لها. وإذا قيل: «فيلزم أن تكون الذات فاعلة وقابلة وذاك باطل»، قيل: كلا المقدمتين ممنوعة. فإن كون الذات مستلزمة لصفتها القائمة بها لا يقتضي أن تكون فاعلة لها؛ بل يمتنع أن تكون الذات فاعلة للصفة اللازمة في الممكن، فكيف في رب العالمين؟ بل أن يكون الرب فاعلاً لما هو لازم له وإن كان بائناً عنه، كالفلك، فكيف يكون فاعلاً لصفته اللازمة له؟ وإن قدر أنهم سموا هذا الاستلزام «فعلاً»، وقالوا: فاعلة هي بهذا الاعتبار قيل لهم فلا نسلم أنه لا يجوز كون الشيء الواحد فاعلاً وقابلاً بهذا الاعتبار، بل ولا باعتبار آخر.
The third station: The totality of essence and attribute does not need the four causes. And the Necessary Existent which the contingent things point to is the Existent by itself, self-subsisting, the Lord of the worlds, who does not need an agent nor an efficient cause, but He Himself and His attributes do not need any of the four causes. [Subheading: The fact that His attributes are necessarily existent] As for His attributes themselves, they have no agent, no efficient cause, no final cause, and no formal cause. So they are necessarily existent if by 'Necessary Existent' one of these meanings is intended. But if by 'Necessary Existent' something more general is intended, such that it includes what has no locus—i.e., the essence being a locus for the attributes subsisting in it—then they are not necessarily existent according to this interpretation; rather they are possibly existent, and the essence entails them and is a locus for them. And if it is said: 'Then it follows that the essence is an agent and a recipient, and that is false,' it is said: Both premises are denied. For the essence's entailment of its subsisting attribute does not require that it be an agent for it; rather it is impossible for the essence to be the agent of the inseparable attribute in the contingent, so how about the Lord of the worlds? Rather for the Lord to be an agent of what is necessary for Him even if separate from Him, like the celestial sphere, how could He be an agent of His inseparable attribute? And if it is supposed that they call this entailment 'act' and say: She is an agent in this respect, it is said to them: We do not concede that it is impermissible for one thing to be both agent and recipient in this respect, nor even in another respect.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the logicians' claim that the attributes of the Necessary Existent require the four causes. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the third station: the totality of essence and attribute does not need the four causes. He then describes the Necessary Existent as self-sufficient and not needing any cause. He addresses the attributes specifically, saying they have no efficient cause, final cause, or formal cause. However, he distinguishes two meanings of 'necessary existence': if it means one of these specific meanings, the attributes are necessary; but if it means something broader that includes having no substrate, then the attributes are not necessary in that sense because they inhere in the essence. He refutes the objection that the essence would then be both agent and recipient for its attributes by arguing that entailment does not imply agency, and it is impossible for the essence to be the agent of its inseparable attribute. He also allows that if one calls this entailment 'act', then it is possible for one thing to be both agent and recipient in that sense.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
العلل الأربع
فاعل
قابل
غائية
صورية

A) Because attributes are accidents and accidents cannot have efficient causes.
B) Because the essence itself is the efficient cause of the attributes, but not in the usual sense.
C) Because the attributes are necessarily existent with the essence and the essence is not an agent for them.
D) Because the attributes are created and then attached to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Existent and His attributes are free from the four causes; the attributes are necessary entailments of the essence, not caused products.

Refuting Entailment as Agency
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the objection that the essence would be both agent and recipient of its attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that God's essence being the locus of attributes implies that it is their efficient cause, using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning.

Core Idea
Entailment does not equal causation; the essence does not 'make' its attributes.

Ibn Taymiyya anticipates a powerful objection: if the essence entails its attributes, then it must be both agent (making the attributes) and recipient (having them). He dismantles this objection by denying both premises and drawing an analogy with contingent things. This lesson focuses on his logical moves.

Guided Path
1
Extract the objection
Find the sentence starting with 'وإذا قيل' (And if it is said). This is the imagined objection: that the essence would be both agent and recipient, which is impossible.
The objection is a common philosophical critique of the Kalam position on attributes.
2
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's response: denial of premises
He says 'كلا المقدمتين ممنوعة' (both premises are denied). Identify the two premises: 1) Entailment implies agency, 2) An entity cannot be both agent and recipient. He denies them based on the impossibility of the essence being the agent of its inseparable attribute.
He provides a reason: in contingent things, the essence cannot be the agent of its inseparable attribute, so a fortiori it cannot be in the divine case.
3
Recognize the use of 'a fortiori' analogy
Ibn Taymiyya argues that if something is impossible for contingent things (essence being agent of its attributes), it is even more impossible for God. He uses the phrase 'فكيف في رب العالمين' (how about the Lord of the worlds).
This is a qiyas awlawi (a fortiori) argument.
4
Consider the terminological caveat
At the end, he addresses those who might call the entailment an 'act' (fiʿl). He allows that if one redefines 'act' to mean entailment, it is not problematic to say God is both agent and recipient in that sense.
This shows Ibn Taymiyya's flexibility: he does not reject the term outright but insists on clear definitions.

كون صفاته تعالى واجبة الوجود

وأما نفس صفاته فليس لها فاعل ولا علة فاعلة ولا علة غائية، ولا صورية.

فهي واجبة الوجود إذا عُني بـ «واجب الوجود» أحد هذه المعاني.

وإن عنى بـ «واجب الوجود» ما هو أعم من ذلك حتى يدخل فيه ما ليس له محل كون الذات محلا للصفات يقوم به فليست واجبة الوجود بهذا التفسير، بل هي ممكنة الوجود، والذات مستلزمة لها، وهي محل لها. وإذا قيل: «فيلزم أن تكون الذات فاعلة وقابلة وذاك باطل»، قيل: كلا المقدمتين ممنوعة. فإن كون الذات مستلزمة لصفتها القائمة بها لا يقتضي أن تكون فاعلة لها؛ بل يمتنع أن تكون الذات فاعلة للصفة اللازمة في الممكن، فكيف في رب العالمين؟ بل أن يكون الرب فاعلاً لما هو لازم له وإن كان بائناً عنه، كالفلك، فكيف يكون فاعلاً لصفته اللازمة له؟ وإن قدر أنهم سموا هذا الاستلزام «فعلاً»، وقالوا: فاعلة هي بهذا الاعتبار قيل لهم فلا نسلم أنه لا يجوز كون الشيء الواحد فاعلاً وقابلاً بهذا الاعتبار، بل ولا باعتبار آخر.
The third station: The totality of essence and attribute does not need the four causes. And the Necessary Existent which the contingent things point to is the Existent by itself, self-subsisting, the Lord of the worlds, who does not need an agent nor an efficient cause, but He Himself and His attributes do not need any of the four causes. [Subheading: The fact that His attributes are necessarily existent] As for His attributes themselves, they have no agent, no efficient cause, no final cause, and no formal cause. So they are necessarily existent if by 'Necessary Existent' one of these meanings is intended. But if by 'Necessary Existent' something more general is intended, such that it includes what has no locus—i.e., the essence being a locus for the attributes subsisting in it—then they are not necessarily existent according to this interpretation; rather they are possibly existent, and the essence entails them and is a locus for them. And if it is said: 'Then it follows that the essence is an agent and a recipient, and that is false,' it is said: Both premises are denied. For the essence's entailment of its subsisting attribute does not require that it be an agent for it; rather it is impossible for the essence to be the agent of the inseparable attribute in the contingent, so how about the Lord of the worlds? Rather for the Lord to be an agent of what is necessary for Him even if separate from Him, like the celestial sphere, how could He be an agent of His inseparable attribute? And if it is supposed that they call this entailment 'act' and say: She is an agent in this respect, it is said to them: We do not concede that it is impermissible for one thing to be both agent and recipient in this respect, nor even in another respect.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the logicians' claim that the attributes of the Necessary Existent require the four causes. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the third station: the totality of essence and attribute does not need the four causes. He then describes the Necessary Existent as self-sufficient and not needing any cause. He addresses the attributes specifically, saying they have no efficient cause, final cause, or formal cause. However, he distinguishes two meanings of 'necessary existence': if it means one of these specific meanings, the attributes are necessary; but if it means something broader that includes having no substrate, then the attributes are not necessary in that sense because they inhere in the essence. He refutes the objection that the essence would then be both agent and recipient for its attributes by arguing that entailment does not imply agency, and it is impossible for the essence to be the agent of its inseparable attribute. He also allows that if one calls this entailment 'act', then it is possible for one thing to be both agent and recipient in that sense.

Key Terms
فاعل
قابل
مستلزمة
صفة لازمة
استلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Ibn Taymiyya's denial of the first premise in the objection?
A) He argues that the essence cannot be a recipient of its own attributes.
B) He argues that in contingent things, the essence cannot be the agent of its inseparable attribute, so a fortiori God cannot.
C) He argues that entailment is identical to efficient causation.
D) He argues that the objection is based on a misunderstanding of the four causes.

Key Takeaway
Entailment (istilzam) of attributes by the essence does not imply efficient causation; the objection collapses once this confusion is removed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In a mock jadal session, one person argues: 'If God is powerful by a power, then power must be a cause of God's acts and God would be dependent on His power.' The other person responds using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning from this passage.
