Identifying Circular Reasoning in Logicians' Proof
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' circular proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circular reasoning (musadara) in Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' argument that the Necessary Existent cannot be both agent and recipient.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya exposes that the logicians' proof against composition is circular.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's 'Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin' attacks a foundational premise of the logicians' theology. He shows that their argument for the simplicity of the Necessary Existent relies on two premises that each depend on the other. We will dissect this circularity to sharpen your ability to spot such fallacies in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the two propositions that are used in the proof. The first is that the Necessary Existent has no composition (negation of tarkib). The second is that the one cannot be both agent and recipient (fāʿil and qābil).
2
Trace how they are used
Notice that to prove no composition, they use the premise that the one cannot be both agent and recipient. Conversely, to prove that the one cannot be both agent and recipient, they rely on the negation of composition.
3
Recognize the circularity
Each premise is used as evidence for the other, creating a circle. In logical terms, this is called musadara (petitio principii) or begging the question.
4
Conclude the logical consequence
Because the proof depends on this circle, neither premise is established independently. The argument fails to prove its conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن استدلوا على ذلك بحجتهم المعروفة المبنية على نفي التركيب، فلا يمكنهم جعل ذلك مقدمة في نفس الدليل، لأن هذا مصادرة على المطلوب، وهو جعل من المقدمتين المطلوب مقدمة في إثبات نفسه بعبارة أخرى. فإنهم إذا نفوا التركيب عن «الواجب» بناءً على مقدمات، منها: أن الواحد لا يكون فاعلاً وقابلاً، وأثبتوا هذه المقدمة بناءً على نفي «التركيب»، كانوا قد أثبتوا كلاً من المقدمتين بالأخرى، فلا تكون واحدة منهما ثابتة.
If they argue for that with their well-known argument based on the negation of composition, then they cannot make that a premise in the same proof, because this is a petition of principle (circular reasoning): they make one of the two premises the thing sought, using it to prove itself in other words. For when they negate composition from the Necessary based on premises, including that the one cannot be both agent and recipient, and they establish this premise based on the negation of composition, they have established each of the two premises by the other, so neither of them is established.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' proof that the Necessary Existent has no composition. Ibn Taymiyya points out a circularity: their argument that the Necessary Existent cannot be both agent and recipient is used to prove that it has no composition, but the negation of composition itself is used to prove that it cannot be both agent and recipient. Thus they rely on each premise to prove the other, so neither is established independently. This is a classic refutation of a circular argument.

Key Terms
التركيب
مصادرة
الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Begging the question (petitio principii)
B) False dilemma
C) Hasty generalization
D) Affirming the consequent

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that circular reasoning renders a proof invalid because no premise is independently established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Contradiction: Allowing Entailment of Effects but Not Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya exposes inconsistency in logicians' position on divine entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze the logical inconsistency in the claim that God's essence can entail separate effects but not intrinsic attributes.

Core Idea
If entailment of effects does not negate necessity, then entailment of attributes should also be allowed.

Ibn Taymiyya here targets a deep inconsistency in the logicians' and theologians' conception of God. They accept that God's essence necessitates the existence of created effects, yet they deny that His essence necessitates His attributes, fearing this would imply need or composition. Ibn Taymiyya uses their own principle to turn the tables: if effects are allowed, attributes are even more fitting. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for the divine nature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
First, locate where Ibn Taymiyya states the logicians' contradictory positions: they allow the essence to entail separate effects (e.g., creation) but forbid it from entailing attributes (e.g., knowledge, power). Note that they consider the latter a form of dependence.
These are the two horns of the contradiction.
2
Extract the common principle
The logicians accept that the essence entails effects without compromising its necessity (wujub al-wujud). Ibn Taymiyya points out that the same principle—entailment does not negate necessity—should apply to attributes.
3
Apply the principle consistently
If the entailment of effects is harmless, then by analogous reasoning (awla), the entailment of attributes is even less problematic. Conversely, if entailment of attributes would negate necessity, then the same should apply to effects, leading to absurd consequences (e.g., God becoming an agent after not being one).
This is a reductio ad absurdum of their position.
4
Recognize the critique's force
Understand why Ibn Taymiyya calls this one of the 'greatest contradictions.' The logicians cannot have it both ways; their selective application of the entailment principle is arbitrary and rationally indefensible.
This exposes a flaw in their theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبدع الممكنات هو واجب الوجود، والدليل الدال على إثبات واجب الوجود دل على إثبات فاعل مبدع لـ «الممكنات»، والمبدع لها يمتنع أن يكون صفة قائمة بغيره. فإن ذلك الموصوف هو الفاعل حينئذ دون مجرد الصفة، وهذا الواجب الذي دلت عليه آياته ليس يفتقر إلى علة من العلل الأربع مع اتصافه بصفاته اللازمة له.

إذا جاز استلزام الذات للمفعولات فاستلزامها للصفات أولى

ومن أعظم تناقض هؤلاء أنهم يقولون: الذات إذا استلزمت الصفات كان ذلك افتقاراً منها إلى الغير، فلا تكون واجبة وهم يقولون: إن الذات مستلزمة لمفعولاتها المنفصلة عنها، ولا يجعلون ذلك منافياً لوجوب وجودها بنفسها. فإن كان الاستلزام للمفعولات لا ينافي وجوب الوجود، فالاستلزام للصفات أولى أن لا ينافيه. وإن كان ذلك الاستلزام ينافي وجوب الوجود كان حينئذ قد عرض له أن يفعل بعد امتناع كونه فاعلا بعد أن لم يكن فاعلاً، وهذا ممتنع عندهم. وهذا أظهر المعقولات التي قهروا بها أن لم يكن المتكلمين - الجهمية، والقدرية، ومن وافقهم من الأشعرية، والكلابية، وأتباعهم. ولم يميز الطائفتان بين فاعل النوع و فاعل العين.

ثم إذا جاز أن يفعل، فـ «الفعل» ثبوتي فيلزم قيامه به. ودعوى أن «المفعول» عين الفعل مكابرة للعقل. وإذا جاز قيام «الفعل» به كان قيام «الصفات» بطريق الأولى.

والذين جعلوا «المفعول» عين الفعل من أهل الكلام كالأشعرية ومن وافقهم من حنبلي وشافعي ومالكي وغيرهم إنما ألجأهم إلى ذلك فرارهم من قيام الحوادث بالقديم و تسلسل الحوادث. وهذان كل منهما غير ممتنع عند [٢٢٠] هؤلاء الفلاسفة؛ مع أن أولئك المتكلمين الثفاة حجتهم على النفي ضعيفة. وجماهير المسلمين وطوائفهم على خلاف ذلك. والقول بأن «الخلق» غير
The Creator of the possible things is the Necessary Existent. The proof establishing the Necessary Existent establishes a Creator who originates the possible things, and the Creator of them cannot be an attribute subsisting in another. For the described entity is the agent, not the mere attribute. This Necessary Existent which His signs indicate does not need any of the four causes despite being characterized by His necessary attributes. [Subheading: If entailment of the essence for effects is allowed, then its entailment for attributes is more fitting.] Among the greatest contradictions of these people is that they say: if the essence entails the attributes, that is a need for another, so it would not be necessary, yet they say: the essence entails its separate effects, and they do not consider that contradictory to its necessity. If entailment of effects does not negate necessity, then entailment of attributes is more fitting not to negate it. And if that entailment negates necessity, then it would have acquired the ability to act after being unable, which is impossible according to them. This is one of the clearest rational arguments by which they defeated the theologians – the Jahmiyya, Qadariyya, and those who agreed with them among the Ashʿaris, Kullabis, and their followers. The two groups did not distinguish between the agent of the type and the agent of the individual. Then if it is allowed to act, then 'act' is a positive attribute, so it necessitates its subsistence in Him. The claim that 'the effect' is identical to the act is an absurdity against reason. If subsistence of the act in Him is allowed, then subsistence of attributes is even more primary. Those who made 'the effect' identical to the act among the theologians, like the Ashʿaris and those who followed them from Hanbalis, Shafiʿis, Malikis, and others, were forced into this only by their flight from accidents subsisting in the eternal and the chain of events. Both of these are not impossible according to these philosophers, while the argument of those coarse theologians for negation is weak. The majority of Muslims and their groups are contrary to that. The saying that 'creation' is not...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the inconsistency in the logicians' and theologians' positions. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Necessary Existent, as the Creator of contingent things, cannot be a mere attribute but must be a self-subsistent entity. He then highlights a contradiction: the logicians allow that the essence entails its separate effects without negating necessity, but they claim that entailment of attributes would imply need. He shows this is inconsistent. He also critiques the view that the effect is identical to the act, calling it an absurdity. He explains that some theologians (like the Ashʿaris) adopted this view to avoid accidents subsisting in God, but their argument is weak. He concludes that the majority of Muslims reject that view.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes a logical inconsistency: if divine entailment of effects does not compromise necessity, then entailment of attributes should be even more acceptable.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Identity of Effect and Act
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the identity of effect and act using the priority of attributes over effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's conditional argument against identifying the effect with the act and evaluate its implications for divine attributes.

Core Idea
If effects don't negate God's self-sufficiency, attributes should not either.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes on a key claim of the philosophers: that the effect (al-maf'ul) is identical to the act (al-fi'l). He uses a conditional argument to show that this leads to contradictions and that attributes are more primary than effects. Understanding his reasoning is essential for grasping the Islamic position on divine attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
The claim to refute is that the effect (al-maf'ul) is identical with the act (al-fi'l). This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Extract the first conditional premise
Ibn Taymiyya states: 'If it is permissible for the essence to necessitate effects, then its necessitation of attributes is more primary.' This is the core conditional.
The antecedent is accepted by the opponents (effects are necessitated).
3
Identify the second conditional branch
He adds a parallel conditional: if effects did negate necessity, then God would have to become an agent after not being one, which is impossible.
This shows the absurdity of the opposing view.
4
Analyze the conclusion
The act is positive (thubuti) and must subsist in the agent; hence it cannot be identical with the effect. Attributes are even more deserving of subsistence.
5
Assess the overall argument
The argument uses two conditionals to show that the identity claim is incoherent: it either contradicts divine self-sufficiency or leads to an impossible change in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل الدال على إثبات واجب الوجود دل على إثبات فاعل مبدع للممكنات، والمبدع لها يمتنع أن يكون صفة قائمة بغيره. فإن ذلك الموصوف هو الفاعل حينئذ دون مجرّد الصفة، وهذا الواجب الذي دلت عليه آياته ليس يفتقر إلى علة من العلل الأربع مع اتصافه بصفاته اللازمة له . إذا جاز استلزام الذات للمفعولات فاستلزامها للصفات أولى. ومن أعظم تناقض هؤلاء أنهم يقولون : الذات إذا استلزمت الصفاتِ كان ذلك افتقاراً منها إلى الغير، فلا تكون واجبة وهم يقولون : إن الذات مستلزمة لمفعولاتها المنفصلة عنها. ولا يجعلون ذلك منافياً لوجوب وجودها بنفسها. فإن كان الاستلزام للمفعولات لا ينافي وجوب الوجود، فالاستلزام للصفات أولى أن لا ينافيه . وإن كان ذلك الاستلزام ينافي وجوب الوجود كان حينئذ قد عَرَض له أن يفعل بعد امتناع كونه فاعلا بعد أن لم يكن فاعلاً، وهذا ممتنع عندهم. ثم إذا جاز أن يفعل، فـ «الفعل» ثبوتي فيلزم قيامه به. ودعوى أن «المفعول» عين الفعل مكابرة للعقل . وإذا جاز قيام «الفعل» به كان قيام «الصفات» بطريق الأولى. فساد القول بأن «المفعول» عين الفعل.
The proof that demonstrates the existence of the Necessary Being demonstrates the existence of an originating agent for possible things, and it is impossible for the originator of them to be an attribute subsisting in another. For then the described entity would be the agent, not the mere attribute. This Necessary Being, whom His signs indicate, does not need any of the four causes while being described with His necessary attributes. If it is permissible for the essence to necessitate effects, then its necessitation of attributes is more primary. Among the greatest contradictions of these people is that they say: If the essence necessitates attributes, that would be a need for another, so it would not be necessary—yet they say that the essence necessitates its separate effects, and they do not consider that contradictory to its self-sufficiency. For if the necessitation of effects does not negate the necessity of existence, then the necessitation of attributes is even more unlikely to negate it. And if that necessitation does negate the necessity of existence, then it would have occurred that it acts after being impossible to be an agent after not having been an agent, and this is impossible according to them. Then, if it is permissible for it to act, then the 'act' is positive, so its subsistence in Him is necessary. The claim that the 'effect' is identical with the act is an insolence against reason. And if the subsistence of the 'act' in Him is permissible, then the subsistence of the 'attributes' is even more primary. Corruption of the saying that the effect is identical with the act.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that the effect (al-maf'ul) is identical with the act (al-fi'l). Ibn Taymiyya begins by establishing that the proof of the Necessary Being proves an originating agent (fa'il mubdi') that cannot be an attribute subsisting in another. This agent does not need any of the four causes. He then sets up a conditional argument: if it is permissible for the essence (al-dhat) to necessitate effects (maf'ulat), then it is even more primary for it to necessitate attributes. He highlights the contradiction of those who accept that the essence necessitates its separate effects but deny that it can necessitate attributes without compromising its necessity. He then argues: if the necessitation of effects does not negate necessity, then the necessitation of attributes is even less likely to negate it; conversely, if it does negate necessity, then the essence would have to become an agent after not being one, which is impossible. Next, he argues that the act is positive (thubuti) and therefore must subsist in the agent. The claim that the effect is identical with the act is a violation of reason. If the act can subsist in the agent, then attributes are even more deserving of subsistence. The lesson concludes with the statement of the corruption of the identity claim. All examples must be from Islamic sciences; here the argument is purely theological and rational, relevant to discussions on divine attributes and creation.

المفعول
الفعل
الصفات
وجوب الوجود

C) It is impossible because attributes are separate.
D) It is irrelevant to self-sufficiency.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses a conditional argument to show that if effects do not negate divine self-sufficiency, then attributes are even more primary, thus refuting the identity of effect and act.

Consensus on the Distinction Between Creation and Created
Ibn Taymiyya cites the unanimous doctrine of the Salaf and scholars on creation being distinct from the created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the authoritative groups cited by Ibn Taymiyya to support the distinction between al-khalq and al-makhluq and explain the epistemological weight of this consensus.

Core Idea
For the Salaf, creation is not the created—and this is backed by an overwhelming consensus.

Here Ibn Taymiyya deploys a powerful argument from authority and consensus. He does not merely assert the distinction between the act of creation (al-khalq) and the created thing (al-makhluq); he demonstrates that it is the unanimous position of the Salaf, the Hadith scholars, the major theological schools, the four madhhabs, the Sufis, and Ahl al-Sunna. This wide-ranging agreement lends the doctrine epistemic certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Find the statement: 'creation is other than the created.' Understand that this distinguishes between the divine act and its effect, a key theological point.
2
List the authoritative groups cited
Scan the Arabic for the groups: the Salaf, Imam al-Bukhari, imams of Hadith, theological sects (Hishamiyya, Kulabiyya, Karramiyya), jurists of the four madhhabs, Sufis, and Ahl al-Sunna. Notice the diversity.
3
Analyze the structure of the consensus argument
Recognize that Ibn Taymiyya cites specific individuals (al-Bukhari, al-Kalabadhi, al-Baghawi) and groups. This is not a vague appeal but a documented consensus across multiple disciplines.
4
Consider the epistemological implication
If such a broad consensus exists, then any contrary view (e.g., equating khalq and makhluq) would be an innovation (bid'a). This makes the distinction a necessary belief for Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول بأن «الخَلْق» غير المخلوق هو مذهب السلف قاطبة. وذكر البخاري في كتاب «خلق أفعال العباد» الخلق غير المخلوق هو أنه قول العلماء مطلقاً بلا نزاع وهو قول أئمة الحديث وجمهورهم؛ وقول أكثر طوائف الكلام، كالهشامية، والكُلابية، والكرامية؛ وقول جمهور الفقهاء من أصحاب أبي حنيفة، وأحمد، ومالك، والشافعي؛ وقول الصوفية، كما حكاه التعرف لمذهب التصوف؛ وهو قول أهل السنة فيما حكاه البغوي صاحب «شرح السنة» عنهم.
And the saying that 'creation' is other than the created is the doctrine of the Salaf entirely. Al-Bukhari mentioned in his book 'Khalq Af'al al-'Ibad' that creation is other than the created, that it is the saying of the scholars absolutely without dispute, and it is the saying of the imams of Hadith and their majority; and the saying of most theological sects, such as the Hishamiyya, the Kulabiyya, and the Karramiyya; and the saying of the majority of jurists among the followers of Abu Hanifa, Ahmad, Malik, and al-Shafi'i; and the saying of the Sufis, as related in 'al-Ta'arruf li-Madhhab al-Tasawwuf'; and it is the saying of Ahl al-Sunna as related by al-Baghawi, author of 'Sharh al-Sunna', from them.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of authorities supporting the doctrine that creation (al-khalq) is other than the created (al-makhluq). Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that this is the unanimous doctrine of the Salaf (early generations). He then cites Imam al-Bukhari, who in his book 'Khalq Af'al al-'Ibad' reports that this is the consensus of all scholars with no dispute. The list includes: the imams of Hadith and the majority of them; most theological sects (e.g., Hishamiyya, Kulabiyya, Karramiyya); the majority of jurists from the four major madhhabs (Hanafi, Hanbali, Maliki, Shafi'i); the Sufis, as recorded in al-Kalabadhi's 'al-Ta'arruf'; and Ahl al-Sunna as reported by al-Baghawi. This wide-ranging consensus demonstrates that the distinction between the act and the effect is a foundational belief in Islam, contrary to the views of some rationalists who conflate them.

Key Terms
الخَلْق (al-khalq): The act of creation itself, distinct from the created thing. [Note: In theological debates, some equate khalq with makhluq.]
المخلوق (al-makhluq): The created entity, the product of the act of creation. [Note: No significant shift.]
السلف (al-salaf): The early generations of Muslims, including the Companions and their followers, considered authoritative in doctrine. [Note: No significant shift.]
أئمة الحديث (a'immah al-hadith): The leading scholars of Hadith, known for preserving the prophetic traditions. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Salaf and scholars across all Islamic disciplines unanimously affirm that creation is distinct from the created, forming a foundational theological principle.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of Creation-Created Identity
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' identity claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that creation is identical to the created, and evaluate the logical strength of his responses.

Core Idea
Separating the act of creation from the created thing preserves God's eternal attributes without temporalizing His essence.

Ibn Taymiyya here dismantles a core argument used by logicians to equate God's creative act with the created world. By carefully analyzing a two-horned dilemma, he shows that the supposed consequences—either the world's eternality or an infinite regress—do not follow. His response hinges on a crucial theological distinction between what is 'originated' (hadith) and what is 'created' (makhluq). This lesson will walk you through his reasoning so you can apply it in debates about divine attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Find the exact statement Ibn Taymiyya is refuting: that the effect (al-maf'ul) is identical with the act (al-fi'l), specifically that creation (al-khalq) is the same as the created (al-makhluq). This is the position of the logicians he is criticizing.
The passage begins with 'فساد القول بأن المفعل عين الفعل'.
2
Extract the logicians' dilemma
The logicians argue: If creation were distinct from the created, then creation must be either eternal (qadim) or originated (hadith). If eternal, then the created would be eternal, implying the world is eternal. If originated, then creation would need another creation, leading to infinite regress and making God a locus of temporal events. Write down both horns.
The two horns are presented after 'حجة هؤلاء أنه...'.
3
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's response to the first horn
Ibn Taymiyya points out that many scholars (Hanafis, Karramiyya, etc.) already accept that God's will (al-irada) is eternal while its object (al-murad) is temporal. By analogy, creation (al-khalq) can be eternal while the created (al-makhluq) is temporal, so the first horn does not force the world's eternality.
Look for the phrase 'يقول لكم من توافقونه على قدم الإرادة'.
4
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's response to the second horn
He denies that an originated creation would require another creation. He argues that even the opponents admit that separate created things (like the world) can arise without a preceding 'creation'—they are simply willed by God. If that is possible for separate entities, it is even more so for acts connected to God (like speech). Moreover, he introduces a key distinction: not every originated thing is 'created'; 'created' applies only to what is separate from God.
Focus on the distinction between 'حادث' and 'مخلوق'.
5
Evaluate the overall refutation
Assess whether Ibn Taymiyya successfully rebuts both horns. The first horn is answered by analogy with will. The second horn is answered by denying the need for an additional creation and by redefining terms. Consider whether the response would satisfy the original logicians or whether there is circularity.
Ibn Taymiyya ends by addressing a potential objection that the dispute is merely verbal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث ـ فساد القول بأن «المفعول» عين الفعل
وحجة هؤلاء أنه لو كان الخَلْقُ غير المخلوق» لكان إما قديماً وإما حادثاً. فإن كان قديماً لزم قِدَمُ المخلوق»، فيلزم قدم العالم. وإن كان حادثاً فإنه يفتقر إلى «خَلْق» آخر، ويلزم التسلسل، ويلزم أيضاً كون الرب محلاً للحوادث .
فيقال لهم : جميع لزم قدم واحدة منها . فقولكم : لو كان قديماً لزم قدم المخلوق»، يقول لكم من توافقونه على قدم «الإرادة»: نحن وأنتم متفقون على قدم «الإرادة ) وإن تأخَّر «المُراد»، فتأخُرُ «المخلوق» عن «الخلق كذلك، أو أولى. وهذا جواب الحنفية، والكَرَّامية، وكثير من الحنبلية، والشافعية والمالكية والصوفية، وأهل الحديث لهم.
وأما قولهم : «إن كان محدثاً افتقر إلى خلق آخر ، فهذا أيضاً ممنوع . فإنهم يسلّمون أن المخلوقاتِ محدثة منفصلة بدون حدوث «خَلْق». فإذا جاز هذا في الحادث المنفصل عن المحدث، فلأن يجوز حدوث الحادث المتصل به بدون «خلق» بطريق الأولى ولهذا كان كثير من هؤلاء، أو أكثرهم هم يقولون إن «الخلق» الذي قام به حادث»، لا «محدث»؛ ويقولون ما قام به من «الفعل» حَدَث بنفس القدرة» و «الإرادة»، لا يفتقر إلى «خلق»، وإنما يفتقر إلى «الخلق» «المخلوقُ»، و «المخلوق» ما كان منفصلاً عنه. وكثير منهم يسمونه «محدَثاً» ويقولون: هو «محدث»، ليس بـ «مخلوق»، فإن «المخلوق» ما خَلَقه بائناً عنه وأما نفسُ فعله، وكلامه، ورضاه، وغضبه، وفرحه الذي يقوم بذاته بـ «قدرته» فإنه وإن كان حادثاً» و «محدثاً» فليس بـ «مخلوق». وليس كلُّ حادث ولا محدَث» «مخلوقاً» عند هؤلاء. فإن قيل : النزاع في ذلك لفظي، قيل : هذا لا يضرهم. فإن مَنْ سمى ذلك القائم «خلق»
The third station: The corruption of the statement that 'the effect is identical with the act'. And their argument is that if 'creation' were other than 'the created', then it would be either eternal or originated. If it were eternal, then the eternality of the created would follow, and thus the eternality of the world. And if it were originated, then it would need another 'creation', and infinite regress would follow, and also that the Lord would be a locus for temporal events. So it is said to them: All [of these] entail the eternality of one of them. So your statement: 'If it were eternal, then the eternality of the created would follow' — to you says one who agrees with you on the eternality of 'will': 'We and you agree on the eternality of will, even if the willed is delayed. Thus the delay of the created from creation is likewise, or more appropriate.' And this is the response of the Hanafis, the Karramiyya, many of the Hanbalis, the Shafi'is, the Malikis, the Sufis, and the people of hadith. And as for their statement: 'If it were originated, it would need another creation,' this too is denied. For they acknowledge that created things are originated and separate without the occurrence of a 'creation'. So if this is permissible in the case of the originated that is separate from the originator, then it is even more permissible by primary inference that the originated that is connected to Him occur without a 'creation'. And for this reason, many or most of them say that the 'creation' that subsists in Him is 'originated', not 'originated thing'; and they say that the 'act' that subsists in Him occurs by virtue of power and will itself, and does not need a 'creation'; rather, 'the created' needs 'creation', and 'the created' is what is separate from Him. And many of them call it 'originated thing' and say: it is 'originated', not 'created', for 'the created' is what He created as separate from Him. As for His own act, speech, pleasure, anger, and joy that subsist in His essence by His power, then even if they are 'originated' and 'originated thing', they are not 'created'. And not every originated or originated thing is 'created' according to these. And if it is said: the dispute about that is verbal, it is said: this does not harm them. For whoever calls that which subsists 'creation'...
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that the effect (al-maf'ul) is identical with the act (al-fi'l), specifically that creation (al-khalq) is the same as the created (al-makhluq). He begins by stating the third station of his refutation: the corruption of this equation. Then he presents the argument of those who hold this view: if creation were other than the created, then creation would have to be either eternal or originated. If eternal, then the created would be eternal, implying the world's eternality (a theological problem). If originated, then creation itself would need another creation, leading to infinite regress and making God a locus for temporal events. Ibn Taymiyya then rebuts this dilemma. First, regarding the first horn (eternality), he points out that those who affirm the eternality of God's will (al-irada) alongside the delay of the willed object (al-murad) can analogously accept the delay of the created from creation. This response is adopted by various Islamic schools (Hanafis, Karramiyya, many Hanbalis, Shafi'is, Malikis, Sufis, and hadith folk). Second, regarding the second horn (infinite regress), Ibn Taymiyya denies the necessity. He argues that even the opponents accept that separate created things can originate without an additional act of creation (e.g., by God's will alone). So if that is possible for separate entities, it is even more so for acts that are connected to God (like His speech or pleasure). Ibn Taymiyya then clarifies that many scholars distinguish between 'originated' (hadith) and 'created' (makhluq). Acts that subsist in God's essence, such as His speech, are originated (hadith) but not created (makhluq), because 'created' implies separation. The passage ends by addressing the objection that this is merely a verbal dispute, which Ibn Taymiyya says does not harm their position, as terminology can vary as long as the meaning is correct.

Key Terms
الخلق
المخلوق
حادث
قديم
المفعول

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is eternal (qadim) and therefore not created.
D) It is both created and originated.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the identity of creation and created by distinguishing 'originated' (hadith) from 'created' (makhluq), showing that God's attributes can be temporal without being separate creations.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Philosophers' Denial of Temporal Events
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the philosophers' denial of temporal events in God is inconsistent with their own views and with the Salaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the philosophers' denial of temporal events in God is inconsistent with their own allowance of attributes, and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
The philosophers' own views on divine attributes undermine their rejection of temporal events in God.

In this passage from his critique of the logicians, Ibn Taymiyya targets the philosophers' argument that temporal events cannot subsist in God. He claims their own acceptance of attributes logically entails accepting temporal events like speech and action. By exposing this inconsistency, he shows that the philosophers' position is self-defeating and that the Salaf's affirmation of temporal events is more coherent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers argue for the impossibility of temporal events (hawadith) subsisting in God. This is the claim Ibn Taymiyya attacks.
Look for the phrase 'imtina' hulul al-hawadith' in the Arabic.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's counter
He points out that many philosophers, especially ancient ones like Abu al-Barakat, allow attributes (sifat) to subsist in God. This is crucial because attributes include temporal aspects.
3
Understand the logical implication
If attributes subsist in God, and some attributes (like speech, action) are temporal events, then the philosophers implicitly accept temporal events in God. Thus their denial is inconsistent with their own position.
Ibn Taymiyya classifies three philosophical positions; he claims no philosopher holds the third (attributes yes, actions no).
4
Assess the regress objection
The philosophers often argue that temporal events would lead to infinite regress. Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes regress in effects (successive acts) from regress in causes, and argues the former is permissible and affirmed by the Salaf.
Regress in effects (tasalsul fi al-athar) is not problematic.
5
Compare with the Salaf's view
Ibn Taymiyya cites the Salaf, who affirm that God never ceases to speak when He wills and to act by choice. This is presented as the correct view, consistent with divine perfection.
The Salaf include early imams like Ahmad ibn Hanbal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك احتجاجهم بامتناع حلول الحوادث لا ينفع الفلاسفة فإن هذا إنما نقوه القول بجواز لامتناع قيام الحادث بالقديم. وهؤلاء الفلاسفة يجوّزون قيام الحادث بالقديم ومن جوّز قيام الصفات بالباري منهم جوّز قيام الحوادث به مثل كثير من أساطينهم القدماء والمتأخرين كأبي البركات وغيره فهذان قولان معروفان لهم. وأما القول الثالث، وهو تجويز الصفات دون الأفعال، فهذا لا أعرف به قائلاً منهم. فإن كان قد قاله بعضهم، فالكلام معه كالكلام مع من قال ذلك من أهل الكلام. وعلى هذا فلا يلزم التسلسلُ. وإن لزم فإنما هو تسلسل في الآثار، وهو وجود كلام بعد كلام أو فعل بعد فعل والنزاع في هذا مشهور وإنما يُعرف نفيه عن التسلسل الجَهْمية، والقدرية، ومن وافقهم من كُلابي، وكَرَّامي، ومن وافقهم من المتفقهة. وأما أئمة السلف وأئمة السنة، فلا يمنعون هذا بل يجوّزونه، بل يوجبونه، ويقولون: «إن الله لم يزل متكلماً إذا شاء»، بل يقولون: إنه لم يزل فاعلاً تقوم به الأفعال الاختيارية بمشيئته وقدرته. وهذا كله مبسوط في مواضعه. والمقصود هنا أنه يلزم من كونه فاعلا قيام الصفات به، فإن «الفعل» أمر وجودي، وأن يفعل من أقسام الوجود، ووجود المخلوق المفعول بلا «خلق» ولا «فعل» ممتنع. وإذا قام الخلق به فـ «العلم» و «القدرة» لازمة لـ«الخلق»، وقيامهما به أولى.
And likewise their proof by the impossibility of the indwelling of temporal events does not benefit the philosophers, because this is only negated by the statement of the permissibility of the impossibility of the occurrence of the originated in the eternal. And these philosophers permit the occurrence of the originated in the eternal, and whoever among them permits the subsisting of attributes in the Creator permits the occurrence of temporal events in Him, like many of their ancient and later leading figures, such as Abu al-Barakat and others. So these are two well-known statements from them. As for the third statement—that of permitting attributes but not actions—I do not know of anyone who says it among them. If some have said it, then discourse with him is like discourse with one who said that from the people of kalam. Based on this, then infinite regress is not necessary. And if it does occur, it is only regress in effects—the existence of speech after speech or action after action—and the dispute about this is well known. It is only the Jahmiyya, the Qadariyya, and those who agree with them from among the Kulabiyya, Karramiyya, and those who agree with them from the Fuqaha' that are known to deny it. As for the imams of the Salaf and the imams of the Sunnah, they do not prohibit this; rather, they permit it, indeed they necessitate it. And they say: 'God has never ceased to be speaking whenever He wills.' Rather, they say: He has never ceased to be acting; actions of choice subsist in Him by His will and power. All of this is explained in its places. The intended point here is that from His being an agent, the subsisting of attributes in Him follows. For 'action' is an existential matter, and to act is one of the divisions of existence. And the existence of the created object of action without 'creation' and without 'action' is impossible. And if creation subsists in Him, then 'knowledge' and 'power' are necessary for 'creation', and their subsisting in Him is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the philosophers' (falasifa) argument that temporal events cannot subsist in God (imtina' hulul al-hawadith). Ibn Taymiyya argues that this argument does not help them because they themselves allow the occurrence of the originated in the eternal. He points out that many philosophers, both ancient and later like Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, permit attributes to subsist in God, which logically implies permitting temporal events (since attributes like speech and action are temporal). He identifies three possible positions: (1) permit both attributes and temporal events, (2) permit neither, (3) permit attributes but not actions. He claims that he knows of no philosopher who holds the third position; if someone does, they are like the mutakallimun who deny actions. He then argues that this view does not necessitate infinite regress, and if regress occurs it is only in effects (successive acts or speech), which is not problematic. He contrasts the Jahmiyya, Qadariyya, and some other sects who deny such regress with the Salaf and Sunnis who affirm it, citing the principle that God has never ceased to be speaking whenever He wills and acting by choice. Finally, he asserts that action is existential and that the created object cannot exist without creation or action; if creation subsists in God, then knowledge and power are necessary concomitants of creation, and their subsisting in Him is even more fitting.

Key Terms
حلول الحوادث (hulul al-hawadith): The indwelling or occurrence of temporal events in God; the philosophers deny this, but Ibn Taymiyya argues they implicitly accept it. [Note: In kalam, this is a major issue; Ash'aris generally deny it, while Hanbalis and Karramiyya affirm it.]
قيام الصفات (qiyam al-sifat): The subsistence of attributes in God; used to show consistency with temporal events. [Note: A central topic in 'ilm al-kalam; Ibn Taymiyya links it to the issue of temporal events.]
تسلسل في الآثار (tasalsul fi al-athar): Regress in effects only (e.g., successive acts), not in causes; considered permissible by Ibn Taymiyya. [Note: Distinction from causal regress; used to defend the possibility of temporal succession in God's actions.]
الجهمية (al-Jahmiyya): Followers of Jahm ibn Safwan; known for denying attributes and temporal events in God. [Note: Often paired with Qadariyya; Ibn Taymiyya uses them as examples of those who deny regress.]
السلف (al-Salaf): The early pious predecessors; Ibn Taymiyya cites them as affirming temporal events and attributes in God. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya's appeal to Salaf is a hallmark of his methodology.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They must deny the existence of temporal events.
B) They must also permit temporal events (hawadith) in God.
C) They must reject the Salaf's view.
D) They must affirm regress in causes.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes an inconsistency in the philosophers' position: their own allowance of divine attributes logically requires them to accept temporal events in God, a consequence they explicitly deny.

Exposing the Contradiction in the Logicians' Conception of the One
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' argument against divine attributes by exposing their self-contradictory concept of the One.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradiction in the logicians' argument that only one effect proceeds from the One, and explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers their One a mental fiction.

Core Idea
The logicians' One is both perfect Lord and stripped of all attributes—a contradiction no real being can sustain.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Radd ala al-Mantiqiyyin targets the cornerstone of the philosophers' principle that from the One only one proceeds. He shows that their very conception of the One is self-contradictory, combining perfection (as necessary existent) with imperfection (as devoid of attributes). Understanding this critique is essential for defending the Islamic doctrine of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doctrine under attack
Find the logicians' claim that from the One only one effect can proceed. This is based on the idea that if multiple things proceeded, the One would have multiple attributes, implying composition.
2
Unpack the logicians' definition of the One
Ibn Taymiyya describes their One as 'existence restricted by every negation'—stripped of all attributes. This is a purely negative concept.
3
Follow the twofold argument
First horn: If actions necessitate effects but not attributes, that is contradictory because it arbitrarily separates action from attribute. Second horn: If actions do not necessitate effects either, then their argument collapses entirely.
4
Recognize the deeper critique
Ibn Taymiyya argues that this One has no external reality—it is a mental fiction. Any created being is more perfect because it has positive attributes. Thus, assigning both perfection (as Lord) and imperfection (as bare existence) to the same entity is a contradiction.
5
Connect to the Islamic theological position
The passage concludes that affirming attributes in God is more appropriate than merely affirming actions, and that the philosophers' notion of 'composition' is false. The proper discussion of divine matters is elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان ما نفوا به الصفات تناقضهم في جمعهم بين وصفه بالكمال والنقصان وإنهم إن قالوا يستلزم المفعول ولا يستلزم الصفة لكون الملزوم مفتقراً إلى اللازم فهذا من أعظم التناقض وإن قالوا إنه لا يستلزمه كان ذلك أدل على بطلان قولهم في الصفات والأفعال وكان الدليل يدل على قيام الأفعال به والصفات وقيام الصفات به أولى من قيام الأفعال فبطل ما نفوا به الصفات وسموه تركيباً. والواحد الذي قالوا إنه لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد هو الواحد المسلوب عنه الصفات كلها بل هو الوجود المقيد بكل سلب وهذا لا حقيقة له إلا في الذهن وأي موجود مخلوق قدر فهو أكمل من هذا الخيال الذي لا حقيقة له في الخارج والتعظيم له إنما نشأ من اعتقاد أنه رب العالمين وأنه واجب الوجود ونحو ذلك من التي يظهر كمالها وأما من هذه الجهة النافية السلبية فما من موجود إلا وهو أكمل منه. وإذا كانوا قد جمعوا بين وصفه بذلك الكمال وهذا النقصان الذي يكون كل موجود أكمل منه دل على تناقضهم وفساد قولهم وكان ما قالوه من الحق حقاً وما قالوه من الباطل باطلاً. وهذه المسائل الإلهية مبسوطة في موضعها.
The invalidity of what they negated of the attributes. Their contradiction in combining between describing Him with perfection and imperfection. And if they say: 'The action necessitates [an effect] but does not necessitate the attribute because the necessitated is dependent on the necessary,' then this is the greatest contradiction. And if they say it does not necessitate it, then that is more indicative of the invalidity of their statement regarding attributes and actions, and the evidence indicates the establishment of actions in Him and attributes, and the establishment of attributes in Him is more appropriate than the establishment of actions. Thus what they negated of the attributes, calling it 'composition,' is invalid. And the 'One' which they say only one proceeds from is the One stripped of all attributes; rather it is existence restricted by every negation. This has no reality except in the mind, and any created existent imagined is more perfect than this imagination which has no reality in the external world. Its veneration arises only from the belief that it is the Lord of the worlds and that it is necessary existence, and the like of that which manifests its perfection. But from this negative, privative aspect, there is no existent except that it is more perfect than it. And since they have combined describing Him with that perfection and this imperfection such that every existent is more perfect than it, that indicates their contradiction and the corruption of their statement; what they said of truth is true, and what they said of falsehood is false. These divine matters are expanded in their proper place.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage critiques the logicians' argument that the One (God) cannot have multiple things proceed from Him because that would entail composition and attributes. Ibn Taymiyya points out a contradiction: if they argue that an action necessitates an effect but not an attribute, they are inconsistent. Moreover, their concept of the 'One' is a pure negation of all attributes, which has no reality outside the mind. Any created being is more perfect than this mental fiction. By attributing both perfection (as Lord) and imperfection (as stripped of attributes) to the same entity, they contradict themselves. The divine names and attributes are properly discussed elsewhere. The passage uses the example of God's attributes to show that the philosophers' notion is self-defeating.

Key Terms
الواحد
الصفات
الوجود
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Taymiyya consider the logicians' concept of the One a mental fiction?
A) Because it denies the afterlife.
B) Because it has no external reality and any created being is more perfect.
C) Because the logicians never defined it.
D) Because it contradicts the law of non-contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the principle that from the One only one proceeds by exposing the self-contradictory and mentally constructed nature of the philosophers' conception of the One.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the passage again and write a one-paragraph summary of Ibn Taymiyya's objection in your own words, identifying the two horns of the dilemma he presents.
?
In a debate, a philosopher claims that God cannot have knowledge and power as distinct attributes because that would make God composite. Using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning, formulate a response that points out the contradiction in the philosopher's own concept of the One.

Restricted Causes: Confining Rulings to Textual Cases
Author explains the purpose of using restricted causes to prevent analogy beyond the textual case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a restricted cause (al-ʿilla al-qāṣira) and explain its role in preventing analogical extension.

Core Idea
Restricted causes confine a ruling to its textual case, blocking unwarranted analogy.

In Islamic legal reasoning, not all causes (ʿilal) are meant to travel beyond the text that established them. Some are deliberately restricted to the specific case mentioned in the source, serving as a safeguard against unjustified analogy. This lesson explores the first benefit of such restricted causes as articulated by the author, with clear examples from fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two benefits
Read the first sentence: the author states there are two benefits to using restricted causes. Name them: (1) restricting the ruling to the textual case, (2) preventing analogy (ilḥāq).
These benefits are the core of the passage.
2
Define restricted cause
A restricted cause (ʿilla qāṣira) is a cause that does not extend beyond the case explicitly mentioned in the textual evidence. It explains why the ruling applies only to that case.
Contrast with transmissible cause (ʿilla mutaʿaddiya) which allows analogy.
3
Analyze the examples
The author gives four examples: carrion prohibition, blood prohibition, specification of sacrificial animals, and the fixed punishment for wine. For each, identify the textual case and how the cause prevents inclusion of other items.
Example: Prohibition of carrion (textual case) – the cause excludes ritually slaughtered meat.
4
Distinguish restricted from transmissible
The author notes that even those who claim all rulings are text-based still use restricted causes. A transmissible cause is only employed when a second text extends the ruling to other cases. Understand when each type is applied.
Transmissible cause requires additional textual support beyond the original case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي ذلك فائدتان : قصر الحكم على مورد النص ومنع الإلحاق، لئلا يظن الظان أن الحكم يثبت فيما سوى مورد النص . كما يعلَّل تحريم الميتة بعلة تمنع دخول المذكى فيها ؛ ويعلل تحريم الدم بعلة تمنع دخول العَرَق والرّيق وغيرهما فيها ؛ ويعلل اختصاص الهدي والأضحية بالأنعام بعلة تمنع دخول غيرها فيها ؛ ويعلل وجوب الحدّ في الخمر بعلة تمنع دخول الدم والميتة فيها ؛ وأمثال ذلك كثيرة. بل مَنْ يقول إن جميع الأحكام تثبت بالنص، يقول : إنها معللة بـ علل قاصرة، لكن إنما يعلل بـ «العلة المتعدية» نص يتناول بعض أنواع الحكم فيعلل ذلك الحكم بعلة تتعدى إلى سائر النوع الذي دل على ثبوت الحكم فيه نص آخر .
And in that are two benefits: Restricting the ruling to the textual case and preventing analogy, lest someone think that the ruling is established in other than the textual case. For example, the prohibition of carrion is explained by a cause that prevents the inclusion of ritually slaughtered meat into it; and the prohibition of blood is explained by a cause that prevents the inclusion of sweat, saliva, and others into it; and the specification of the sacrificial animal and the udhiya to cattle is explained by a cause that prevents the inclusion of other animals into it; and the obligatory fixed punishment for wine is explained by a cause that prevents the inclusion of blood and carrion into it; and many similar examples. Rather, whoever says that all rulings are established by textual evidence says that they are explained by restricted causes, but a transmissible cause is only used when a text addresses some types of a ruling, and then that ruling is explained by a cause that extends to the rest of the type for which the ruling's establishment is indicated by another text.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines the first benefit of using restricted causes (ʿilal qāṣira) in legal reasoning. The author clarifies that the purpose is twofold: to restrict the ruling to the textual case (qasr al-ḥukm ʿalā mawrid al-naṣṣ) and to prevent unwarranted analogy (manʿ al-ilḥāq). This is needed to avoid the mistaken belief that the ruling applies beyond the specified case. The author gives several examples from Islamic law: the prohibition of carrion is explained by a cause that excludes ritually slaughtered meat; the prohibition of blood excludes sweat and saliva; the specification of sacrificial animals to cattle excludes other animals; and the ḥadd penalty for wine excludes blood and carrion. These all use restricted causes. The author further notes that even those who hold that all rulings are text-based still use restricted causes—they do not use transmissible causes (ʿilla mutaʿaddiya) unless there is another text extending the ruling.

Key Terms
العلة القاصرة
العلة المتعدية
الإلحاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of using a restricted cause (ʿilla qāṣira) in legal reasoning?
A) To extend the ruling to all similar cases
B) To restrict the ruling to the textual case and prevent analogy
C) To negate the existence of any cause
D) To make the ruling universally applicable

Key Takeaway
Restricted causes preserve the specificity of textual rulings and prevent unjustified analogical extension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the hadith: 'The killer does not inherit.' Determine whether the cause (being the killer) is restricted or transmissible. Justify your answer by explaining whether the cause can extend to other situations like accidental killing.
?
Identify whether the following cause is restricted or transmissible: 'The prohibition of ribā in wheat is due to it being a staple food (qūt).' Does this cause allow analogy to other staple foods not mentioned in the text?

The Second Benefit: Knowing the Wisdom of Legislation
Author explains how understanding the wisdom behind laws increases faith and refutes atheist doubts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how knowing the wisdom (ḥikma) behind Sharia rulings strengthens faith (īmān) and refutes the doubts of atheists who claim the law is arbitrary.

Core Idea
Knowing the wisdom turns law from blind submission into reasoned faith.

Why did Allah legislate certain commands and prohibitions? Is it merely to test our obedience, or is there a deeper purpose? In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents the second benefit of studying the causes behind rulings: it reveals the interests (maṣāliḥ) that the Sharia seeks to secure for us in this life and the next. This knowledge not only increases faith and certainty but also provides a powerful response to atheists who argue that divine laws are arbitrary and meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed benefits
Read the Arabic passage and list the benefits the author attributes to knowing the wisdom (ḥikma) of Sharia. Look for: increases faith (īmān), increases knowledge (ʿilm), aids belief (taṣdīq) and obedience (ṭāʿa), and refutes atheist doubts.
These are listed in the second sentence: 'فإن ذلك مما يزيد به الإيمان والعلم، ويكون أعونَ على التصديق والطاعة، وأقطعَ لشُبه أهلِ الإلحاد والشَّناعة'
2
Analyze the structure of the argument
Notice how the author connects each benefit to an aspect of faith: knowledge of wisdom strengthens both intellectual conviction (ilm, tasdiq) and practical compliance (ta'a). Then he contrasts this with the atheist position that Sharia is mere subjugation (qahr ta'abbud) and arbitrary will (mahd al-mashi'a).
The contrast is explicit: 'لم يشرع لحكمة ورحمة بل لمجرد قهر التعبد ومحض المشيئة'
3
Understand the refutation of atheist doubts
The author asserts that knowing the wisdom 'cuts off' the doubts of atheists. Why? Because if every ruling has a wise purpose (maslaha), then the Sharia is not arbitrary but merciful — making atheist objections baseless.
The atheist claim is that Allah commands without reason, just to test. The response is that all rulings have wisdom and mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفائدة الثانية : معرفة حكم الشرع وما اشتمل عليه من مصالح العباد في المعاش والمعاد. فإن ذلك مما يزيد به الإيمان والعلم، ويكون أعونَ على التصديق والطاعة، وأقطعَ لشُبه أهلِ الإلحاد والشَّناعة، وأنصرَ لقول من يقول : إن الشرع جميعه إنما شُرع لحكمة ورحمة، لم يشرع لا لحكمة ورحمة بل لمجرد قهر التعبد ومحض المشيئة .
And the second benefit: Knowing the ruling of the Sharia and what it comprises of the interests of the servants in livelihood and the afterlife. For that increases faith and knowledge, and is more helpful for belief and obedience, and more decisive against the doubts of the people of atheism and objection, and more supportive of the statement of whoever says: All of the Sharia was only legislated for wisdom and mercy; it was not legislated without wisdom and mercy but merely for the sake of subjugation by worship and mere will.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents the second benefit of understanding the causes behind legal rulings: it leads to knowledge of the Sharia's purposes (masāliḥ al-ʿibād) in this world and the next. According to the author, this knowledge increases faith (īmān) and knowledge (ʿilm), aids in belief (taṣdīq) and obedience (ṭāʿa), and decisively refutes the doubts (shubuhāt) of atheists (ahl al-ilḥād) who argue that divine laws are arbitrary. It supports the position that all legislation is based on wisdom (ḥikma) and mercy (raḥma), not mere compulsion and will.

Key Terms
مصالح العباد
الإلحاد
التعبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The claims of philosophers about logic

C) The arguments of jurists about analogy
D) The positions of the Mu'tazila on free will

Key Takeaway
Knowing the wisdom behind every Sharia ruling transforms obedience from blind submission into reasoned faith and provides a decisive refutation of atheist claims of arbitrariness.

Exhaustive Division on the Vision of God
Ibn Taymiyya applies exhaustive division to the theological issue of God's visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of exhaustive division in a theological argument and evaluate which explanation of vision's validating factor is stronger based on the principle of pure existence.

Core Idea
Exhaustive division forces a choice: vision attaches to perfect existence or to deficiency.

Ibn Taymiyya uses the logical tool of exhaustive division (taqsim hasir) to analyze the disputed question of whether God can be seen. He contrasts two opinions about what makes an object visible: one claims it is pure existence, the other claims it is deficiency and originatedness. By applying exhaustive division, he argues that the first view is more coherent because vision itself is a pure existential relation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options in the division
Read the Arabic and find the two competing explanations of what makes an object visible. The division is between those who say the muṣaḥḥiḥ is pure existence and those who say it is deficiency.
The division is exhaustive: every possible view falls under one of these two categories.
2
Extract the underlying principle
Understand the common basis each side appeals to: one relies on the idea that more perfect existence is more visible; the other relies on the idea that only contingent, originated things can be seen.
3
Apply the criterion of exhaustive coverage
Check that the division covers all possibilities. Ibn Taymiyya implies that the muṣaḥḥiḥ must be either pure existence or deficiency; there is no third option.
This is the essence of taqsim hasir: a division that leaves no middle ground.
4
Assess Ibn Taymiyya's argument for the first option
He argues that vision is pure existence, so it relates only to existents. Therefore, the more perfect the existence, the more the object deserves to be seen. He uses the analogy of light and the sun to illustrate that the problem is not with the object but with the perceiver's capacity.
This reasoning supports the conclusion that God, being most perfect, is most deserving of vision.
5
Derive the theological implication
The inability to see God in this life is due to human weakness, not impossibility. Thus, vision of God is possible in the afterlife, as held by the Ash'ari school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمثيل التقسيم الحاصر في مسئلة «الرؤية» ثم إذا اختلف الناس في ما يجوز رؤيته، فقال بعضهم: المصحح للرؤية أمر لا يكون إلا وجودياً محضاً، فما كان وجوده أكمل كان أحق أن يرى، وقال آخر: بل المصحح لها ما يختص بالوجود الناقص الذي هو أولى بالعدم، مثل كون الشيء محدثاً مسبوقاً بالعدم، أو ممكناً يقبل العدم: كان قول من علل إمكان الرؤية بما يشترك فيه القديم والحادث، والواجب والممكن أولى من هذا. فإن الرؤية وجود محض، وهي إنما تتعلق بموجود لا بمعدوم. فما كان أكمل وجوداً، بل كان وجوده واجباً، فهو أحق بها مما يلازمه العدم. ولهذا يشترط فيها النور الذي هو بالوجود أولى الظلمة، والنور الأشد كالشمس لم يمتنع رؤيته لذاته، بل لضعف الأبصار. فهذا أنا نعجز عن رؤية الله مع ضعف أبصارنا، ولهذا لم يطق موسى رؤية الله في الدنيا. لكن لا يمتنع أن تكون رؤيته ممكنة والله قادر على تقوية أبصارنا لنراه.
An example of exhaustive division in the issue of vision (seeing God). Then when people differed concerning what can be seen, some said: The validating factor for vision is something that is purely existential, so whatever has more perfect existence is more deserving of being seen. Another said: Rather the validating factor is what is specific to deficient existence that is more deserving of non-existence, such as a thing being originated and preceded by non-existence, or possible and susceptible to non-existence. So the statement of one who explains the possibility of vision by what is common between the eternal and the originated, the necessary and the possible, is more appropriate than this. For vision is pure existence, and it only relates to an existent, not a non-existent. So whatever is more perfect in existence, indeed whose existence is necessary, is more deserving of it than what is accompanied by non-existence. For this reason, light, which is more deserving of existence than darkness, is required for vision; and intense light like the sun is not impossible to see due to itself, but due to weakness of eyesight. So we are unable to see God due to weakness of our eyesight, and for this reason Moses could not see God in the world. But it is not impossible that His vision is possible, and God is able to strengthen our eyesight so that we see Him.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage provides an example of exhaustive division applied to the theological issue of whether God can be seen. The author contrasts two opinions on the condition (musahhih) for vision: one says it is pure existence, so the more perfect the existence, the more deserving of being seen; the other says it is deficiency and origination. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the first is stronger because vision is a pure existential relation. He uses the analogy of light and the sun to illustrate that the problem is not with the object but with the perceiver's capacity. This supports the Ash'ari position that God can be seen.

Key Terms
الرؤية
المصحح
الوجود المحض
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the correct validating factor (muṣaḥḥiḥ) for vision?
A) Deficiency and originatedness.
B) Pure existence and perfection.
C) Color and direction.
D) Being possible and contingent.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that exhaustive division, when applied to the muṣaḥḥiḥ of vision, leads to the conclusion that the most perfect existent is most deserving of being seen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply exhaustive division to the issue of whether God's knowledge includes future contingents. List two possible accounts of how God knows future events (e.g., eternal knowledge vs. temporal emergence) and argue for one using the same method Ibn Taymiyya used for vision.

The Ambiguity of 'Mutahayyiz': Linguistic vs. Theological Meaning
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'mutahayyiz' against theological usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the equivocation in the term 'mutahayyiz' as used in theological arguments against the vision of God and explain how Ibn Taymiyya exposes the shift in meaning.

Core Idea
Theological jargon and Quranic Arabic do not speak the same language about 'occupying space'.

When theologians argue that God cannot be seen because seeing requires being 'mutahayyiz' (occupying space), Ibn Taymiyya steps in to ask: what exactly do you mean? He shows that the term in its original Arabic meaning is not what the theologians assume. This lesson unpacks his linguistic critique and shows how equivocation can undermine a seemingly logical objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The passage begins with a hypothetical objection: if God were seen, He would have to be mutahayyiz (occupying space), which is impossible for God. This is the claim under critique.
Notice that the objection assumes a technical definition of mutahayyiz.
2
Locate the ambiguous term
Ibn Taymiyya identifies 'mutahayyiz' as ambiguous (mujmal). He insists we must ask what the objector means by it.
Ambiguity here means the term has different meanings in different contexts.
3
Distinguish the two meanings
First, he gives the Quranic linguistic meaning: 'what is encompassed by another' (citing Q. 8:16). Second, he explains the Kalam technical meaning: 'what occupies a place (hayyiz)', where hayyiz is an estimated non-existent entity.
In the Quranic usage, mutahayyiz describes a person moving to a group, not a property of all physical objects.
4
Assess the equivocation
The objection works only if mutahayyiz means the Kalam technical term. But if it means the Quranic linguistic meaning, then being mutahayyiz does not imply being a body or occupying space in the problematic sense. Thus the objection equivocates.
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the fallacy of equivocation without needing to reject the term outright.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قال: لو رؤى لكان متحيزاً، أو جسماً، أو كان في جهة، أو كان ذا لون، وذلك منتف عن الله، قيل له: جميع هذه الألفاظ مجملة لم يأت شرع بنفي مسمياتها حتى تنفى بالشرع، وإنما ينفيها من ينفيها بالعقل فيستفسر عن مراده، إذ البحث في المعاني المعقولة، لا في مجرد هذه الألفاظ. ما يطلق عليه فيقال: ما تريد بأن المرئي لا بد أن يكون متحيّزاً؟ فإن «المتحيز» في لغة العرب التي نزل بها القرآن يعنى به «ما يحوزه غيره»، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَوْ مُتَحَرًا إِلَى فتتم ﴾ [سورة الأنفال: ١٦]. فهذا تحيز موجود يحيط به موجود غيره إلى موجودات تحيط به، وسمي «متحيزاً» لأنه تحيز من هؤلاء إلى هؤلاء. والمتكلمون يريدون بـ «التحيز» «ما شغل الحيّز»، و «الحيّز» عندهم تقدير مكان، ليس أمراً موجوداً. فالعالم عندهم متحيّز، وليس في حيز وجودي، و «المكان» عند أكثرهم وجودي.
If he says: If He were seen, He would have to be occupying space (mutahayyiz), or a body, or in a direction, or have color, and that is negated of God, it is said to him: All these words are ambiguous; Shari'a has come to negate their referents, so they are negated by Shari'a, but the one who negates them negates by reason, so he must be asked to explain his intent, for the investigation is about intelligible meanings, not mere words. So one asks him: What do you mean by saying that the visible object must be mutahayyiz? For 'mutahayyiz' in the Arabic language in which the Qur'an was revealed means 'what is encompassed by another,' as in His statement: 'or moving to a group' (Q. 8:16). So this is an existing 'tahayyuz' which an existing other encompasses, to existing things that encompass it, and it is called 'mutahayyiz' because it moves (tahayyaza) from these to those. The theologians intend by 'tahayyuz' 'what occupies a place (hayyiz),' and 'hayyiz' for them is an estimated place, not an existing thing. So the world according to them is mutahayyiz, but it is not in an existent place, and 'place' according to most of them is existent.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a critical linguistic analysis of the term 'mutahayyiz' (occupying space) used in objections against the vision of God. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the term is ambiguous (mujmal) and must be clarified. He distinguishes between the Arabic linguistic meaning (one who is encompassed by others, as in the Quranic verse about moving to a group) and the theological technical term (what occupies a place, where 'place' is an estimated location). By showing the equivocation, he undermines the objection that seeing God would require Him to be mutahayyiz in the theologians' sense, since in the linguistic sense it is not impossible for God.

Key Terms
متحيز
التحيز
الحيّز
المجمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what does the word 'mutahayyiz' mean in the Arabic language of the Quran?
A) What occupies a place (hayyiz).
B) What is encompassed by another.
C) What is in a direction.
D) What is a body.

Key Takeaway
Equivocation on the term 'mutahayyiz' undermines the objection that God cannot be seen because seeing requires occupying space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Critique of Eternal Celestial Spheres with Possibility of Non-Existence
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that celestial spheres are eternal yet possible of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyya judges the claim that celestial spheres are eternal yet possible of non-existence as contradictory, and identify his evidence from the consensus of rational people.

Core Idea
Eternal existence and possibility of non-existence are logically incompatible.

Ibn Taymiyya tackles a key claim from the philosophers: that the celestial spheres are eternal (qadim) yet admit the possibility of non-existence (mumkin al-'adam). He argues this is a self-contradictory position, and he supports his critique by noting that even Aristotle and his followers agreed that the possible is only what is originated. This lesson unpacks his logical consistency argument and its foundations in the consensus of rational people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Ibn Taymiyya targets the assertion that the celestial spheres are eternal (qadim) yet possible of non-existence (mumkin al-'adam). Understand that the philosophers mean these spheres exist eternally but their existence is not necessary by themselves; they could, in principle, not exist.
2
Analyze the common attribute argument
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the common attribute between the necessary-by-itself (wajib bi-nafsihi) and the necessary-by-another (wajib bi-ghayrihi) is existence, and this common attribute is eternal. If something is eternal, it cannot be subject to possibility of non-existence because possibility implies potential change in an eternal being.
The logic: if X is eternal, it has always existed; possibility of non-existence would mean it could cease to exist, which contradicts its eternal nature.
3
Note the consensus of rational people
Ibn Taymiyya states that all rational people, including Aristotle, agree that the possible is only the originated (muhdath) that can exist and then not exist. He points out that even Ibn Sina and his followers, who initially claimed eternal spheres can be possible, later contradicted themselves by stating that the perpetual is not possible.
4
Identify the contradiction in Ibn Sina's position
Ibn Taymiyya accuses Ibn Sina of trying to reconcile the philosophers' eternal cosmos with revelation, but in doing so he ended up contradicting both reason and the messengers. His attempt to combine the two led to an inconsistent stance that violates the principle that the possible is only originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - بطلان القول بقدم الأفلاك مع إمكان عدمها. فإن هذا باطل لما ذكرناه من أن المشترك بين الواجب بنفسه والواجب بغيره هو مستلزم لِقِدَم المشترك بين القديم الموجود بنفسه والقديم الموجود بغيره. فمن ادعى قديماً موجوداً بغيره، وقال إنه مع هذا يمكن عدمه، فقوله متناقض، كما بسط في موضعه. ولهذا لم يقل أحد من العقلاء : إنها قديمة يمكن عدمها الإمكان المعروف . وإنما أدعوا أن لها ماهية باعتبار نفسها، فقيل : الوجود والعدم، ولكن وجب لها الوجود من غيرها. وقد تبين بطلان هذا في غير هذا الموضع وبين أن هذا قول مخالف لجميع العقلاء، حتى أرسطو وأتباعه عندهم لا يكون ممكناً إلا المحدث الذي يمكن وجوده وعدمه وحتى هؤلاء الذين قالوا بأنها قديمة يمكن وجودها وعدمه، كابن سينا وأتباعه تناقضوا ووافقوا سلفهم وسائر العقلاء فذكروا في عدة مواضع أن الممكن الذي يقبل الوجود والعدم لا يكون إلا محدثاً، وأن كل ما كان دائماً لا يكون ممكناً . وأما القديم الذي لا يمكن عدمه فليس عندهم ممكناً. وإن كان وجوبه بغيره . وإنما خالف في هذا طائفة من الفلاسفة كابن سينا وأمثاله الذين أرادوا أن يجمعوا بين قول سلفهم وبين ما جاءت به الرسل مع دلالة العقول عليه، فلم يمكنهم ذلك إلا بما خالفوا به الرسول مع مخالفة العقول، مع مخالفة سَلَفهم فيما أصابوا فيه، وموافقتهم فيما أخطئوا فيه وكان كُفْراً في الملل، ومع تناقضهم ومخالفة جميع العقلاء.
The third station - the invalidity of the claim of the eternity of the celestial spheres with the possibility of their non-existence. For this is false due to what we have mentioned: that the common [attribute] between the necessary-by-itself and the necessary-by-another entails the eternity of the common [attribute] between the eternal existent-by-itself and the eternal existent-by-another. So whoever claims an eternal existent-by-another and says that despite this it can cease to exist, his statement is contradictory, as has been elaborated in its place. For this reason, none of the rational people have said: they are eternal and can cease to exist in the known sense of possibility. Rather, they claimed that they have a quiddity in themselves, so it is said: existence and non-existence, but existence became necessary from another. And the invalidity of this has been shown elsewhere and it has been made clear that this statement is contrary to all rational people, even Aristotle and his followers: with them, nothing is possible except the originated thing that can exist and not exist. And even those who said that they are eternal and can exist and not exist, like Ibn Sina and his followers, contradicted themselves and agreed with their predecessors and all rational people in that they mentioned in many places that the possible that admits existence and non-existence is only originated, and that everything that is perpetual is not possible. As for the eternal that cannot cease to exist, it is not possible with them even if its necessity is from another. Only a group of philosophers like Ibn Sina and his likes who wanted to combine the statement of their predecessors with what the messengers brought, along with the evidence of reason, opposed this. They could not do that except by opposing the messenger along with opposing reason, opposing their predecessors in what they were correct in, and agreeing with them in what they erred in and was disbelief in the religions, along with their contradictions and opposition to all rational people.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophical claim that the celestial spheres are eternal (qadim) yet are possible of non-existence (mumkin al-'adam). Ibn Taymiyya argues that this is self-contradictory because the common attribute between the necessary-by-itself and the necessary-by-another - which is existence - is what is eternal. If something is eternal, it cannot be possible of non-existence in the usual sense. He notes that even Aristotle and his followers agreed that the possible is only the originated (muhdath) that can both exist and not exist. The philosophers who claimed otherwise, like Ibn Sina, contradicted themselves and also agreed with others that the perpetual is not possible. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that those who tried to reconcile the philosophers' view with revelation ended up opposing both the messengers and sound reason.

ممكن
واجب بنفسه
واجب بغيره

B) Because Aristotle said that the spheres are not possible.
C) Because the spheres are not created.
D) Because they are made of matter.

Key Takeaway
Eternal existence and possibility of non-existence are logically incompatible; any being subject to possibility must be originated.
