Refuting the Common Attribute Argument
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that a ruling can be attributed to a common attribute while other attributes differ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error in the philosophers' claim that a ruling can be attributed to a common attribute when other attributes differ, and explain why those other attributes cannot be the cause.

Core Idea
A common attribute is not the cause if the ruling appears without other attributes.

The philosophers object that the presence of a common attribute in a branch case does not prove it is the cause, because the ruling might have a different cause. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this by showing that if the ruling occurs with the common attribute while other attributes are absent, those other attributes cannot have been causes in the original.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' objection
They claim that the establishment of the ruling with the common attribute in a case where other attributes are absent does not prove the common attribute is the cause, because the ruling might have a different cause in that case.
2
Pinpoint Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
He says this is an error because if the ruling occurs with the common attribute while other attributes are absent, then those other attributes cannot be effective in the original; otherwise, the ruling would not occur elsewhere.
3
Understand the requirement for negation
To conclude that the common attribute is the cause, one must negate any other possible cause either by a decisive proof, an apparent proof, or the principle of absence.
4
Distinguish two types of negation
Negation of causation by an attribute in the original case vs. absolute negation. The first needs a specific negator, the second covers all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم: «ثبوتُ الحكم مع المشترك في صورة مع تخلف غيره من الأوصاف جواب قولهم المقارنة له في الأصل مما لا يوجب استقلاله بالتعليل، لجواز أن يكون الحكم في تلك معللاً بعلة أخرى»، فيقال : هذا غلط وذلك أنه متى ثبت الحكم مع المشترك في صورة مع تخلف غيره من الأوصاف، يمتنع أن تكون الأوصافُ الزائدة المقارنة له في الأصل مؤثرة في الحكم مع تخلف غيره من الأوصاف. فإنها مختصة بالأصل، فلو كانت مؤثرة لم يجز أن يوجد الحكم في غير الأصل. وحينئذ فعدمها لا يؤثر. والصورة الأخرى قد ثبت فيها وجود المشترك . وأما إمكان وجود وصف آخر مستقل بعلة فهذا لا بد من نفيه إما بدليل قاطع أو ظاهر، أو بأن الأصل عدمه، ويكون القياس حينئذ يقينياً أو ظنيا . والكلام فيما إذا حصرت أقسام العلة، ونُفي التعليل عن كل منها إلا واحد. والنفي قد يكون لنفي التعليل بها في الأصل، وقد يكون لنفي التعليل مطلقاً. فالأول يحتاج معه إلى النافي في تلك الصورة، وأما الثاني فهو يتناول النفي في تلك الصورة وغيرها . جواب قولهم وقولهم : وإن كان لا علة له سواه فجائز أن يكون علة لخصوصه لا لعمومه»، فيقال : هذا هو في معنى السؤال الأول. وهو أن يكون الحكم ثبت لذات المحل، لا لأمر منفصل، وهو التعليل بالعلة القاصرة الواقفة على مورد النص . وأما قولهم إن بين أن ذلك الوصف يستلزم الحكم، وأن الحكم لازم لعموم ذاته، فمع بُعْدِه يَستغني عن التمثيل»، فيقال : لا بُعْدَ في ذلك، بل كلُّ ما دل على أن الحد الأوسط يستلزم الأكبر فإنه يستدل به على جعل ذلك الحد وصفاً مشتركاً بين أصل وفرع، ويلزمه الحكم. وأما قوله : إنه يستغني عن التمثيل»، فيقال: نعم، والتمثيل في مثل هذا يذكر الإيضاح، وليتصوّر للفرع نظير، لأن الكلي إنما وجوده كلي في الذهن لا في الخارج، فإذا عُرف تحققه في الخارج كان أيسر لوجود نظيره. ولأن المثال قد يكون ميسِّراً لإثبات التعليل بل قد لا يمكن بدونه وسائر ما تثبت به العلةُ من الدوران والمناسبة وغير ذلك إذا أخذ معه السبر والتقسيم أمكن كون «القياس» قطعياً. وأيضاً، فقد يكون قياس التمثيل يقينياً في كذا، فإن الجمع بين الأصل والفرع كما يكون بإبداء الجامع يكون بإلغاء الفارق، وهو أن يُعلم أن هذا مثل هذا، لا يفترقان في مثل هذا الحكم، ومساوي المساوي مساو، والعلم بالمساواة والمماثلة مما قد يعلم بالعقل، كما يعلم بالسمع، فإذا عُلم حكم أحد المتماثلين علم أن الآخر مثله، لا سيما إذا كان الكلام فيما تجرَّد من المعقولات، مثل القول بأن شيئاً من السَّواد عَرَض
As for their statement: 'The establishment of the ruling with the common [attribute] in a case while other attributes are absent does not necessitate its independence for causation, because it is possible that the ruling in that case is caused by another cause,' it is said: This is an error. Because whenever the ruling is established with the common attribute in a case while other attributes are absent, it is impossible that the additional attributes accompanying it in the original case are effective in the ruling in the presence of the absence of other attributes. For they are specific to the original; if they were effective, the ruling would not occur in other than the original. So their absence does not affect. And the other case has established the existence of the common [attribute]. As for the possibility of the existence of another attribute independent as a cause, this must be negated either by a decisive proof or an apparent one, or by the principle that it is absent, and then the syllogism would be certain or probable. And the discussion is concerning when the divisions of the cause are enumerated, and causation is negated from all except one. Negation may be negation of causation by it in the original, or negation of causation absolutely. The first requires the negator in that case, as for the second, it includes negation in that case and others. Their statement: 'And if there is no cause other than it, then it is possible that it is a cause for its specificity not for its generality,' it is said: This is similar to the first question, that the ruling is established for the essence of the subject, not for a separate thing, which is causation by the particular cause restricted to the text's instance. As for their statement: 'But if it is clear that the attribute entails the ruling, and that the ruling is necessary for its universal essence, then despite its improbability it dispenses with the example,' it is said: There is no improbability in that. Rather, everything that indicates that the middle term entails the major term is used to make that term a common attribute between an original and a branch, and the ruling is necessary for it. As for his statement: 'It dispenses with the example,' it is said: Yes, but the example in such a case is mentioned for clarification, and so that the branch can have a likeness conceived, because the universal only exists as universal in the mind, not in the external. So if its realization in the external is known, it is easier to find its likeness. And because the example may facilitate the proof of causation; indeed, it may not be possible without it. And all the other means by which cause is established, such as co-variation and suitability, when taken with examination and division, can make the syllogism decisive. Also, the analogical syllogism may be certain in some cases; for the connection between the original and branch can be by presenting the commonality or by eliminating the difference, which is to know that this is like this, they do not differ in such a ruling, and the equal of the equal is equal. Knowledge of equality and similarity may be known by reason as it is known by revelation. So if the ruling of one of the two similars is known, the other is known to be like it, especially when the discussion concerns abstract intelligibles, such as the statement that something of blackness is an accident.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of several philosophical arguments concerning causality and analogical reasoning. Ibn Taymiyya first addresses the claim that a ruling can be attributed to a common attribute even when other attributes differ. He argues that if the ruling exists in the presence of the common attribute while other attributes are absent, those other attributes cannot be the cause of the ruling in the original case; otherwise the ruling would not occur elsewhere. He then discusses the need to negate independent causes. Next, he refutes the idea that if there is only one cause, it could be specific rather than general; he equates this to a 'particular cause' that is tied to the text. He then defends the use of examples (tamthil) in analogical reasoning, stating that examples clarify and help conceive the branch. He argues that analogical syllogism can be certain when similarity is known through reason or revelation. He emphasizes that the universal exists only in the mind, so external examples are useful.

Key Terms
مشترك
دليل قاطع
دليل ظاهر
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is it an error to claim that the ruling can be attributed to a common attribute while other attributes differ?
A) Because the common attribute itself is not the cause; the other attributes are.
B) Because if the ruling exists with the common attribute while others are absent, those others cannot be the cause in the original.
C) Because the philosophers are always wrong.
D) Because the ruling must have multiple causes.

Key Takeaway
When a ruling is established with a common attribute in a case lacking other attributes, those other attributes cannot be the cause in the original case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a real analogy from usul al-fiqh (e.g., prohibition of khamr extended to nabidh). Write a brief objection that a philosopher might make using the common attribute argument, then reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's response using the principle that the presence of the common attribute without the other attributes negates the efficacy of those others.

Defending Analogical Reasoning and the Necessity of Examples
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that a unique cause must be specific and defends analogical reasoning with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's defense of analogical reasoning as potentially certain and justify the necessity of examples in analogical arguments.

Core Idea
Analogical reasoning can be certain when based on known equality, and examples are needed because universals are mental.

Ibn Taymiyya continues by addressing a related objection: if there is only one cause, it might be specific to the original, not general. He dismisses this as similar to the previous error. He then defends the use of examples in analogical reasoning, arguing that they are necessary for clarification and that analogical syllogism can be certain when based on examination, division, and known equality, whether by reason or revelation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the second objection
Philosophers argue that if there is only one cause, it might be specific (qasira) rather than general, i.e., only for the original. Ibn Taymiyya says this is similar to the first objection.
He equates it to the claim that the ruling is for the essence itself, not for a separate attribute.
2
Understand the response on examples
Some say that if entailment is clear, no example is needed. Ibn Taymiyya responds that examples are mentioned for clarification and to help conceive the branch, because universals exist only in the mind.
Examples facilitate the proof of causation and may even be necessary.
3
Explore how analogy becomes certain
Analogical syllogism can be decisive when combined with methods like examination and division (sabr wa taqsim), co-variation, and suitability. It can also be certain by eliminating differences (ilgha' al-fariq) or presenting commonality (jami').
Knowledge of equality can be by reason or revelation.
4
Note the role of universal and external
The universal only exists in the mind, so an external example helps realize its likeness. When known equality is established, the ruling of one similar applies to the other.
This is especially true for abstract intelligibles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أنه متى ثبت الحكم مع المشترك في صورة مع تخلف غيره من الأوصاف، يمتنع أن تكون الأوصافُ الزائدة المقارنة له في الأصل مؤثرة في الحكم مع تخلف غيره من الأوصاف. فإنها مختصة بالأصل، فلو كانت مؤثرة لم يجز أن يوجد الحكم في غير الأصل. وحينئذ فعدمها لا يؤثر. والصورة الأخرى قد ثبت فيها وجود المشترك . وأما إمكان وجود وصف آخر مستقل بعلة فهذا لا بد من نفيه إما بدليل قاطع أو ظاهر، أو بأن الأصل عدمه، ويكون القياس حينئذ يقينياً أو ظنيا . والكلام فيما إذا حصرت أقسام العلة، ونُفي التعليل عن كل منها إلا واحد. والنفي قد يكون لنفي التعليل بها في الأصل، وقد يكون لنفي التعليل مطلقاً. فالأول يحتاج معه إلى النافي في تلك الصورة، وأما الثاني فهو يتناول النفي في تلك الصورة وغيرها . جواب قولهم وقولهم : وإن كان لا علة له سواه فجائز أن يكون علة لخصوصه لا لعمومه»، فيقال : هذا هو في معنى السؤال الأول. وهو أن يكون الحكم ثبت لذات المحل، لا لأمر منفصل، وهو التعليل بالعلة القاصرة الواقفة على مورد النص . وأما قولهم إن بين أن ذلك الوصف يستلزم الحكم، وأن الحكم لازم لعموم ذاته، فمع بُعْدِه يَستغني عن التمثيل»، فيقال : لا بُعْدَ في ذلك، بل كلُّ ما دل على أن الحد الأوسط يستلزم الأكبر فإنه يستدل به على جعل ذلك الحد وصفاً مشتركاً بين أصل وفرع، ويلزمه الحكم. وأما قوله : إنه يستغني عن التمثيل»، فيقال: نعم، والتمثيل في مثل هذا يذكر الإيضاح، وليتصوّر للفرع نظير، لأن الكلي إنما وجوده كلي في الذهن لا في الخارج، فإذا عُرف تحققه في الخارج كان أيسر لوجود نظيره. ولأن المثال قد يكون ميسِّراً لإثبات التعليل بل قد لا يمكن بدونه وسائر ما تثبت به العلةُ من الدوران والمناسبة وغير ذلك إذا أخذ معه السبر والتقسيم أمكن كون «القياس» قطعياً. وأيضاً، فقد يكون قياس التمثيل يقينياً في كذا، فإن الجمع بين الأصل والفرع كما يكون بإبداء الجامع يكون بإلغاء الفارق، وهو أن يُعلم أن هذا مثل هذا، لا يفترقان في مثل هذا الحكم، ومساوي المساوي مساو، والعلم بالمساواة والمماثلة مما قد يعلم بالعقل، كما يعلم بالسمع، فإذا عُلم حكم أحد المتماثلين علم أن الآخر مثله، لا سيما إذا كان الكلام فيما تجرَّد من المعقولات، مثل القول بأن شيئاً من السَّواد عَرَض
As for their statement: 'The establishment of the ruling with the common [attribute] in a case while other attributes are absent does not necessitate its independence for causation, because it is possible that the ruling in that case is caused by another cause,' it is said: This is an error. Because whenever the ruling is established with the common attribute in a case while other attributes are absent, it is impossible that the additional attributes accompanying it in the original case are effective in the ruling in the presence of the absence of other attributes. For they are specific to the original; if they were effective, the ruling would not occur in other than the original. So their absence does not affect. And the other case has established the existence of the common [attribute]. As for the possibility of the existence of another attribute independent as a cause, this must be negated either by a decisive proof or an apparent one, or by the principle that it is absent, and then the syllogism would be certain or probable. And the discussion is concerning when the divisions of the cause are enumerated, and causation is negated from all except one. Negation may be negation of causation by it in the original, or negation of causation absolutely. The first requires the negator in that case, as for the second, it includes negation in that case and others. Their statement: 'And if there is no cause other than it, then it is possible that it is a cause for its specificity not for its generality,' it is said: This is similar to the first question, that the ruling is established for the essence of the subject, not for a separate thing, which is causation by the particular cause restricted to the text's instance. As for their statement: 'But if it is clear that the attribute entails the ruling, and that the ruling is necessary for its universal essence, then despite its improbability it dispenses with the example,' it is said: There is no improbability in that. Rather, everything that indicates that the middle term entails the major term is used to make that term a common attribute between an original and a branch, and the ruling is necessary for it. As for his statement: 'It dispenses with the example,' it is said: Yes, but the example in such a case is mentioned for clarification, and so that the branch can have a likeness conceived, because the universal only exists as universal in the mind, not in the external. So if its realization in the external is known, it is easier to find its likeness. And because the example may facilitate the proof of causation; indeed, it may not be possible without it. And all the other means by which cause is established, such as co-variation and suitability, when taken with examination and division, can make the syllogism decisive. Also, the analogical syllogism may be certain in some cases; for the connection between the original and branch can be by presenting the commonality or by eliminating the difference, which is to know that this is like this, they do not differ in such a ruling, and the equal of the equal is equal. Knowledge of equality and similarity may be known by reason as it is known by revelation. So if the ruling of one of the two similars is known, the other is known to be like it, especially when the discussion concerns abstract intelligibles, such as the statement that something of blackness is an accident.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of several philosophical arguments concerning causality and analogical reasoning. Ibn Taymiyya first addresses the claim that a ruling can be attributed to a common attribute even when other attributes differ. He argues that if the ruling exists in the presence of the common attribute while other attributes are absent, those other attributes cannot be the cause of the ruling in the original case; otherwise the ruling would not occur elsewhere. He then discusses the need to negate independent causes. Next, he refutes the idea that if there is only one cause, it could be specific rather than general; he equates this to a 'particular cause' that is tied to the text. He then defends the use of examples (tamthil) in analogical reasoning, stating that examples clarify and help conceive the branch. He argues that analogical syllogism can be certain when similarity is known through reason or revelation. He emphasizes that the universal exists only in the mind, so external examples are useful.

Key Terms
علة قاصرة
قياس تمثيل
إلغاء الفارق
سبر وتقسيم

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Taymiyya insist on using examples in analogical reasoning?
A) Because examples make analogies more beautiful.
B) Because the universal exists only in the mind, so external examples help conceive the branch.
C) Because without examples, the ruling cannot be established.
D) Because examples are required by revelation.

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning can be certain when based on known equality, and examples are essential to connect the mental universal with external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument that the world is temporally originated because it is composed of parts that change. Write a brief analogy using the method of eliminating differences (ilgha' al-fariq) and explain why this analogy could be certain according to Ibn Taymiyya.
